Summary 0:
EU rules on marketing communications for collective investment undertakings (AIFs) and UCITS (management companies)
EU rules for marketing communications (management firms)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 — rules on pre-marketing and marketing communications of collective investments in collective investment undertaking* and social entrepreneurship funds (UCITS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to marketing communications and the information to be disclosed to investors.
It aims to enhance the regulatory framework applicable to collective investments* and to improve investor protection.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
a UCITS management company;
EuVECA managers* and EuSEF managers;
UCITS management companies.
EU countries must establish a central database containing summaries of national requirements for marketing communication and hyperlinks to the information published on the websites of the competent authorities.
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) must ensure that:
all marketing communications addressed to investors are identifiable as such;
the risks and rewards of purchasing units or shares of an AIF or UCITS are described in an equally prominent manner;
investors’ rights and obligations are clearly defined in the marketing communications;
all fees and charges are publicly disclosed on the website of the relevant authorities;
any processing of personal data carried out in relation to cross-border marketing activities is proportionate to the supervisory tasks carried out and publicly disclosed;
fees and charges listed in the central database.
the European Commission publishes a report every 2 years on the application of the regulation.
AIFMs. AIFMs must ensure:
that all marketing communications are clearly identifiable;
that the risks and benefits of investing in UCITS and AIFs are clearly described in a clear, understandable and proportionate manner.
Management companies must:
ensure that marketing communications do not contain misleading information;
notify and notify investors of any changes to their rights or obligations;
provide investors with a key investor information document (KIID) as required by Directive 2009/65/EC and Directive 2011/61/EU.
In addition, they must ensure the disclosure of the rights and responsibilities of the management company and of its management board.
They must also ensure that the information they provide to investors in marketing communications is not misleading and that they do not mislead or deceive investors in any way.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 2 August 2010 and had to become law in the EU countries by 2 August 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Collective investment undertaking: an investment undertaking that is managed by a management company.
* UCITS: a unit-linked investment trust (UCIT) that is a UCITS authorised in accordance with Article 5 of Regulation (EC) No 345/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council and is managed externally or internally managed, depending on its legal form.

Summary 1:
Ensuring products are safe on the EU market: Regulation (EU) 2019/515 (market surveillance framework) (European Agency for Safety and Health at Work)
Establishing a market surveillance framework for products entering the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2001/95/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council laying down the general safety requirements for all consumer products and providing for specific obligations and powers of the Member States in relation to dangerous products as well as for the exchange of information to that effect through the Rapid Information Exchange System (RAPEX)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the organisation of market surveillance of goods entering the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products that are subject to the EU harmonisation legislation listed in Annex I concerning manufactured products other than food, feed, medicinal products for human and veterinary use, living plants and animals, products of human origin and products of plants or animals relating directly to their future reproduction.
It does not apply to the following products:
food and feed;
medicines for human or veterinary use;
living plant and animal products;
live animals;
products of plant or animal origin.
Requirements for market surveillance authorities
EU countries must ensure that:
an economic operator is established in order to place products on the Union’s market;
there is a liaison point with the market surveillance authority;
the surveillance authorities have access to the Product Contact Points established under Regulation (EC) No 765/2008;
a product is considered to have been made available on the market if the offer for sale is targeted at end users in the EU.
An economic operator must act responsibly and in full accordance with the legal requirements applicable when placing or making products available to the market, so as to ensure compliance with the legislation on products.
Risk assessment
The Regulation sets out the methodology and criteria for assessing risks. It also lays down the rules for the harmonisation of EU legislation on the control of dangerous products and on the sharing of information between the surveillance authorities and the economic operators concerned.
Importers and fulfilment service providers must ensure the safety of consumers and the smooth functioning of the internal market, including where the economic operator acts both as an importer as regards certain products and as a fulfilment services provider as regards other products. In addition, the regulation requires that the competent authorities of EU countries:
ensure that the authorised importer is informed of any non-compliance of the legislation;
identify the importer of the product and inform the competent authority of the EU country in which the product is to be placed;
establish a contact point for the authorities in the case of non-compliant products, so that they can address any requests for information regarding a product's compliance with EU legislation.
In addition, EU countries must:
implement effective and proportionate controls on the import and export of dangerous and potentially dangerous products; and
establish procedures for the access of authorised importers to the product contact points established under the Regulation.
Exchange of information
In order to ensure the effective enforcement of the regulation, the Commission must establish an electronic system for exchanging information between EU countries’ competent authorities. It must also establish a system for sharing information on products that have been found to be a risk to public health or to the health and safety of end users.
The Commission must also:
inform the EU countries of any measures taken to prevent, detect and combat the introduction of dangerous or potentially dangerous goods into the EU;
make it possible for the Commission to monitor the implementation of these measures;
use electronic means of communication to ensure that all EU countries have the same level of access to this information.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  I am a member of the Commission and I am responsible for implementing the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Market surveillance framework’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 2:
Exercise of copyright and related rights: online retransmission of television programmes and retransmissions of television and radio programmes originating in non-EU countries (Directive 2014/26/EU)
Exemption of broadcasting organisations from the obligation to communicate or make available to the public programmes in their ancillary online services.
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/24/EU on the exercise of copyright, related rights and licensing of online services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the rights holders of audiovisual works (i.e. television programmes) and broadcasting organisations (radio programmes) are able to exploit the rights they hold for the purpose of providing online services to users.
The directive applies to:
online services that are a direct or indirect link to the broadcasting service;
online programmes that are not part of the original broadcast.
KEY POINTS
Scope of the directive
It applies to online services that, while having a clear and subordinate relationship with the broadcast, can be accessed by users separately from that broadcasting service without there being a precondition for them to have to obtain access to it.
It does not apply to the following:
ancillary services that give access to material that enriches or otherwise expands the programme broadcast by the broadcasting organisation, including by way of previewing, extending, supplementing or reviewing the relevant programme’s content.
Radio programmes that originate in other EU countries.
Internet programmes originating outside the EU.
Rights holders’ obligations
Radio and television programmes originate in countries other than the country of their principal establishment. The directive does not, however, apply to broadcasting organisations that are based in the same EU country as their principal establishments.
Licences
Radio broadcasters must obtain licences from the European Commission and the European Broadcasting Union (EBU). These licences are valid for a period of 5 years. They must be renewed at least once every 5 years and may be renewed for a further 5 years if necessary.
When determining reasonable licensing terms, including the license fee, the economic value of the use of the rights in trade, as well as the value allocated to the means of broadcasting, should be taken into account.
In addition, broadcasting organisations must ensure that:
the rights holders are notified of any infringements of their rights;
the broadcasting organisations are informed of any changes to the rights that may be made to their programmes; and
the European Commission has the power to grant or refuse authorisation to infringements.
Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 on the rights of collective management organisations (collective management organisations) and the broadcasting organisations themselves
It sets out rules on the right of distribution of the retransmitted programmes and the right to communicate with the public.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
European Commission website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU, Euratom).
-
OJ L 183, 1.1.2016, pp. 1-18.

Summary 3:
The.eu top-level domain (TLD) and its associated scripts
The.EU top- level domain (tLD)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2006 implementing the.eu country code top-Level domain
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union (EU).eu Top-Level Domain (‘.eu TLD’) to help build an online Union identity and to encourage cross-border online activities.
It sets out the rules for the organisation, administration and management of the.EU TLD.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) Member States. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
Eligibility criteria
Registration of one or more domain names can be requested by any of the following:
a Union citizen, independently of their place of residence;
a natural person who is not a Union citizen and who is a resident of an EU country;
an undertaking established in the EU;
any organisation established in an EU Member State without prejudice to the application of national law.
Registry
The European Commission designates a registry to manage the domain name.
The registry is managed by the European Internet Governance Group (EIG). The EIG is composed of representatives of the EU countries, international organisations and the Commission. It is chaired by the Commission and is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
It entered into force on 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
The EIG was established under Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 582/2006.
For more information, see:
European Union Top Level Domain Registry (European Commission).

Summary 4:
Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) 2021-2027: transport and energy infrastructure
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the Connecting European Facility (the CEF) (COM(2021) 749 final - not published in the Official Journal)
Communicating from the European Commission, the Commission, and the European Social and Cohesion Committee on the European Union (ESC) Connecting Europe Fund (The CEF).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1058 establishing the European Transport Facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European transport facility (the ‘CEF’).
The aim of the CEF is to accelerate investment in the field of trans-European networks and to leverage funding from both the public and the private sectors, while increasing legal certainty and respecting the principle of technological neutrality.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the eligibility criteria for projects that are eligible for financing from the Cohesion Fund (the EU’s social and economic fund). The CEF covers:
trans-European transport infrastructure (TEN-T) projects;
energy infrastructure (e.g. smart grids, smart grids and smart grids);
transport infrastructure (i.e. rail and road networks);
smart grids;
electricity infrastructure (smart grids and energy storage);
energy-related projects.
Projects eligible for CEF funding
Transport projects
Trans-
projects are designed to:
improve the efficiency of existing infrastructure;
improve access to the transport network;
facilitate the use of alternative fuels;
ensure the availability of renewable hydrogen or bio-methane to be used in transport;
promote the development of new technologies in the transport sector;
increase the number of cross-border rail connections between EU countries;
enhance the capacity of existing rail networks;
provide for the expansion of the European Rail Traffic Management System (ERTMS) by 2030;
support the implementation of the Trans-European Maritime Transport System (EMTS) by 2023.
EU countries must submit a list of projects eligible for funding.
The European Commission will review the list of eligible projects at the end of 2021. The review will take into account:
the number of projects that have already begun to be considered eligible as of 1 January 2021;
the geographical coverage of the EU; and
the development of the trans-border transport network.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
                  
The Transport Facility is part of the overall European cohesion fund (MFF) and is one of the largest investment programmes in the EU.
For more information, see:
Transit Facility (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cross-European network: a network of roads, railways and other modes of transport that connects all EU countries and the EU‘s islands and regions.
* Smart grid: a system of smart grids that enables the transmission of electricity, gas and other energy from one grid to another.
Electricity: the transmission and distribution of electricity from one energy source to another, including the storage of energy from the grid to the grid.
Transportation infrastructure: the network of road, rail and other forms of transport, including smart grids.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 749/2021 of 21 June 2021 establishing the Connected Europe Facility and repealing Regulation (EC) No 1315/2013 (OJ L 347, 23.8.2021, pp. 1-24)

Summary 5:
Removal of terrorist content on the internet (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Removal on the Internet (Regation (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the removal of terrorist material online
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the detection and removal of content online that incites terrorist acts.
It applies to all providers of information society services which store and disseminate to the public information and material provided by a user of the service on request, irrespective of whether the storing and dissemination of such information or material is of a mere technical, automatic and passive nature.
KEY POINTS
The regulation:
requires hosting service providers to remove or disable access to terrorist content, following an assessment by the competent authorities;
requires the competent authority to provide the hosting service provider with information on procedures and applicable deadlines at least 12 hours in advance of issuing for the first time a removal order.
The competent authority must:
assess whether material constitutes terrorist content within the meaning of this regulation;
identify the nature and wording of statements, the context in which the statements were made and their potential to lead to harmful consequences in respect of the security and safety of persons;
ensure the authenticity of the removal order, including the accuracy of the date and the time of sending and receiving the order;
consider the economic and social impact of the content being disseminated, as well as the number of users and the size of the hosting provider’s capacity to remove the content.
In addition, the regulation sets out a number of measures to be taken in the event of a breach of the above conditions.
Hosting service providers must ensure that:
the competent authority:
ensures that the content is removed within 24 hours of receiving a request for removal;
implements all the necessary measures to prevent the dissemination of the terrorist content;
publishes a written record of the order and the deadlines for its implementation.
EU countries must ensure the following:
that the competent national authorities:
have access to all the information they need to carry out their tasks;
have the power to:
impose sanctions, including criminal proceedings, for infringements of EU law;
take appropriate measures to protect the personal data of individuals who have been victims of terrorist acts;
establish a system of cooperation between national authorities and hosting services providers.
Where the hosting services provider fails to comply with the above requirements, it may be subject to criminal proceedings and/or to fines.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a series of measures designed to tackle terrorist content online. These include:
Directive 2000/31/EC establishing the European Network for the Prevention of Electronic Crime (ENPC);
Directives 2000/32/EC and 2000/34/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on the fight against terrorism and on the prevention of online incitement to commit acts of terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Fighting terrorism’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 of 21 December 2009 on the implementation of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1082/2009 on the protection of the internet against terrorist material and repealing Council Regulation (Euratom, EC) No 882/2008 (OJ L 347, 24.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (ec) No 540/2009 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027
Framework Programmes for Research & Innovation 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/2027 establishing the framework programme for research and innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the EU’s multiannual financial framework (MFF) 2021–2027.
It sets out the objectives and priorities of the programme.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme aims to:
promote the creation, better diffusion and transfer of high-quality and excellent knowledge and high quality technologies in the EU;
facilitate the recruitment of talent at all levels and contribute to full engagement of the EU's talent pool;
strengthen collaborative links and strengthen the impact of R&I in developing, supporting and implementing EU policies;
contribute to the sustainable development of Europe.
Objectives
The objectives of Horizon Europe include:
creating, better spreading and transferring knowledge and innovative technologies in Europe;
promoting the integration of European knowledge assets into innovation;
encouraging the exchange of best practices and best practices;
improving the quality of scientific and technological research and development (R&D) activities in Europe and in the world;
enhancing the competitiveness of the European Union (EU).
Programmes
The Programme is divided into 4 strands:
Widening Participation and Strengthening the European Research Agenda (ERA);
Global Challenges and European Industrial Competitiveness;
European Industrial Innovation (EI);
Innovation for Europe (IoT).
The programmes are designed to contribute to the development of innovative products and services in Europe, in line with the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development (the '2030 Agenda'), the SDGs and the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change.
Funding
The overall budget for the programme is €1.8 billion. This covers research on cultural and creative sectors, including the establishment of a European cultural heritage collaborative space and the creation of a network of research institutes in the fields of culture and creative sciences.
The ERA is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Social Fund (ESF). The EIB is responsible for the overall budget of the Programme, while the ESI contributes to at least 3.3% of the overall programme budget. The ESI is funded through the EIB and the EoS.
Specific programmes
The ESI covers:
research on cultural, creative and intellectual heritage;
science, technology and innovation (S&T) on global challenges and European industrial competitiveness;
research and innovation on innovation for Europe and the world.
Awards
The awardees are selected on the basis of:
the quality of the research, technological research, product development and demonstration activities carried out;
the impact of the activities; and
the types and purpose of the actions carried out and the impacts that are sought.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Horizon Europe’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 7:
EU programme for the development of the European Space Agency (ESA) (2017-2021)
EU Programme for the Development of the Europe Space Agency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 837/2013 establishing the European Union programme for research and innovation in the field of space
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU programme for Research and Innovation (ESA) under the Horizon Europe programme.
It aims to develop the European space sector and to strengthen Europe’s autonomy in accessing and using space in a secure and safe environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESA programme covers the period from 1 January 2017 to 31 December 2021.
The objectives of the programme are to:
ensure that the EU has autonomous, reliable and cost-effective access to space, especially as regards critical infrastructure and technology, public security and the security of the EU and its Member States;
promote the use and uptake of the data, information and services provided, as well as to develop downstream applications based on those data and information;
support the competitiveness and innovation of the space sector industries within the EU, in particular small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), start-ups and innovative businesses.
Funding
The programme is financed by the EU budget and the European Investment Bank (EIB). It is funded by the European Commission and the EU Member States’ general budget. The budget for the period 2013-2020 is €2.5 billion.
In order to meet the objectives of ESA, the programme will be funded by:
the EU budget for Horizon Europe;
the general EU budget; and
the European Union budget.
Infrastructure dedicated to the programme, which could be supported under Horizon Europe, aiming for coherence with activities in this domain by ESA.
Implementing the programme
The Commission will promote synergies with other related EU programmes and financial instruments, which would allow, where possible, use of access to risk finance, innovation partnerships, and cumulative or blended funding.
EU Member States will also be able to make use of the same technical assistance in performing tasks entrusted to them under this regulation.
International cooperation
ESA will participate in international cooperation and collaborate in relevant sectoral bodies of the United Nations (UN). It will also cooperate with non-governmental organisations (NGOs) and international organisations.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU is already one of the most competitive in the world in terms of innovation and competitiveness in the area of space. However, it has not yet achieved the full potential of its potential.
For more information, see:
European Space Agency and Horizon Europe (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) 2017/837 of 20 January 2013 establishing the EU Programme for Research, Innovation and Development in the Field of Space (Euratom) (OJ L 94, 1.2.2013, pp. 1-29)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC), No 838/2013 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Programme 2021-2027
European digital innovation programme 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/2027 establishing a programme for Europe’s digital transformations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes a programme to support the European Union (EU) digital transformation to deliver smart growth in areas such as high-quality data infrastructure, connectivity and cybersecurity.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme is for a period of 7 years to align its duration with that of the multiannual financial framework laid down in Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, 2020/2093 (MFF 2021–2027).
It covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote the adoption of advanced digital technologies by industry, in particular by SMEs and by other entities that employ up to 3 000 people (mid-caps), by public organisations and by the academic community;
encourage the development of new products and services based on data;
improve further the EU's digital capacities;
address market failures or sub-optimal investment in the area of information and communication technologies (ICT);
improve the efficiency of the EU digital infrastructure;
ensure the protection of intellectual property rights;
contribute to the creation of a common European data space.
The European Digital Innovation Hubs are set up in each EU Member State.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
1.01.202101
The regulation sets out the eligibility of activities and underlying costs for the programme from the beginning of the 2021 financial year, even if they were implemented and incurred before the grant application was submitted.
Funding
The EU budget is the main source of EU funding for the European programme. The programme is funded by the EU budget. The European Commission is responsible for managing the programme and for the allocation of the funds. The Commission may grant grants, prizes, procurement, indirect management, financial instruments, budgetary guarantees, budgetary assistance and reimbursement of external experts.
A consortium of legal entities may participate in calls for proposals, provided that their representatives have the capacity to undertake legal obligations on behalf of those entities and that they offer guarantees for protecting the financial interests of the Union that are equivalent to those offered by legal persons.
In addition, the Commission must take the utmost account of the opinion of each Member State before selecting a European digital innovation hub in the territory of that Member State, taking into account the opinions of the Member States participating in enhanced cooperation with the European Parliament, the Council, the Court of Auditors and the EPPO.
Implementation
The Commission must ensure that the programme is implemented in line with the rules on the implementation of the European budget. It must also ensure the transparency of innovative financial instruments and mechanisms that involve the Union budget, with respect to their contribution towards achieving EU objectives, both as regards initial expectations and the end results.
It must also take account of:
the Member States’ obligations to ensure that any third parties involved in implementing the programme grant equivalent rights to the Commission, OLAF, the court of auditors and, where appropriate, the European Commission;
the legal personality of the entities receiving EU funds, and
the rights and access granted by the Commission to OLAF and to the Court.

Summary 9:
Sustainable internal market: programme for the implementation of the European Pillar of Social Rights
Sustainable external market: Programme for the operation of the internal market
SUMMARY OF:
Programme for the functioning of the Internal Market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE PROGRAMME?
The programme aims to ensure that the EU’s internal market is a well-functioning market. It is one of the EU's major achievements and its best asset in an increasingly global world, as well as being a core element in achieving the green and digital transformation towards a sustainable economy, in response to the increasing pressures of climate change.
It is based on principles of fairness, transparency and mutual trust, which promote competition and benefits consumers, businesses and employees.
The objectives of the programme are to:
facilitate access to public procurement, the professionalisation of public buyers, the facilitation and improvement of access to procurement markets for SMEs, in particular through advisory services and training, an increase in transparency, integrity and product contact points;
reduce the number of illegal and non-compliant goods entering the market through targeted awareness raising and training;
ensure that conformity assessment bodies continue to fulfil their regulatory requirements, such as impartiality and independence, especially through the use of accreditation.
In addition, the programme supports cooperation with non-EU countries’ competent authorities, as appropriate.
KEY POINTS
Scope
This programme covers the following areas:
public procurement:
the creation of strategic public procurement;
the facilitation of strategic procurement,
the professionalising of public procurement and
access to public buyers
the improvement of the public procurement market for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);
the promotion of the role of SMEs in the economy and the development of the SME-to-enter-market and e-commerce sectors
the development of a single market information system
the establishment of the Single Market Information System (SMIS),
the Single Market Portal which should become a backbone of the upcoming Single Digital Gateway, the Your Europe Advice, SOLVIT, the Internal Markets Information System and the European Market Scoreboard.
Regulation (EU) 2019/515 on the application of the principle of mutual recognition in the area of goods
the implementation and application by EU countries of the legal framework for anti-money laundering and countering terrorism financing
the protection of intellectual property rights and the protection of the environment.
EU countries should also cooperate with non–EU countries in order to ensure the proper functioning of their internal market.
Public procurement
Public authorities should be supported in their efforts to achieve those objectives, for example by providing information and facilitating an exchange of information explaining how the EU internal market works on the ground. They should also promote cooperation with third country authorities, including by:
ensuring that public procurement is transparent and efficient;
promoting transparency and integrity in the procurement process;
improving the quality of the information and communication of public authorities to the public;
providing information and training to the general public on the EU public procurement system.
European statistical programme
The European Statistical Programme (ESP) is the successor programme to the previous European Programme. It aims to provide a framework for the development, production and dissemination of European statistics pursuant to Regulation (EC) No 223/2009. It also covers the implementation by the Member States of the new European statistical programme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE PROGRAMMATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in all Member States by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
   			
 	
For more information, see:
The EU's internal market (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
                  
* Strategic public procurement: the procurement of goods and services by the public authorities of Member States.
* Public procurement: a public procurement process where the public buys and sells goods or services on behalf of a public authority.

Summary 10:
EU rules on firearms and ammunition — classification and traceability of firearms, essential components and ammunition
European rules on guns and ammunition - classification and tracing of firearms and essential components
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/679 on the classification of firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It codifies and repeals Directive 2004/36/EC and its subsequent amendments.
It aims to make it easier for law enforcement authorities to trace firearms and their essential components.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
firearms and ammunition classified in the following categories:
automatic firearms;
machineguns;
battery-operated firearms.
Dealers and brokers
museums and collectors.
EU countries may grant authorisations to recognised museums and collectors for the acquisition and possession of firearms in category A when necessary for historical, cultural, scientific, technical, educational or heritage purposes, provided that they have taken the necessary measures to address any risks to public security or public order, including by way of proper storage.
National competent authorities may restrict access to those records and all related personal data to competent authorities for up to 10 years after the destruction of the firearm or essential components concerned, for the purpose of granting or withdrawing authorisations or for customs proceedings, including the possible imposition of administrative penalties, where that access is necessary for the enforcement of criminal law.
Ammunitions are classified in category B.
Firearms are classified according to the number of rounds they can fire. They are classified as follows:
non-automatic firearms, including those with a calibre of.22 or smaller than.22, unless they have been converted from automatic firearms, or if they use rimfire percussion.
Ammunition is classified as a semi-automatic firearm unless it has been converted into an automatic firearm.
Muzzle-loading firearms are classified on the basis of their capacity:
calibre of ammunition;
firearm of calibre 1.5 or less;
muzzleload of ammunition.
In addition, ammunition is classified in accordance with the rules on the exchange of information between dealers and brokers, on the one hand, and national competent authorities on the other.
The European firearms pass is regarded as the main document needed by target shooters and other persons authorised for the possession of a firearm during a journey to another EU country. It is a document issued by the European Union and is valid for a period of 5 years. It must be accompanied by a photograph of the holder of the European Firearms Pass and a copy of the EU passport.
Exceptions to the above rules are made for:
museum and collectors;
manufacturers and importers of firearms; and
consultants of firearms. The directive does not apply to the manufacture of firearms or the modification or conversion of a complete firearm, such as the shortening of a whole firearm, leading to a change in their category or subcategory.

Summary 11:
Maritime National Single Windows (EMSWe) for port calls (until 2025)
Maritime Network of Single Windows for the provision of information on port calls
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment, establishment, operation and making available of a maritime National Single Window
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a maritime national single window (EMSW) to provide information on the port services provided by national authorities or port operators when a ship makes a port call.
It aims to improve the efficiency of maritime transport and to reduce administrative burden.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes a comprehensive EMSWe data set which covers all information elements that might be requested by the national authorities and port operators for administrative or operational purposes.
The national authorities, port operators or terminal operators must report the information they receive to the national single windows.
National authorities must ensure that:
the information they provide to an EMSWe single window is in a language easily understood by the public;
the data they provide are in a format that can be easily read and understood by all users of the single window; and
the national authorities do not use the same language for all information.
EU countries must not introduce new reporting obligations unless such introduction has been approved by the European Commission.
A national authority may request the Commission to introduce or amend data elements in the data set, in accordance with the reporting obligations contained in the national legislation and the EU legal acts.
In addition, national authorities must:
ensure that the information provided through the single windows is not shared with certain other entities, such as port or terminal operator, when authorised by the declarant;
keep the number of items to a minimum;
notify the Commission of any new data elements that have been introduced by the EU countries.
Each EU country must establish and operate a single window for each category of port services listed in Article 1(2) of the EU’s law on port services. The national authorities are responsible for the operation of the maritime single windows and for the training of their staff. They must also ensure that their staff are trained in the relevant language and that they have access to all the information that is necessary for their operation.
Every 2 years, the Commission must publish a report on the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  Icons of the European Union (EU).

Summary 12:
Interoperability of electronic road toll systems
Interoperation of Electronic road tolls
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/33/EC on the interoperability of the electronic toll systems of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve interoperability* of the EU’s electronic road-toll systems (EETS) and to reduce the cost of, and the burdens linked to, the payment of tolls across the EU.
KEY POINTS
EETS providers are required to:
provide users of light-duty vehicles with a 5,8 GHz radio frequency identification (OBE) for the calculation of applied toll on the vehicles of EETS users by the EETS providers;
cooperate fully with toll chargers in their enforcement efforts, so as to increase the overall efficiency of the system;
assist the European Commission in the implementation of the directive.
The directive repeals and recasts Directive 98/8/EC.
Scope
The Directive applies to all EETS systems, whether they are based on distance-based or time-based tolls. It does not apply to systems where the road fee due is linked to the time spent by the vehicle on the tolled infrastructure (for example, vignettes) or to the vehicle passing a specific point.
Rights and obligations of the main EETS actors, that is to say, the toll service providers, toll charger and EETS user
The main rights and obligations are set out in the annex to the Directive. These include:
the right of the toll charger to accredit or re-accredit EETS service providers before the operational launch of a new or substantially modified EETS system. The toll charger must design and follow the procedure for the accreditation or, respectively, re-assessment of the service providers in such a way that the procedure can be concluded at the latest one month before the operation launch of the new system.
Toll chargers must:
not be liable for tolls that their clients have not paid, when the latter are equipped with an OBE that has been declared to the toll charger as invalidated.
Payment of a toll to an EETS provider should be considered as fulfilling the user's obligations towards the relevant toll charger.
Eets providers should:
pay the toll on their behalf and in their name, since invoicing directly in the name of the EATS provider can have adverse administrative and tax implications;
ensure that the toll is calculated in accordance with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 on the application of the law of the Member States concerning the payment and collection of road charges;
not charge tolls on the vehicle of a road user who has not paid the road toll.
In addition, the directive sets out the rules on:
identification and processing of the data collected by EETS services;
the collection of information on vehicles and owners or holders of vehicles for which there was a failure to pay road fees;
data protection of the personal data of road users.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Electronic road toll system (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Electronic toll system: a system that allows users to pay a road toll by means of an electronic device.
* Time-based: the amount of time taken by a vehicle to cover the distance covered by a given point in time.
Distance-based system: an electronic system that charges a toll based on the time the vehicle spent on the road.
Time-based systems: systems that charge a toll for the duration of a given journey.

Summary 13:
Joint deployment of immigration liaison officers to non-EU countries (Recast of Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Joint deployments of immigration liaisons to non–EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2019 on the deployment of liaison officers in the field of migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to promote the joint deployment of EU immigration officers to third countries in order to improve operational cooperation and information sharing between EU countries and to respond to needs at EU level.
It recasts and repeals the previous regulation on immigration.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, the European Commission and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (BERC). It does not apply to the United Kingdom (1), Ireland (1) and Denmark (1).
EU countries are required to ensure that their competent authorities cooperate with each other on migration-related issues and that liaison officers are deployed by different competent authorities and that their mandates and tasks may overlap.
Each EU country must designate an immigration liaison officer to deal with migration issues. These officers are responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on irregular and uncontrolled migratory flows;
ensuring the efficient management of migration flows; and
monitoring the return operations of the EU’s border control and border control authorities.
The European Commission may deploy liaison officers on a temporary or longer-term basis to third-country countries.
exchange information with the relevant national authorities and the relevant international organisations.
Information collected by liaison officers must be made available through a secure web-based information exchange platform in accordance with applicable EU data protection legislation.
Liability of data
The Commission and EU countries must ensure that the information collected by the liaison officers is used only for the purposes of implementing the EU migration policy. The Commission must also ensure that data are not transferred to the national competent authorities of non-european countries. In the case of readmission agreements, personal data must be transferred only to the competent authorities in the EU countries for the purpose of implementing return operations.
In addition, the Commission must:
ensure that information collected and exchanged by the officers is made available to the relevant authorities of the countries covered by the regulation;
notify the European Parliament and of the Council of the European Union of any changes to the annexes to the regulation concerning the application of certain provisions of the Schengen acquis;
keep a record of the information that the officers collect, analyse and transmit to the Commission, the EU agencies and other EU instruments or structures, including the work of local cooperation groups, in particular by focusing on operational aspects.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 18 February 2019.

Summary 14:
Security of identity cards and residence cards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1030/2002 on identity cards
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the security of EU citizens’ identity cards.
It sets out the security standards and technical specifications for identity cards, as well as residence cards, issued in the EU.
KEY POINTS
  
The regulation applies to EU citizens and their family members who are not nationals of an EU country and who do not have the necessary travel documents.
Issuing and renewing identity cards or passports
EU countries are to issue and renew identity cards to their nationals in accordance with national laws.
Residence cards issued to citizens of the EU are to be called ‘Residence card of a family member of a EU citizen’.
The European Commission has delegated powers to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEA) to ensure that the regulation is implemented in line with the EU’s rules on the right of free movement of citizens.
In addition, the EEA has adopted a number of implementing acts, including:
Directive 2004/38/EC on travel documents for EU citizens, and
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on biometric passports and residence permits for third-country nationals.
Implementing powers conferred on the Commission are conferred on it in order to:
to ensure that future security standards are taken into account, where appropriate;
to implement the technical specifications adopted by the European Commission for the identity cards; and
to adopt the standards and specifications for the biometric passport and residence card.
To ensure that these standards are not undermine the interoperability of the card and residence documents, EU countries must ensure that they do not diminish the efficiency of the common security features or negatively affect the cross-border compatibility of the cards, such as the capability that the cards can be read by machines used by EU countries other than those which issue the cards. The Commission must also take into account the specific needs of children and of vulnerable persons.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2002.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management (ESP) and biometric matching services
Interoperation of EU Information systems for borders, security, security and migration
SUMMARY OF:
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions — European search portal for border and immigration management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE COMMUNICATION?
It sets out a new approach to the management of data for borders and migration.
KEY POINTS
The communication calls on the European Union (EU) authorities and EU agencies to:
ensure the fast, seamless, efficient, systematic and controlled access by Member State authorities and European Union agencies to the EES, VIS, ETIAS, Eurodac, Europol, SIS and ECRIS-TCN databases;
provide access for the purposes of preventing, detecting or investigating terrorist offences or other serious criminal offences to Europol and Interpol databases, insofar as this is needed to perform their tasks in accordance with their access rights.
To achieve this, the communication calls for:
the creation of an interoperable search portal, a shared biometric match service (shared BMS), a common identity repository (CIR) and a multiple-identity detector (MID) to be established as interoperability components;
a common identity database to be set up in the CIR;
the use of biometric data for the purpose of identifying persons and travel documents;
an interoperability of the shared BMS and the use of the MID.
The Communication calls on Member States and the European Border and Coast Guard to ensure that national authorities and border control authorities have the necessary information at their disposal to prevent, detect or investigate terrorist offences.

The Commission has adopted the communication on behalf of the European Council.

Summary 16:
Renewable energy target for 2030
Renewables for 2030 (recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1999 — renewable energy target
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
It recasts and repeals Directive 2008/98/EC on renewable energy.
It aims to encourage the deployment of renewable energy in the electricity sector, the heating and cooling sector and the transport sector.
KEY POINTS
The directive sets out the rules for renewable energy procurement in the European Union (EU).
EU countries must:
allow renewable energy projects to participate in tendering procedures and direct marketing;
allow the participation of installations located on their territory to tenders opened to them by other EU countries.
Open to all producers of electricity from renewable sources on a non-discriminatory basis;
open renewable energy support schemes to all renewable energy producers in the EU.
The European Commission must assess the cost-effectiveness of these schemes, including:
the overall cost-benefit ratio, i.e. capital financing costs, on the costs of project development and therefore on the overall cost of deploying renewable energy;
the contribution of hydropower and wind power for the purposes of calculating the renewable energy contribution to the EU target.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that the calculation methods take into account the share of renewable electricity capacity used in such systems in all end-use sectors.
They must also ensure that renewable energy used in electricity transmission and distribution systems is calculated in accordance with the EU’sustainable energy efficiency targets.
the European Commission may take measures at EU level in order to ensure the achievement of the target if an ambition gap is identified during the assessment of the integrated national energy and climate plans.
If a country does not maintain its baseline share over a 12-month period, it must, within 12 months of the end of that period, take additional measures to regain that baseline share. The Commission may also take measures that may allow for cost-effective integration of such additional renewable energy capacity, whether they are of a regulatory nature (for instance related to market design) or provide for additional investments in various sources of flexibility (such as interconnections, storage, demand response or flexible generation).
The Commission must also assess the impact of cross-border support schemes on the internal energy market and can, under certain conditions, help Member States achieve the target more cost-efficiently.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  The renewable energy targets for 2030 and Member States’ contributions to that target, including their baseline shares in relation to their national overall targets for 2020, are among the elements which have an overarching importance for the EU's energy and environmental policy.
For more information, see:
‘The renewable energy progress report’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 17:
Regulation of electronic communications networks and services — European electronic communications code (EEC)
Regulation (EU) 2016/679 on the European electronic communication code
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2016/698/EU — European Electronic Communications Code
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to establish a single European electronic Communications Code (ECC) for electronic communications services.
It recasts and repeals Directive 2002/19/EC, 2002/20/EC and 2002/22/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all electronic communication services, including:
internet access services as defined in Article 2 of Directive 2014/53/EU and consumer equipment used for digital television;
interpersonal communications services;
communications services consisting wholly or mainly in the conveyance of signals.
The scope of the directive also extends to:
radio equipment;
the use of radio spectrum;
mobile radio equipment.
Rights and obligations
The EEC covers:
the right of the end-user to be paid for access to a service;
access to the internet, and services not covered by this directive, such as the provision of web-based and not communications-related content.
In addition, the directive sets out the rights and obligations of the service provider to ensure that the service complies with the rules of the EEC and that it is not liable for infringements of these rights.
Rules on remuneration
EU countries must ensure that service providers do not charge for the right of access to the service and that they do not to charge users for advertising.
the end-users to be exposed to advertisements as a condition for gaining access to an electronic communications service
the service provider is liable for the payment of a fee for providing the service.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEAR ENTER INTO FORCE?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise and modernise the EU’s electronic communications regulatory framework. These include:
recasting and recasting the existing Directives;
recasting and recasting the five previous Directives on electronic communications;
recasting Regulation (EC) 2002/58/EC;
decisions on the application of the rules and obligations contained in the directive.

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of heavy-duty vehicles’ CO2 emissions and fuel consumption
Monitoring & reporting of light commercial vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2400 — monitoring and reporting requirements for manufacturers and the European Environment Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the starting years for the monitoring* and reporting* requirements of manufacturers for the emission* and fuel* emissions* of heavy commercial vehicles in the European Union (EU).
It establishes a system for the exchange of data between the competent authorities of the EU countries and manufacturers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
new heavy vehicles and all new trailers;
new trailers falling within the scope of the regulation;
light commercial vehicles falling within its scope;
heavy-duty vehicle categories falling under its scope.
EU Member States must monitor and report to the European Commission data on the registration of all new heavy and new trailers, including data on powertrains as well as the relevant bodywork.
Manufacturers must report the following data to the Commission:
the number of heavy vehicles registered in the Member States;
the total number of new heavy vehicles;
fuel consumption;
air drag value ranges.
The Commission may impose a fine on manufacturers if they fail to report the required data within the applicable deadline or if the manufacturer deviates from the data.
Reporting and reporting procedure
The Member States are responsible for:
monitoring the results of on-road verification tests;
reporting the aerodynamic performance of the vehicles; and
reporting to the air drag values of the heavy vehicles.
In addition, the Commission has the power to impose fines on manufacturers for any infringements of the reporting obligations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions in the light commercial vehicle sector.
For more information, see:
Light commercial vehicles (European Commission).

Summary 19:
Protection of the security of gas supply in the EU in the event of disruptions of the gas supply (Regulation (EU) 2016/852)
Protection and mitigation of the Security of Gas Supply in the European Union in the Event of Disruption of the Gas Supply
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EC) No 852/2016 on the protection of the internal gas market in the case of disruptions in the supply of natural gas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all the necessary measures are taken to safeguard an uninterrupted supply of gas throughout the EU, in particular to protected customers.
It sets out the rules and procedures for the preparation and implementation of preventive action plans and emergency plans.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are not covered by the regulation.
Preventive action plans
Each EU country must establish a risk-based approach based on the major transnational risks to the safety of its gas supply. These risk groups are defined based on:
the risk of a natural gas supply disruption from Russia;
the risks of a disruption of the transmission system from non-EU countries.
To ensure maximum preparedness, the competent authorities of a given risk group must, after consulting stakeholders, establish a preventive action plan and an emergency plan that will contain regional chapters.
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up the regional chapters of these plans and the emergency plans, which must be approved by the national authorities of each EU country.
National authorities must consult the European Network of Transmission System Operators (ENTSOGS) and the European Coordination System for Gas.
Regional coordination system for gas flows
In addition, ENTSOG, a network of transmission system operators, must be involved in carrying out simulations.
EU countries must ensure that:
gas flows are not diverted from the EU’s internal energy market;
transmission system operators are required to enable permanent physical bi-directional capacity on all cross-border interconnections unless an exemption has been granted from that obligation.
If a gas supply is interrupted, the affected system users are entitled to a fair compensation to be applied directly and promptly between them.
An emergency plan must be in place for the duration of any gas supply interruption. It must include the following:
a description of the measures to be taken by national authorities in case of a supply disruption;
a list of the affected gas users;
an estimate of the total volume of gas to be supplied to the affected market users; and
the number of people covered by solidarity protected customers, including healthcare, essential social care, emergency and security services, including where those services are performed by a public administration.
Emergency plans
The emergency plans must include a description of all the measures that national authorities must take to ensure the continuity of supply to the protected market users, including:
measures to reduce the impact of any disruption of supply on the health and safety of the protected customers;
measures for the restoration of gas flows to the regulated market users.
In case of an emergency, national authorities should:
ensure that the emergency plan is in place;
take all necessary measures to restore gas flows;
provide information to the public and the public authorities of the EU countries concerned;
make available to the European Commission the information necessary for the implementation of the plan;
draw up a list of gas users covered by national emergency plans;
identify the number of persons covered by EU countries’ emergency plans and national authorities’ national contingency plans, as well as the number and duration of the national emergency plan.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  баланскабаканка (European Commission)
Directive 2014/40/EU on the security and resilience of the European gas system (OJ L 347, 30.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
See consolidated version.

‘Protected customers’ on the European Agency for Safety and Security at Work’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* Solidarity protected customer: any EU country that has a national authority responsible for protecting its citizens’ health, safety and security. It is therefore possible for EU countries to treat, in accordance with that framework, healthcare, social care and emergency services as solidarity protected citizens, including if they are provided by public administrations.

Summary 20:
Energy efficiency of energy-related products: energy label and information sheets (Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on energy efficiency)
Energy efficiency: Energy label and product information sheets
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — energy label
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the energy label* and the product information sheet* of products on sale in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that consumers can make informed choices when choosing energy-efficient products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products placed on the EU market for the first time, including second-hand imported products. It does not apply to products that have already been on the market for a limited period of time.
It does not cover:
energy-related electrical appliances;
space and water heating products;
water heaters;
air-conditioning systems;
gas-powered heating and cooling systems.
Ecodesign
Products must bear the following information on the label:
the energy class of the product and the range of efficiency classes;
the number of products sold in the top classes of class A, B, C, D, E, F, G, H, I, J, K, L, M, N, N and P.
the total energy consumption.
Suppliers of products marketed in accordance with Directive 2010/30/EU before the date of entry into force of this regulation must ensure that the energy class and the efficiency classes are clearly visible on the product’s label.
they must also indicate the absolute energy consumption in addition to the label scale, in order to allow customers to predict the direct impact of their choices on their energy bills.
Where it is not feasible to display the product in all forms of distance selling, visual advertisements and technical promotional material, potential customers should be provided at least with the product class and efficiency classes.
The European Commission must set up a product database consisting of a public and a compliance part, which should be accessible via an online portal. It should also be possible to access the technical documentation uploaded to the product database by suppliers on a voluntary basis.
If a product group is rescaled, it should be avoided by replacing the labels on the affected products displayed in shops within a short timeframe, and by organising adequate consumer information campaigns clearly indicating that a new version of the label has been introduced.
In addition, the Commission should set up an electronic tool for dealers to receive information sheets, to facilitate the monitoring of compliance and to provide up-to-date market data for the regulatory process on revisions of product-specific labels.
EU Member States must ensure the following:
suppliers submit technical documentation to the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee on a regular basis;
consumers have the right to ask the Commission for technical information on products they buy and to be informed of any changes to their energy label. The Commission must make this information available on its website.
Consumers must be able to ask questions of suppliers and the Commission on the basis of the information they have been provided.
National market surveillance authorities have the power to access that information when necessary, either by way of technical documentation or by means of additional parts of the electronic product database uploaded by suppliers. They must inform the Commission of any infringements of the provisions of the regulation and the delegated acts adopted pursuant thereto.
Importers must ensure their products comply with the regulation.
Manufacturers and importers of products are responsible for ensuring that their products meet the requirements laid down in this regulation. They are also responsible for:
ensuring that the products they place on the European market conform to the requirements set out in the regulation;
providing the Commission with technical documentation on their products and the information it needs to assess the products’ energy efficiency;
notifying the Commission, the European Trade and Consumers’ Representative and the Member States of any amendments to the regulation that they consider necessary to ensure the product complies with the requirements;
making sure that the Commission has access to all the technical information that they have provided to the database.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Directive 2010/31/EU has applied since 1 January 2018.
Regulation(EU) 2018/856 has applied from 1 January 2019.
Amending Regulation (EC, EC) No 768/2008 has applied to energy-based products since 1 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
Energy label
For more information, see:
‘Energy label’ (European Commission).

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data in the EU
Protecting data on the internet
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/680 on the protection of personal data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the protection and management of the personal data of natural persons in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rights and obligations of those who process and determine the processing of their personal data, as well as equivalent powers for monitoring and ensuring compliance with the rules of the regulation.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to natural persons, whatever their nationality or place of residence, in relation to:
processing of their data by EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies;
processing by a controller or processor not established in an EU country;
monitoring of the behaviour of such data subjects in so far as their behaviour takes place within the EU;
the processing of data subjects who are in another EU country.
It does not apply to the processing by an intermediary service provider.
EU countries may entrust competent authorities with tasks which are not necessarily carried out for the prevention, investigation, detection or prosecution of criminal offences or the execution of criminal penalties, including the safeguarding against and preventing threats to public security, so that the processing for those other purposes falls within the scope of this regulation. However, for the performance of a task carried out in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority vested in the controller, EU countries are allowed to maintain or introduce national provisions to further specify the application of the rules applicable to such tasks.
Directive 95/46/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 1995 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States concerning the free movement of data between Member States (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2016.

Summary 22:
Harmonised indices of consumer prices (HICP, HICP-CT, OOH price index) and of the house price index (HPI) at EU and national level.
HARMONIED INDICES OF CONSUMER PRICES
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1176/2011 laying down a common framework for the development, production and dissemination of harmonised indices* of consumer price indices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the common framework to develop, produce and disseminate harmonised index* of:
the harmonised consumer price index;
the household final monetary consumption expenditure index (OHCP);
the home price index.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It covers the following categories of products:
food and beverages;
goods and services;
services;
other goods and services.
The indices are based on:
a ‘Laspeyres-type index’: the price of a product is denoted by p, the price reference period is 0, and the comparison period is t.
An ‘OOH’ price index is based on the price changes and weights of products included in the household final money consumption expenditure.
A ‘House Price Index’ (HCP-CT) is a weighted index of the prices of the products that make up a household’s total monetary consumption. It covers the whole of the EU.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with the harmonised statistics as defined in point (12) of Article 2.
Eurostat must verify the sources and methods used by EU countries to calculate the indices and monitor the implementation of the regulation.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts concern:
sampling and representativity;
collection and treatment of prices;
replacements and quality adjustment;
index compilation;
revisions;
special indices;
treatment of products in specific areas.
In addition, the Commission must adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 10 for the amendment of Annex I, in order to ensure comparability of the indices at international level. These implementing acts must be adopted by the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC).
The Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) on the application of this regulation. The Commission must also submit a proposal for amending this regulation with regard to the inclusion of the OOH index into the HCP coverage.
Repeal
The Regulation repeals Regulation (EC) No 2494/95 and its subsequent amendments.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  I.
For more information, see:
‘The Harmonised Indices of Consumer Prices’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Consumer price index: an index based on observed prices, which include taxes on products. It is the main indicator of price stability in the EU and is used by Eurostat to assess the economic situation and the state of the economy.
OOH: a weighted average of the price change and the weights of all the products in a household.
House Price index: the average price change of a household over a 10-year period, which includes taxes on all the items in that household. The index covers the entire of the country, except for Denmark.

Summary 23:
Anti-dumping measures
Anti-deflating measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2004 on the application of the 1994 Anti-Dumping Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 652/2003, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2004.
It aims to ensure that the rules of the agreement are applied correctly.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to imports of goods from non-EU countries into the EU and to exports of goods to the EU. It does not apply to the following:
the import of agricultural products from the EU or a non-member country of the World Trade Organisation (WTO) which are subject to provisional duties;
the export of agricultural goods to non-WTO countries which are not subject to a provisional duty.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2005.
Provisional duties may be imposed no earlier than 60 days from initiation and no later than 9 months thereafter.
Imposition of provisional duties may also be imposed:
within 60 days of the start-up of an investigation;
within 9 months of the end of the investigation; and
no later than 30 days from the end.
Investigations or proceedings may be terminated where the dumping is de minimis or the injury is negligible, and it is appropriate to define those situations.
In addition, the regulation provides for a procedure for interim reviews or investigations to determine whether provisional duties are warranted.
An advisory procedure is used for the adoption of provisional measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Anti-Deflating Measures’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 24:
Countervailing measures against unfair subsidies
Countervailable measures against unjustified subsidies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 on the application of the rules provided for in the Subsidies Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC, No 594/2004) No 652/2004, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2006.
KEY POINTS
Scope
A product is considered to be subsidised if it benefits from a countervailable subsidy (i.e. if it is imported from the country of origin but is exported to the EU from an intermediate country).
The regulation defines the terms ‘subsidies’ and ‘countervailable subsidies’.
Subsidies must be specific and must be calculated according to the following criteria:
the amount of the total subsidy;
the total value of the subsidy.
The amount of a subsidy must be determined by allocating the value of imports and exports of the product to the European Union (EU).
A subsidy may not be contingent on export performance, unless the facts demonstrate that it is in fact tied to actual or anticipated exportation or export earnings.
A loan guarantee by a government is not considered to confer a benefit, unless there is a difference between the amount that the firm receiving the guarantee pays on a loan guaranteed by the government and the amount of commercial loan in the absence of the government guarantee.
Provisional duties may be imposed on the export of a product if they are necessary to offset the subsidy or to prevent it from spreading to other EU countries.
Defensive measures may be adopted to prevent the spread of the subsidies.
EU countries may apply safeguard measures to protect the legitimate interests of the recipients of subsidies. These measures may include:
suspension of the granting authority’s powers to grant subsidies;
imposition of provisional duties on imports of the same product from non-EU countries;
restrictions on export taxes, duties or other charges levied on exports of that product.
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 sets out the conditions for adopting provisional and definitive duties, and for the termination of an investigation without measures. It also lays down the procedures for the rejection of complaints or the initiation of proceedings.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2006. It repeals and replaces Regulation (EEC, no 594)/2004.

Summary 25:
Facility for the recovery and resilience of the EU’s economic and financial recovery and recovery plans (Facility 2020/2094) (European Semester for Economic Policy Coordination)
Facilitation Facility for the Recovery and Resilience of the European Economic and Financial Union (Fiscal Semester)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on the Facility for Economic and Fiscal Coordination for the European Semester
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the facility for economic and fiscal policy coordination (FEC) for Member States in the European Union (EU).
It aims to help Member States to recover from the COVID-19 crisis and to prepare for and respond to future shocks.
It sets out the specific objectives of the Facility.
KEY POINTS
The Facility is designed to:
provide financial support to Member States for the implementation of reforms and investments set out in their recovery and resilient plans;
ensure that the measures supported by the Facility are consistent with the objectives and priorities identified in the framework of the ‘European semester for economic policy coordination’;
contribute to the green transition, including biodiversity, the development of sustainable infrastructure and transport, and industrialisation and reindustrialisation;
mitigate the effect of the crisis on the economy;
promote entrepreneurship, social economy, the sustainable development of the social economy and the industrialisation of the economy, and mitigate the impact of COVID–19.
Member States must ensure that measures included in their plans comply with the principle of ‘do no significant harm’ within the meaning of Article 17 of Regulation (EU) 2020/852 of the Treaty on the European Pillar of Social Rights (see summary).
The Regulation sets out:
a maximum financial contribution calculated for each Member State and taking into account the estimated total costs of the plan, which should be paid based on the achievement of results by reference to milestones and targets of the plans.
A mechanism to ensure the link between the Facility and sound economic governance is established, allowing the Commission to suspend all or part of the commitments or payments under the Facility if it considers that Member States have not met their financial commitments or commitments.
The Commission may also decide to suspend or reduce the amount of financial support provided.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
The COVID‑19 crisis had serious economic and social consequences as well as asymmetrical effects on Member States and had to be dealt with swiftly and effectively.
For more information, see:
‘Recovery and resilience’ on the Commission's website.
