Summary 26:
Technical support instrument for EU countries’ efforts to implement reforms (until 2023) (Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2021/241) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation establishing a technical support instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) instrument to continue and enhance support for Member States in the implementation of reforms (the ‘instrument’).
It aims to promote the EU’s economic, social and territorial cohesion by supporting Member States efforts to:
design, develop and implement reforms;
prepare, amend, implement and revise recovery and resilience plans;
ensure that the Commission can organise additional dedicated calls in response to specific emerging needs of Member States, including, as a matter of priority, for the preparation, amendment, implementation and revision of recovery and resilient plans.
It sets out the management modes, the forms of funding for the technical support measures and the content of work programmes, which must be adopted by way of implementing acts.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument is designed to support:
the implementation of the European Semester for economic policy coordination;
the preparation, development and implementation of economic adjustment programmes;
reforms in the context of economic governance processes, in particular those effectively addressing the country-specific recommendations or actions related to implementation of EU law, and reforms in relation to the implementing an economic adjustment programme.
Objectives
The general objective of the instrument is to promote:
economic and social cohesion by:
improving the quality of the labour market;
strengthening the capacity of the public administration;
increasing the effectiveness of the judicial and administrative systems;
enhancing the efficiency of the administrative system.
The specific objectives are to assist national authorities in their endeavours to design and develop reforms, including through exchange of good practices, appropriate processes and methodologies, stakeholder involvement, where appropriate, and more effective and efficient human resources management.
In addition, the instrument aims to tackle broader environmental and social challenges within the EU, including the protection of natural capital, preserving biodiversity and the support to the circular economy and the energy transition, in accordance with the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development.
Funding
The regulation sets out a financial envelope for the instrument, which is to constitute the prime reference amount, within the meaning of the Interinstitutional Agreement of 16 December 2020 between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission on budgetary discipline, on cooperation in budgetary matters and on sound financial management, as well as on new own resources, including a roadmap towards the introduction of new own funds.
Implementation
The European Parliament and the Council of the EU have the power to adopt an annual report on the instrument's implementation and to invite the Commission to participate in an exchange of views with the competent committee.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 1 January 2021.

Summary 27:
Compensation and assistance for rail passengers: Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 (recast) (European Union (EU) 2017/852)
Compensation, assistance and assistance in the event of delay, missed connection or cancellation of a rail passenger service: Regulation [Official Journal L 347 of 20.11.2017].
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rights of rail passengers to:
recover or compensation in the case of delays, missed connections or cancellations of rail journeys;
assistance to persons with disabilities and persons with reduced mobility;
reimbursement or assistance in case of death of a passenger.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to rail passengers taking international and domestic journeys. It does not apply to rail passenger services offered for historic or touristic use.
Passengers must be able to request reimbursement or assistance if:
the delay is caused by extraordinary circumstances such as extreme weather conditions or major natural disasters endangering the safe operation of the service;
the train is delayed for more than 30 minutes;
there is a delay of more than 2 hours; or
the service is cancelled because of a technical fault.
A railway undertaking is not obliged to pay compensation if it can prove that the delay was due to extraordinary circumstances, such as a major natural disaster or the absence of a train.
EU countries may exempt regional services from certain obligations as regards the provision of through-tickets and re-routing.
In addition to the above, EU countries may also grant exemptions from the obligation to provide through-ticketing.
European Union countries must ensure that:
travel information is provided to passengers;
rail ticket vending and tour operators provide information to passengers concerning the accessibility of rail services, access conditions of rolling stock and the facilities on board.
The European Commission must ensure the following:
passengers have access to travel information on rail services;
passenger information is available on the website of the railway undertaking or the railway company providing the service.
the European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
Rail passengers have the right to be reimbursed or reimbursed in the following cases:
delays,
missed connections,
cancelled services,
delay of trains,
disruption of services, and
the cancellation of services.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852 amends the regulation to ensure that the European Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts in the light of developments in the field of transport policy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive 2009/34/EC establishes the European Union’s legal framework for the carriage of passengers by rail. It repeals and replaces the previous regulation, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2007.
It applies from 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality of rail transport in order to encourage the wider use of public transport. These include:
improving access to rail services for people with disabilities;
ensuring that rail passengers have equal access to the same level of protection as the general public;
strengthening rights of compensation and assistance;
introducing the right of travel information for passengers.
For more information, see:
Passenger rights (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Through ticket: a ticket issued by a railway company to passengers travelling by rail, i.e. a ticket that is valid for the duration of the journey.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   ம  ̱  
Regulation (EC, No 1372/2007 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 December 2007 establishing a framework for rail passenger rights (Recast) (OJ L 347, 20.10.2007, pp. 1–8)

Summary 28:
Pre-accession assistance for the Western Balkans
Pre–accession aid for the western Balkans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/947 establishing an instrument for pre‐accession* assistance to the beneficiaries listed in Annex I for the 2021-2027 period (IPA III)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union instrument for providing pre‐Accession assistance to beneficiaries of the Western Balkan Association (WBA) for the period 2021–2027.
It aims to support the efforts of the beneficiaries to advance regional, macro‐regional and cross‐border cooperation and territorial development, including through the implementation of EU macro-regional strategies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the period between 2021 and 2027. It does not apply to:
the Western Balkans’ accession process;
the implementation of the United Nations 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development adopted in September 2015 as a universal agenda, to which the EU and its Member States are fully committed and which all beneficiaries have endorsed.
Beneficiaries covered by the regulation are:
a number of countries, including Albania, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Montenegro, Kosovo, Serbia and Montenegro;
an EU country, the Republic of Macedonia;
a non-EU country (non-euro area country) that is not part of the enlargement process.
Funding
The Regulation sets out the conditions for the EU’s financial support for the WBA. It is based on a performance-based approach and the fair share principle, ensuring progress in all beneficiaries.
The beneficiaries covered by this regulation must:
ensure that their progress is recognised and recognised by the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EPEU);
promote the development of a social market economy;
take the necessary measures to tackle poverty and inequality;
improve the quality of education and training for young people;
achieve sustainable development and climate change goals;
ensuring that the region is prepared to address global challenges.
In addition, the regulation sets out a number of specific objectives, including:
strengthening gender equality and the empowerment of women and girls in EU external action and increasing efforts to reach the minimum standards of performance indicated by the EU Gender Action Plans;
promoting good neighbourly relations and regional cooperation;
developing the capacity of regional and cross-border cooperation, territorial development and regional integration;
contributing to the sustainable development of the region and to the fight against climate change.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
The WBA is a candidate country for European Union membership. The European Commission emphasised the firm, merit-based prospect of EU membership for the region in its communication of 6 February 2018 entitled ‘A credible enlargement perspective for and enhanced EU engagement with the West Balkans.’

Summary 29:
EU development cooperation instrument (EDI) 2018-2030 — implementing the 2030 Agenda and the Addis Ababa Action Agenda (ODA)
EU Development Cooperation Instrument (ODA) 2018/2030
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Decision (EU) 2019/2031 establishing the EU development cooperation Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE INITIATION?
The decision establishes the European Union’s (EU's) instrument for development cooperation.
The instrument aims to provide the EU with a common approach to development cooperation by the EU and its Member States to implement the sustainable economic, social and environmental development of developing countries, with the primary aim of eradicating poverty, as well as to strengthen international peace, preventing conflicts and strengthening international security.
KEY POINTS
The objective of the instrument is to:
contribute to the achievement of the 2030 Sustainable Development Agenda (Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs);
support the implementation of the EU 2030 Agenda;
promote sustainable development in Africa, Asia and Latin America;
support regional cooperation in the area of security, migration, climate change and the environment;
strengthen regional cooperation with the African Union (ACP) and the Caribbean and Pacific (ACP).
The development cooperation policy also contributes to the EU's external action, in particular to foster sustainable economic and social development in developing countries.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
The Instrument entered into force on 1 January 2019.
It has been in force since 1 January 2018.
.
BACKGROUND
The 2030 Agenda is a transformative framework to eradicate poverty and achieve sustainable development globally. The 2030 Agenda, together with the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’), and the 3rd International Conference on Financing for Development (the AddisAbroad Action Agenda, the ‘Addis Abuba Action Agenda’, is the international community's response to global challenges and trends in relation to sustainable development.
For more information, see:
‘The EU's development cooperation instruments’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 30:
Whistleblowers’ protection: new rules for the protection of whistle-blowers (EU directive on the protection against reprisals) (Directive (EU) 2017/1371)
Whistle-blower’s protection: New rules for protecting whistleblowers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2013/30/EU — protection of whistleblowers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the protection for whistleblowers who report breaches of EU law that are harmful to the public interest.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to the EU countries not bound by the EU financial services directive.
EU countries must ensure that whistleblowers are protected against retaliation for reporting evasive and/or abusive arrangements that could otherwise go undetected, with a view to strengthening the ability of competent authorities to safeguard the proper functioning of the internal market and remove distortions and barriers to trade that affect the competitiveness of businesses in the EU, and that are directly linked to the free movement of businesses.
It does not cover:
public procurement;
the supply of products or the provision of services.
Reporting of breaches by whistleblowers can be key to detecting and preventing, reducing or eliminating risks to public health and to consumer protection resulting from breaches of Union rules, which might otherwise remain hidden.
In addition, whistleblowers can help to disclose breaches in the area of the food chain and animal health, protection and welfare, as well as on animal health and welfare.
the protection of personal data.
Scope of the directive
the directive covers:
food and feed safety;
animal health;
environmental protection;
health and welfare;
security;
information security.
information protection.
Protection of whistleblowers’ personal data
The legislation also applies to:
employers’ organisations;
public authorities;
contractors’ bodies;
state authorities; and
the European Anti-Fraud Office for the Prohibition of Transnational Organised Crime (OLAF).
Scope and scope
The scope of the legislation does not include:
the provision of goods and services;
recipients’ rights and obligations in relation to the execution of works, the supply of goods or services, or
the procurement of products and services, and
public access to information.
Exemptions from protection
The law does not extend to the following areas:
transport and logistics;
human resources management;
social security;
employment and social security systems;
education and training;
emergency services and health and safety.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management
European integration of the external borders
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — strengthening the European Border and Coast Guard (EBCG)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union Agency for the Protection of the Borders of the Schengen Area (EUBG).
It aims to strengthen the EBCG’s capacity to respond to any challenges at its external borders.
KEY POINTS
The EUBG has a mandate to:
prevent and detect cross-border crime, such as migrant smuggling, trafficking in human beings and terrorism, where it has obtained relevant information through its activities;
cooperate with EU countries and the European Commission on border surveillance and the carrying out of border checks;
ensure constant preparedness at both EU and national levels;
organise and coordinate rapid border interventions and rapid border response teams;
provide information and cooperation between EU countries’ national authorities and the ECBG;
exchange information and cooperate with the Commission.
The regulation establishes a multiannual cycle for providing strategic guidelines to all the relevant actors at EU level and at national level in the area of border management and return so that those actors are able to implement European integrated border control in a coherent manner.
It also establishes a framework for the exchange of information and the operational cooperation between the EGBG and the Commission, as well as for the establishment of a standing corps (the ‘standing corps’).
The standing corps are composed of officers from EU countries. They are responsible for:
border surveillance and border control;
border control and border surveillance; and
border checks.
In addition, the standing corps:
are equipped with:
emergency response equipment;
are based in the EU countries;
operate at the request of the competent authorities of the EU and the Member States;
have the capacity to detect small vessels;
carry out search and rescue operations;
conduct risk analysis and risk assessments;
coordinate rapid border intervention and rapid response teams.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation has applied since 1 May 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the protection of its borders and to restore the normal functioning of the area. Since taking up its responsibilities on 1 May 2005, it has been successful in assisting Member States with implementing the operational aspects of external border management through joint operations, risk analysis, information exchange, relations with non-EU countries, and the return of returnees.
Since the beginning of the migratory crisis in 2015, the Commission has taken up important initiatives and has proposed a series of measures with a view to strengthening the security of the borders and restoring the normal function of the European Schengun area.
For more information, see:
European integrated borders (European Commission).

Information and communications technology (ICT) cybersecurity
Information and communication technology
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1148 on cybersecurity
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Network and Information Security Agency (ENISA).
It aims to improve cybersecurity in the EU so that network and information systems, communications networks, digital products, services and devices used by citizens, organisations and businesses are better protected from cyber threats.
KEY POINTS

It is responsible for:
ensuring that the security of the ICT products, ICT services or ICT processes designed by organisations and the public sector are configured in a way that ensures a higher level of security, thereby reducing the burden on users of having to configure an ICT product.
Assisting the development and updating of cybersecurity strategies at EU level and, upon request, at EU country level, in particular on cybersecurity, and promoting the dissemination of such strategies and follow the progress of their implementation.
Ensuring the exchange of best practices between EU countries.
Providing operational support to the operational cooperation of EU countries in the field of cybersecurity.
Implementing the Cybersecurity Strategy of the European Union (2013) and the last revision of ENISA's mandate.
In addition, the regulation establishes a Cooperation Group created by the regulation. It is composed of representatives of the EU countries, the European Commission and the European Institute of Cybersecurity (EICS).

It includes, among other things, the ability to detect, prevent and respond to cyber threats, as well as the prevention of and response to these threats. It also includes, for example, the protection of information and communication technologies (ICTs) against the threat of cyber attacks.

Summary 33:
Gross national product (GNI) and Gross national income (GNA) data
Gross National Product (GNI)* data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 establishing a procedure to verify and assess the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of Gross National Product and Gross National Income data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for the European Commission to verify, assess and verify the quality and reliability of the comparable, reliable and exhaustive quality of gross national product and gross national income data.
It repeals Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1287/2003 and repeals Council Decision 2014/335/EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the methodology for the calculation of the gross national productivity (GNP) and gross domestic product (GDP) data and GNI.
GDP is the final result of the production activity of resident producer units. It is also the balancing item in the total economy production account;
expenditure approach: GDP is the sum of final uses of goods and services by resident institutional units (final consumption and gross capital formation) plus exports and minus imports of goods, services and services;
income approach: Gross income account (compensation of employees, taxes on production and imports less subsidies, gross operating surplus and mixed income of the economy).
EU countries must calculate GNI as defined in Article 1 in the context of national accounts compilation.
The Commission (Eurostat) is responsible for the verification of the quality of the GNI data. It carries out GNI information visits in order to verify the sources, their uses and the methods in the inventory of GNI aggregates and their components and to verify compliance with ESA 2010, as well as to ensure that the data are comparable.
In order to ensure the reliability, exhaustiveness and highest possible degree of comparability of the data, the power to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) is delegated to the Commission in respect of the list of issues to be addressed in every verification cycle. The Commission must establish, by means of implementing acts, specific measures to make GNI and GDP data more comparable and exhaustive, based on the list defined in the delegated acts referred to in the second subparagraph of paragraph 2 of this regulation.
Implementing acts
Each year, the Commission must draw up an inventory guide in close cooperation with the expert group set up by the GNP Committee and the European System of Social and Economic Accounts Committee (ESSC). The inventory guide must be consistent with the ESA 2010 and duplication and overloading must be avoided.
National statistical authorities of EU countries and Eurostat must cooperate closely in the preparation of the inventory guide.
Every 5 years, Eurostat carries out verification visits to each EU country to check the quality, the reliability and the exhaustiveness of the information it has collected.
Each 5-year verification cycle, it must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Commission on the results of the verification visits and on the implementation of the implementing acts.
Repeal
This regulation repeals and replaces Council Regulation (EEC) No 1193/2003.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 18 April 2013.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Gross national product: an annual GNI at market prices under the methodology set out in Article 2(7) of the EU system of accounts (ESA 2010) which established the revised European system of Accounts (see summary).
* GDP: the final product of the final production of resident producers units.
** Gross domestic product: the total output of the total economic activity of all resident producers.
* Income account: the income of resident employees.
ACT
Council Regulation (U.S.A. 2017/852/EU of 18 April 2017 establishing a process for the verification and assessment of the reliability of Gross national Product (GNG) data (OJ L 127, 18.4.2017, pp. 1-13)

Summary 34:
EU type-approval rules for vehicles and systems following the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the European Union
EU type approval rules for vehicle and system approvals following the departure of the UK
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1628 on the application of certain EU rules relating to vehicle type approval
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for applying EU type approval* rules to vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units which fall within the scope of Directive 2007/46/EC (see summary).
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EC) No 167/2013 on type approval.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following types of vehicles:
vehicles and systems:
cars and systems of all types;
light commercial vehicles;
motorcycles;
trucks;
categories of light commercial vehicles.
It does not apply to certain types of systems, such as airbags, fire extinguishers, seat belts and seat belts.
Vehicle type approval procedures
Manufacturers may apply on a voluntary basis for a European Union type approval of a vehicle type previously approved in the UK, on the basis of certain requirements applicable to new types of system, components or separate technical unit, where the type of vehicle remains otherwise identical to the one approved in that country.
The vehicle manufacturer must notify the European Commission of the vehicle type they wish to be approved. The European Commission, which is responsible for granting EU type approvals, must inform the manufacturer of the new EU authority that is to be set up.
before the vehicle is placed on the EU market.
If a vehicle manufacturer wishes to apply for a type approval, the manufacturer must:
apply for a EU typeapproval for a vehicle of the same type as the one previously approved by the UK type approval authority;
use the procedure set out in the regulation;
apply to the new authority for the approval of vehicles of a different type than the one already approved by that authority.
When the vehicle manufacturer submits a vehicle for EU approval, it must inform that the vehicle meets all the requirements of the regulation and that it has been approved in accordance with the rules of the EU. The vehicle manufacturer may also apply to the EU authority to assume obligations regarding recalls, repair and maintenance information and in-service conformity checks with respect to their vehicles. The EU authority granting the EU type–approval takes over those obligations also with respect of vehicles, components, components* based on the same types and already placed on EU markets.
Where the EU–UK type‑approval procedure is not sufficiently achieved by the EU Member States but can rather, by reason of its scale and effects, be better achieved at EU level, the Union may adopt measures in order to ensure that there is a responsible type–appraval authority. The regulation also provides for an exemption from the specific requirements concerning the designation and notification of technical services of third countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
The United Kingdom withdraws from the EU and becomes a third country (non-EU country) as of 1 January 2020.
For more information, see:
The EU’s vehicles and vehicles systems (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Vehicle type: any vehicle, system or component of any type which is intended for sale in the Member States.
* System: any type of system or technical unit that is intended to be installed on a vehicle.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1628/2016 of 30 March 2016 on the approximation of the laws of Member States relating to type approval (OJ L 157, 30.3.2016, pp. 1-18)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2016 /1628 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 35:
Europol regulation on data protection and personal data protection
Europol Regulation on the protection and data protection of personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on the regulation on the application of the European Police Office (Europol)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the legal basis for Europol, the European Union’s (EU) police office.
It sets out the rules and conditions for the processing of data by Europol.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries, EU bodies, third countries, international organisations and, under stringent conditions, private parties, as well as data coming from publicly available sources, in order to develop an understanding of criminal phenomena and trends, to gather information about criminal networks, and to detect links between different criminal offences.
EU countries must provide Europol with all relevant information, with the exception of data relating to personal data, to the extent necessary for the performance of its tasks.
When Europol requests information, EU countries must also include information about any alleged cyber attacks and bilateral and multilateral exchanges of information with other EU countries on crime falling within Europol's objectives.
The management board of Europol is appointed by the European Commission and the EU countries to effectively supervise its work. It is assisted by a Management Board of Eurojust.
Member States must ensure that Europol and Eurojust have access to, in a reciprocal manner within their respective mandates, access to and the information that has been provided for the purpose of cross-checking in accordance with specific safeguards and guarantees provided for in this regulation.
In exceptional cases, Member States may cooperate directly with competent authorities in Member States, subject to the conditions defined by Member States.
Personal data are transferred to Europol only if necessary for preventing and combating crime that falls within its objectives. Member States and international organisations must determine the purpose or purposes for which Europol may process the data they provide and to restrict access rights.
Where personal data cannot be transferred, the recipient must give an undertaking that the data will be used by the recipient or transferred onward to a competent authority of a non-EU country solely for the original purpose for which they were originally transferred.
Data protection
The Management Board, in agreement with the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) is responsible for the protection of the personal data of EU citizens. The EDPS has the power to authorise Europol to transfer personal data in exceptional cases where specific rules so require and authorise the transfer of the data to the competent authorities of an EU country or to an international organisation. It must also ensure that personal data are stored in a secure, anonymous and unauthorised manner and that the transfer is subject to appropriate safeguards.
If personal data is transferred to an organisation other than Europol for a specific purpose, the transfer must be based on an adequacy decision, an international agreement concluded by the EU or an existing cooperation agreement, or on a request from a Member State, the Management Board must authorise a set of specific rules for the transfer. The Management Board is assisted in this process by the Executive Director of the Europol Directorate-General for Law Enforcement and Security.
All Member States are required to keep Europol informed of any serious cyber attacks affecting their information systems and to provide it with the fullest and most up-to-date information possible. When Europol receives a request for information on a serious attack of suspected criminal origin against information systems affecting EU bodies or two or more Member States it must respond to it without delay, taking into account the fact that the rapidity of the response is a key factor in successfully tackling computer crime.
Information provided by the Member States must be made available to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF), Eurojust and the European Judicial Network (EJN).
The European Commission, the Council of the EU and Member States should ensure that the management board is fully informed of all requests for information from the Member State concerned and that it is kept informed of the results of these requests. The management board must also make efforts to limit the turnover of its representatives, with a view to ensuring the continuity of its work, while keeping the national units informed at the latter's request.
Each Member State must also inform the European Parliament and the Council, the Court of Justice and the Committee of the Regions of any amendments to the regulation. The regulation repeals Decision 2009/371/JHA on the establishment of a European police office (the Europol Convention) and its subsequent amendments to it.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
European Police Office
About Europol (European External Action Service).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC), Euratom, Euratom and Euratom Implementing Regulation (Euratom).
Successive



Summary 36:
European instrument for temporary support to mitigate unemployment risks in an emergency (SURE) following the COVID-19 outbreak (until 1 February 2020)
European instrument of temporary support for temporary help to mitigate employment risks in the labour market following COVID‐19 outbreak
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 332/2002 establishing a European instrument for short-time work schemes
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Instrument for Temporary Support to Mitigate Unemployment Risks in an Emergency following the outbreak caused by COVID‑19.
It enables the European Union (EU) to grant EU financial assistance to an EU country that is in difficulties or is seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond its control.
KEY POINTS
The instrument is designed to provide short-term support to EU countries in the event of an outbreak of a contagious communicable disease.
EU countries may request assistance under the Instrument (‘financial assistance’) where its actual and possibly also planned public expenditure has suddenly and severely increased as of 1 February 2019 due to national measures directly related to short-temporary work schemes and similar measures to address the socio-economic effects of the exceptional occurrence caused by the outbreak.
The Member States concerned must provide the European Commission with:
the amount of the loan, its maximum average maturity, its pricing formula, the maximum number of instalments, its availability period and the other detailed rules needed for the granting of the financial assistance;
a description of the national schemes or similar measures;
an assessment of the impact of the outbreak on the labour markets of the Member States;
evidence of the measures taken by Member States to mitigate the impact on employment for individuals and the most affected economic sectors;
information of the compliance with the conditions set out in the regulation.
Member States must also provide the Commission with the following information:
an estimate of the costs of the expenditure;
the total contingent liabilities of the EU, including under the balance of payments facility established by Council Regulation (EC).
The Commission may, on a proposal from the Commission, draw on the margin available under the own-resources ceiling for payment appropriations to the extent that it is deemed sustainable by the Commission.
In addition, Member States are required to provide guarantees. These guarantees are irrevocable, unconditional and on demand, while additional safeguards are in place to enhance the robustness of the system.
Where the Commission considers that the Member State concerned has not complied with the regulation’s conditions, it may grant financial assistance. The Commission may also extend the period of availability of the instrument where the exceptional occurrences that justify the application of this regulation continue to exist.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 February 2002.
BACKGROUND
  The COVID outbreak has had a massive and disruptive impact on the economic systems of Member States, caused societal disruptions and increased public expenditure in a growing number of Member countries.
For more information, see:
‘Short-term work schemes’ (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Е
Council Decision 2002/332/2002 of 21 December 2002 establishing a specific instrument for the temporary support of short-timers in an economic emergency (the ‘SURE Instrument’).

Summary 37:
Recovery and resolution of financial institutions in the event of a failure of a clearing house (CCP) (Regulation (EU) No 648/2012 on the recovery and resolution framework for financial institutions (Recovery & Resolution of Financial Institutions) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the Recovery & Resolution Framework for the Recovery and Resolution of Credit Clearing Houses
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework for the recovery & resolution of credit clearing houses (CCPs).
It aims to ensure that if a financial institution or a financial market infrastructure that is active in this market faces financial distress or is at the point of failure, such an event does not de-stabilise the entire financial market and damage growth across the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The aim of the regulation is to:
ensure that CCPs set out measures to recover from financial distress, to maintain the critical functions of a CCP which is failing or likely to fail while winding up the remaining activities through normal insolvency proceedings;
avoid a significant adverse effect on the financial system and its ability to serve the real economy while minimising the cost to taxpayers of the CCP’s failure.
Recovery plans
CCPs must set out detailed recovery plans for the following:
a recovery plan for the CCP;
a resolution plan for a CCP that is failing;
the resolution of a failing CCP.
The recovery plan must cover the following aspects:
the recovery of CCPs’ assets and liabilities;
recovery specific stresses to the CCP, that would endanger its viability;
an assessment of the risk of contagion in a crisis, both domestic and cross-border.
the allocation of losses between CCPs, clearing members and, where applicable, their clients.
Resolution plans
The resolution plan must include a recovery plan and a resolution plan.
EU countries’ competent authorities and resolution authorities must be involved in the process of the resolution of failing CCPs. The authorities must provide appropriate justification for their participation, based on their analysis of the negative impact that the resolution could have on their EU countries, to the resolution authority of the CCP.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that the competent authorities have adequate tools at their disposal to handle situations involving CCP failures. These tools should include:
measures to be taken by the CCP to restore its capital and liquidity;
measures taken by CCPs to maintain its critical functions;
procedures to be applied by the resolution authorities to deal with CCPs that fail.
Regulatory oversight and supervision
The regulation sets out the responsibilities of the European Commission, the European Banking Authority and the competent national authorities of EU countries in the case of a failed CCP. It also sets out rules on:
regulatory oversight of the recovery plans and resolution plans;
supervisory treatment of the related exposures towards the CCP. The Commission must also take into account the operational, personal and financial relations of the group entities, insofar it could affect the recovery or resolution actions of the company or could have an impact on other entities in the group.
Implementation of the framework
The framework must be implemented in line with the principles and procedures laid down in Regulation (EC) No 596/2008 on the prudential supervision of credit institutions (see summary). It must also be implemented by the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Insurance and Financial Services Authority (EIFSA).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2012/48/EU has applied since 1 January 2012.
It repeals Regulation No 647/2008 and its subsequent amendments.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the functioning of the single market in financial services, including:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2008 establishing a single market for financial services;
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) 2015/852 establishing a common framework for prudential oversight of credit and credit institutions;
Commission Delegated Regulations (EC and Euratom).
For more information, see:
Credit Clearing House (European Commission).

Summary 38:
European Year for the European Railways (2020-2021) (European Green Deal)
European Year For the European Railway (2021-2022)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/852 — European Year for Railways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European Year to promote rail as a sustainable, innovative, interconnected and intermodal, safe and affordable mode of transport.
The European Green Deal is a new growth strategy that aims to transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use.
To achieve climate neutrality, a 90% reduction in transport emissions is needed by 2050. To achieve this, rail transport must be a game-changer in the fight against climate change. This year, the European Year aims to promote the role of rail in achieving the EU’s climate neutrality objective.
KEY POINTS
The objectives of the year are to:
promote rail as an environmentally sound, sustainable, interconnected, intermodally, safe, affordable and cost-effective mode of transportation;
ensure that rail is a pillar of an efficient logistic network, capable of guaranteeing essential services, even during unexpected emergencies;
improve the safety and security of rail passengers and freight;
strengthen the European rail system, particularly in light of the COVID-19 crisis;
reduce costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the EU;
increase the efficiency of the rail system;
raise awareness of the importance of rail transport;
encourage businesses to switch their transport to rail.
Coordination of activities
The year is to be closely coordinated with on-going activities promoting rail transport. It will be the first year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the EU, namely on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs, administrative burden and technical barriers for rail undertakings, as well as on providing Railways with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
International cooperation
The Commission will, where necessary, cooperate with competent international organisations, while ensuring the visibility of the EU's participation in the year.
In addition, the Commission will invite the participation of the Member States and relevant non-governmental organisations, universities and technology centres to assist it in implementing the year at EU level.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The decision entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND

the creation of a European Rail Traffic Management System (ERTMS), terminals offering modal shift options, and modernised rolling stock.

Summary 39:
European Security and Defence College (ESDC) (European Union institute for security and defence policy (EUISS)
European Security & Defence College
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2016/2382 — establishment, mission, objectives and tasks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes the European Security and Defense College (ESSDC).
The purpose of the ESDC is to provide training and education in the field of the EU’s common defence and security policy (CSDP) in the wider context of the Common Foreign and Security Policy at European level in order to develop and promote a common understanding of CFSP and CSDP among civilian and military personnel and to identify and disseminate best practices in relation to these issues.
The regulation sets out the tasks and objectives of the ESCDC.
KEY POINTS
The ESDC:
has the legal capacity to:
fulfil its tasks and meet its objectives;
enter into contracts and administrative arrangements necessary for its functioning including to implement staff secondments and recruit contract staff;
acquire equipment, in particular teaching equipment;
hold bank accounts;
be a party to legal proceedings.
Organisation
The ESCDC is organised as a network bringing together all the relevant actors in the area of CSDP, including:
civil and military institutes, colleges, academies, universities, institutions, centres of excellence and other actors dealing with security, defence policy issues within the EU as identified by EU countries;
the European Union Institute for Security Studies Centre of Excellence, as well-known for its expertise in the EUISS;
EUISS publications, including through EUISS publications.
Training and education activities
ESDC conducts its own training activities within the limits of its own capabilities. These include:
exchange of experience and best practice;
exchanges of information and best practices on CSDP issues;
research and dissemination of best practice in CSDP-related areas;
cooperation with the European Defence and Security Agency (EDSA) and the European Union Agency for Security Cooperation and Cooperation in Europe (EUSCE);
training and education of experts in the fields of cybersecurity and defence;
organisation of conferences, symposia and workshops;
coordination with other activities of the Union.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 40:
Water reuse: minimum requirements for urban waste water treatment plants
Water reuse : minimum requirements to be met
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1881/2011 laying down minimum standards for water reuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes minimum rules for urban wastewater treatment plants to be reused for agricultural irrigation.
It sets out the rules for the drawing up of a water reuse risk management plan and for the granting of a permit for the production and supply of reclaimed water.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to treated urban wastewater water from urban waste-water treatment plants. It does not apply to treated municipal wastewater.
The water is reused only when it enters a collecting system and is subject to treatment in an urban wastewater-treatment plant.
Reused water is treated in accordance with Directive 91/271/EEC on the reuse of urban wastewater for agricultural purposes.
EU countries must ensure that the water is not used for human consumption and that it meets the needs of the end-users regarding crop categories.
In order to effectively protect the environment and human and animal health, reclamation facility operators are responsible for the quality of the reclaimed water at the point of compliance with the regulation. They must draw up a risk-management plan for the water reuse. This must include the following:
the risk assessment of the water’s potential health and environmental risks;
the protection of water bodies used for the abstraction of water intended for human or animal consumption and relevant safeguard zones;
a description of the risks to the environment.
the information to be provided to the public and the water treatment plant operators;
information to be given to the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee on water and energy policy.
Permits are issued by the competent authority for the grant of the permit to the operator of a wastewater treatment plant. Permits are valid for a period of 5 years. The maximum duration of the 5-year permit is 6 years.
Where the water has been treated in a treatment plant, the EU country concerned must apply for a permit from the Commission. The Commission must inform the Commission of the reasons for granting the permit.
When the Commission issues a permit, the applicant must provide the Commission with the following information:
a list of the types of water to be treated and the type of water used;
details of the sources of the treated water;
an inventory of the quantities of treated and untreated urban wastewater that have been reused.
If the Commission considers that there is a risk of contamination, it must issue a permit to a person or company that has been authorised to use the treated wastewater for the purpose of using it for the purposes of agricultural irrigation, the Commission must issue the same permit to that person or entity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Urban Waste Water Reuse Regulation’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 41:
EU Blue Card for highly qualified third-country nationals
European Union (EU) Blue Card — highly qualified non-EU nationals
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2017/24/EU on the EU Blue Card
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
It aims to improve the mobility and mobility of highly qualified, highly qualified EU citizens who are beneficiaries of the right to free movement*.
It sets out the conditions for the granting of an EU blue card.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to highly qualified nationals who apply to reside in an EU country as researchers in order to carry out research projects, as they fall within the scope of Directive 2016/801 of the European Parliament and of the Council, which introduced a specific procedure for admitting third country nationals for the purpose of research.
It does not apply to:
non-EU citizens who have been granted international protection (e.g. the protection granted by the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia or the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia);
third country nationals who have not yet been granted protection (i.e. those who are not citizens of the EU countries that granted them international protection);
those who have applied to live in another EU country for the purposes of research;
third-country national nationals who are covered by national residence permits.
Beneficiaries of free movement
The blue card is valid for a period of 5 years. It can be renewed for a further 5 years if the holder fulfils certain conditions, including:
a minimum salary threshold of €10,000 per annum for the duration of the 5 years;
a maximum annual salary of €20,000 for the period of the mobility period;
the holder’s residence permit of the country concerned;
an annual work contract of at least 2 years.
The employer may decide to pay a lower salary threshold for specific professions where the EU country concerned considers that there is a particular shortage of available workers and where such professions belong to major group 1 or 2 of the ISCO classification. The employer may also decide to lower the salary threshold if the employer considers that the work to be carried out is inherently demanding such competence.
Rights and conditions
The EU Blue card holder is subject to the same rights and conditions as any other non-European Union citizen.
Member States must ensure a level playing field between EU Blue Cards and national mobility permits, in terms of procedural and equal treatment rights, procedures and access to information.
In addition, Member States must:
ensure that the holder does not suffer from discrimination, including in the labour market;
allow the holder to enjoy additional rights and benefits under national law which are compatible with this directive;
notify the European Commission of any infringements of this directive.
Exemptions
This directive does not cover:
third countries that have granted the right of unrestricted movement of persons covered by the Schengen acquis (the free movement of people agreement between the European Union and its Member States) and third countries on the one hand, or between the EU and non-member countries (non-Member States covered by this directive);
the United Kingdom (1) and the United States of America (1);
any other Member State that granted the rights of unrestricted mobility to persons covered under this Directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2018. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission communication of 13 May 2015 entitled ‘European Agenda on Migration’ calls for an attractive Union-wide scheme for highly-skilled migrants and specifies that a review of Council Directive 2009/50/EC (4) is needed to enable the Union to more effectively attract talent and thereby to address both the demographic challenges faced by the Union and labour and skills shortages in key sectors of the Union economy.
For more information, see:
‘European Blue Card’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 43:
Parental leave and flexible working arrangements: ensuring equal treatment for both parents and carers (Directive 2018/18/EU)
Parents and carer’s leave: guaranteeing equal treatment between them and their employers
Children under the age of 18 are entitled to a minimum of 4.5 months of parental leave which cannot be transferred from one parent to the other, while mothers have the right to take at least 4 months of leave.
ACT
Directive 2017/24/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 17 November 2017 on the right of parents to take parental leave and of carers’ leave (Parental Directive)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It establishes a minimum period of leave which can be taken by both parents.
It aims to encourage fathers to take this leave and to ensure that mothers do not lose out by not taking it.
KEY POINTS
The period within which workers should be entitled to take leave should be linked to:
the age of the child;
the duration of the contract or employment relationship between the worker and the employer; and
the number of working hours.
EU countries are free to decide whether to provide a payment or an allowance for carers' leave, in order to guarantee the effective take-up of the right by carers, in particular by men.
The maximum duration of leave should not exceed 4 months. However, EU countries may decide to extend this to a maximum of 6 months if the child is under 18.
Workers who exercise their right to parental leave must be informed of the length of the leave they wish to take. They must be able to make a request for a flexible working arrangement for the duration of their leave. The duration of this leave must not exceed 6 weeks.
When considering requests for flexibility working arrangements, employers must take into account, inter alia, the duration and operational capacity of the flexible working conditions requested and the employers’ resources and capacity to offer such arrangements.
Employers must ensure that workers who have been dismissed for not taking parental leave or who have not exercised their right of access to leave are protected from any discrimination on the grounds of gender.
In addition, the directive sets out the conditions under which workers who do not exercise their parental leave right may be subject to dismissal on grounds of sex.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive entered into force on 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Taking parental leave’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Parental leave: the period of time during which a parent is not obliged to work to care for a child under 18 months old. It does not apply to leave granted to a parent who is not the primary breadwinner of the family or who has not been granted the same status as a parent himself.

Summary 44:
Defrauding the EU’s financial interests: criminal offences and sanctions
Frauding of the European Union’ s financial interests
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/849 on fraud affecting the EU's financial interests.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It establishes minimum rules relating to the definition of fraud and the application of EU countries’ criminal law and proceedings in the area of fraud.
It aims to protect the EU financial interests by:
ensuring that fraud* is criminalised;
providing for maximum levels of penalties for the offences covered by the directive.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
fraud* at the expense of the general budget of the EU (the ‘EU budget’), including financial operations such as borrowing and lending activities.
The definition of ‘fraud’ includes:
the intentional misappropriation of funds or assets, contrary to the intended purpose;
the deliberate falsification of documents, documents or other documents;
defrauding or concealing the existence of assets;
falsification of financial statements.
Penalties
EU countries must apply maximum penalties of at least 4 years’ imprisonment for each of the following types of fraud:
serious fraud;
non-criminal offences;
infringements of EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies;
violations of the common VAT system.
Sanctions for natural persons
The maximum level of penalties applicable to natural persons is at least four years of imprisonment.
individuals may be subject to fines of up to €10 000 000 for each type of fraud, depending on the nature of the offence.
In addition, EU countries must also apply maximum fines of €5 000 for offences committed on behalf of a legal person.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2015.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Fraud affecting the Union's Financial Interests’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 45:
Harmonisation of interchange fees for card-based payments and internet and mobile payments based on cards (Regulation (EU) No 260/2012)
Harmonomisation of the interchange fees and for cross-border transactions based on payment cards
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 laying down the rules for the harmonisation of payment card interchange fees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the interchange* fees* for card and debit card transactions in the European Union (EU).
The regulation sets out the maximum level of the fees that payment card issuers* and payment service providers* must charge for the use of their payment card systems.
KEY POINTS
Interchange fees
The interchange fees are charged by the payment card issuer to the payment service provider (i.e. the bank that issues the card) for the purpose of processing the payment transactions. These fees are calculated on the basis of:
the merchant service charge to pay if the merchant were to compare the cost of the customer's use of a payment card with those of non-card (cash) payments (taking into account the fee for service paid to the acquiring banks);
the fee that the merchant must pay to the bank for processing the transactions.
To ensure that the interchange fee caps are set at an economically efficient level, the regulation allows national competent authorities to collect information regarding the volume and value of all payment card transactions or of the transactions pertaining to one or more payment services providers.
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation. It concludes that the regulation has been largely successful in ensuring that the European payment card market is more competitive and efficient. However, it also notes that there is still room for improvement in the way the regulation is implemented.
In addition, there is a need to remove obstacles to the efficient functioning of the card market, including in the area of card-related payments. This regulation seeks to:
ensure that the fees charged by payment card schemes are set in a way that is economically efficient;
promote competition, innovation and market entry of new operators;
improve the quality of payment services for consumers and merchants;
facilitate cross-EU payment solutions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2013/13/EU sets out rules on interchange fees. It repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 1308/2006.
Regulation(EU) 2018/856 sets out a harmonisation procedure for the application of certain interchange fees in the EU.
BACKGROUND
European Union (European Commission)
European payment card system (European Central Bank).
KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: a fee that is charged to the card issuer by the merchant to process a payment transaction.
* Debit card: a card that is issued to a merchant to facilitate the payment of a debit or credit card.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Decision (EU), Euratom, of 23 November 2019 laying down rules for harmonisation for the first time of interchange fee and cross-bank payment card transaction fees (OJ L 347, 24.11.2019, pp. 1-24)

  i.e.: Regulation (EC) No 1282/2006 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 October 2006 laying down common rules on the application by the European Central Bank of certain rules relating to the interchange of credit and debit cards and to certain rules on payment card issuance and issuance of certain payment card products and services, and repealing Council Regulations (EC, EC, EC and EFTA) No 1008/2004, (EC), No 1009/2004 and (ECE) No 1017/2004 (Oj L 347 of 24.10.2006, p. 1)

Summary 46:
Transparency of skills and qualifications acquired in formal, non-formal and informal settings (‘Europass’) (European Commission)			
Europass
This Decision establishes a European framework to support the transparency and understanding of qualifications and skills acquired in informal, practical and voluntary settings, including through practical experiences, mobility and volunteering.
ACT
Council Decision (EU) No 1288/2013 of 20 June 2013 establishing a European Framework to support transparency and the understanding of skills acquired through formal, informal and voluntary learning (Europass).
SUMMARY
The Europass framework aims to:
ensure that persons with disabilities have equal access to the labour market and to information and communication technologies and systems;
improve the quality and relevance of skills formation, to make skills more visible and comparable and to improve skills intelligence and especially information for better career choices.
The decision sets up a portfolio of documents known as the Europass, which individuals can use on a voluntary basis. These documents describe their knowledge, skills and competences acquired through practical, informal or voluntary learning, as well as through volunteering, mobility or volunteering. They can be used to make decisions on employment and learning opportunities. They also provide information on validation opportunities, guidance and mobility.
In addition to professional skills, there is an acknowledgement that transversal or soft skills, such as critical thinking, team work, problem solving and creativity, digital or language skills, are increasingly important and are essential prerequisites for personal and professional fulfilment and can be applied in different fields. These skills are recognised in different forms, for example digital open badges. The Europass documents are designed to be easily accessible and easily understood by individuals, employers, guidance practitioners, education and training providers, social partners and employers’ associations.

Summary 47:
Transport of goods by inland waterways: transmission of data
Transport: transport of goods on inland waterways
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 966/2012 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the transmission of European statistics on the transport of Goods by Inland Waterways (codified version)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 223/2009, which had to become law in the European Union (EU) countries by 1 February 2011.
It aims to ensure that the European Commission has access to reliable, up-to-date and comparable statistics on goods transported by inland waterways in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers:
the total volume of goods transported annually by inland waters as national transport;
the number of vessels used for non-commercial purposes by port administrations and public authorities;
vessels used solely for bunkering or storage;
vehicles not used for the carriage of goods such as fishery vessels, dredgers, floating workshops, houseboats and pleasure craft.
EU countries must supply the Commission (Eurostat) with a report containing such information and data as it may request in order to verify the quality of the data transmitted.
Delegated acts
The Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts to adapt the definitions contained therein or to provide for new ones to take relevant definitions amended or adopted at international level into account. These delegated acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 11(2).
Dissemination
European statistics must be disseminated with a frequency similar to that laid down in Regulation (EU, Euratom). The Commission is assisted by the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC), which is composed of representatives of the EU countries’ national statistical institutes and other national authorities. The ESC is responsible for:
assisting the Commission in the preparation of delegated acts;
providing technical assistance to Eurostat in the transmission and dissemination of data;
ensuring the confidentiality of the information transmitted and the data collection methods used;
contributing to the implementation of the delegated acts.
The ESC is assisted in this task by:
Eurostat,
the European statistical system committee and
the ESS.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 February 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Inland waterways (European Commission).

Summary 48:
Statistics at EU level on rail transport (Regulation (EC) No 223/2009)
Statistics at European Union (EU) level
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 224/2009 on the production of rail transport statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common rules for the production and transmission of railway transport statistics at Union level.
It repeals and repeals Regulation No 91/2003.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
rail transport between 2 places (a place of loading/embarkation and a place of unloading/disembarking) located in the reporting country;
national transport between two places (the place where a rail vehicle is loaded/unloaded and the place where it is unloading or unloading).
It does not apply to the following:
light rail;
trains;
trams;
light-rail systems;
buses of locomotives;
transport by rail in the course of combined transport operations.
EU countries must report to the European Commission the total data for undertakings falling below the thresholds referred to in Annexes I and II if those data are not reported under Annexes III and IV, as specified in Annex VIII. Where a railway undertaking operates in more than one EU country, the national authorities concerned must require the undertaking to provide data separately for each country in which it operates so as to enable national statistics to be compiled.
The Commission may adopt implementing acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the delegated acts adopted by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of the European Union.
Quality criteria
The data to be transmitted to the Commission must meet the quality criteria set out in Article 12(1) of the regulation. The Commission must ensure that the data transmitted by the EU countries are of a high quality and that they comply with the quality requirements of the EU’s statistical system.
National authorities must transmit the data they receive from the European Statistical System Committee (ESC) to the EU Statistical Office (ESO). The ESO is responsible for the transmission of the data and for the analysis of the results of the transmission.
Transmission of data
Each year, the Commission transmits the data it receives from EU countries to the ESO, which then transmits them to Eurostat, the European statistical authority responsible for collecting and analysing EU-wide railway transport data. The European Commission must publish a report on the statistical actions that it has taken under the implementing acts adopted under this regulation, using, where appropriate, cost-effectiveness analysis, including an assessment of the burden on respondents and of the production costs.

Summary 49:
Harmonisation of the visa regime for non-EU nationals crossing the external borders of the Schengen acquis and non-Schengen countries (Visa Regulation (EC) No 539/2001)
Harmonomically harmonising the visa requirements and the exemption from the visa requirement for nationals of non-European Union (EU) countries who are subject to a requirement to be in possession of a visa for the crossing of EU countries’ external borders.
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 — harmonisation of visa requirements for non nationals of third countries crossing the EU’s external borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the harmonisation and suspension of the requirement to carry a visa in order to cross EU countries' external borders and for persons who are exempt from that requirement.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to nationals of:
non-EU countries whose nationals do not hold a valid visa for crossing their external borders;
third countries which do not have a visa requirement; and
those whose nationals are exempt.
It does not apply to:
the United Kingdom (1), Iceland, Liechtenstein and Norway (1);
the European Economic Area (EEA) countries (2); and
the countries which are not part of the EU.
Visa requirements
Nationals of the countries covered by the regulation must be issued a visa, unless they are resident in the EU or resident in another EU country.
they must have a valid for more than 90 days in any 180-day period.
EU countries may exempt certain categories of persons from the requirement if they consider that they are not a threat to public policy, security or public order, or public international law or custom.
Non-EU country nationals who are not nationals of an EU country may be exempt from a visa if they do not pose a risk to the public policy or internal security of the country in which they reside.
Exemption from visa requirement
The European Commission may grant a visa exemption to a specific category of persons if it considers that there is a risk that they will not be able to return to the EU within 90 days of being granted the exemption. The Commission must monitor the situation in the third countries concerned and inform the other EU countries and the European Parliament and the Council of the measures which they take pursuant to this regulation.
The Commission may decide to suspend the exemption if there is an increase in the risk of a decrease in cooperation on readmission as well as a substantial increase in risks to the internal security. It must notify one or several EU countries of the reasons for suspending the exemption, taking into account the specific circumstances notified by one or more EU countries or reported by the Commission and the principle of proportionality.
In addition, the Commission may adopt delegated acts on the application of the regulation. These may be adopted by the Council or by the EU countries. The regulation codifies and repeals Council Decision 1999/437/EC on the common policy on visas.
Visas are valid for 90 days, unless the EU country concerned decides to extend the period of validity of the exemption for a further 90 days. The duration of the extension may be extended for a maximum of 5 years. The period of extension may not exceed 5 years if the Member State concerned decides that it is not necessary to extend it for a period of more than 5 years or if there are grounds for concern that it would increase the risks of a serious breach of public order or public security. The Member State may suspend the extension for a limited period of time if, for example, there are serious breaches of public policy and public order. The suspension may also be suspended for a number of reasons, such as:
an increase in security risks;
a deterioration in public order and public security; or
a serious deterioration in human rights and fundamental freedoms.
A temporary suspension may be imposed on a person who is not a Member State resident or resident of another Member State who is a national of a third country. The temporary suspension must be notified to the Commission, the other Member States and the other countries concerned.
If the Member States consider that the suspension is necessary, they must inform the Commission of the reason for the suspension.
Special visa rules
In specific cases where special visa rules are warranted, it should be possible for Member States to apply special rules or impose it on them in accordance with public international rules or custom, in line with international agreements. These rules should be based on the principles of human rights, fundamental freedoms and the implications of regional coherence and reciprocity.
Authorisation of travel documents
The Member States must ensure that travel documents are issued by the competent authority of each Member State and that they comply with the rules laid down in the annexes to the implementing act. The competent authority must issue travel documents at the same time as Member States' experts, and their experts systematically have



Summary 50:
Eurojust — rules for the protection and free movement of operational personal data
Eurojust – rules for protection and the free movement thereof
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out Eurojust’s rules of procedure and rules of protection for the processing, storage and movement of the personal data of EU citizens.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for:
the protection of operational data processed by Eurojust;
the free movement and storage of operational operational data;
data protection in relation to criminal proceedings;
privacy of personal data.
EU citizens’ personal data are subject to the same level of protection as natural persons in the EU.
The European Data Protection Police (EDPS) is responsible for monitoring and ensuring the complete application of the data protection provisions of this regulation with regard to processing of operational Personal Data.
In addition, the EDPS has the right to consult Eurojust regarding submitted requests, to refer matters to Eurojust for the purpose of addressing concerns that have emerged regarding its processing of data, to make proposals for improving the protection of data subjects, and to order Eurojust to carry out specific operations. The EDPS is appointed by the European Commission for a term of office of 4 years. It is assisted by the College of Eurojust, which is composed of representatives of the EU countries. The College is also responsible for non-operational supervision of the organisation and provides it with strategic guidance.
An on-call coordination mechanism within Eurojust is set up to enable it to be available around the clock to intervene in urgent cases.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation repeals Decision 2002/187/JHA of the European Parliament and of the Council.
For more information, see:
European Anti-Fraud Office (Eurojust).
KEY TERMS
* Operational personal data: personal data that Eurojust has processed for the purposes of preventing, detecting and combating crime.
* Criminal proceedings: proceedings where a criminal offence is committed in a non-EU country.

  Е  ъ   бараланска гранка

Summary 51:
Fighting money laundering by means of criminal law (Directive (EU) 2017/1371)
Fighting crime through criminal law
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2016/849/EU on combating money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering and terrorist financing in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the minimum penalties for money laundering offences.
It does not apply to money laundering as regards property derived from criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that all offences that are punishable by a term of imprisonment as set out in this directive are considered predicate offences. These include:
kidnapping, illegal restraint and hostage-taking;
robbery or theft;
smuggling;
tax crimes relating to direct and indirect taxes, as laid down in national law;
extortion;
forgery;
piracy;
insider trading and market manipulation;
cybercrime.
Penalties
The directive sets out minimum sentences for the following offences:
money laundering;
the aiding and abetting of a criminal organisation to commit a criminal offence;
laundering of proceeds of crime.
The following types of money laundering activities are also punishable:
the laundering of the proceeds of criminal activity (‘self-laundering’);
the facilitating of the laundering of proceeds from criminal activity.
In addition, EU countries must take the necessary measures to prevent the proceeds from being used for the financing of terrorism and to prevent them being used to finance terrorist activities.
the freezing and confiscation of instrumentalities* and proceeds* resulting from criminal activities. These measures must be carried out in accordance with the rules of the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAFTA).
The European Commission has adopted a report on the implementation of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
   
 
Directives (EU, Euratom and the EU countries) have applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a signatory to the Council of Europe Convention on Laundering, Search, Seizure and Confiscation of the Proceeds from Crime and on the Financing of Terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
