Summary 52:
Recovery plan for the conservation of Atlantic bluefin tuna (Recovery Plan for the European Union (EU) 2018-2021)
Recovery plans for the Conservation of Atlantic Bluefin Tunas (EU-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/2021 on the application by the EU of the EU’s recovery plan
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the general rules for the application of the recovery plan adopted by the Union of the International Convention for the Convention on the Conservation and Management of Atlantic Tunas, known as the ICCAT Convention.
The EU is party to the convention.
KEY POINTS
The recovery plan takes into account the specificities of the different types of fishing gear and fishing techniques.
Each EU Member State must submit to the European Commission:
an annual fishing plan* for the catching vessels and traps fishing Bluefin tuna in the eastern Atlantic and the Mediterranean;
a yearly fishing capacity management plan ensuring that the Member State's fishing capacity is commensurate with its allocated quota.
Every year, each Member State submits to the Commission a list of the fishing vessels, traps and fishing vessels that may fish for, retain on board, tranship, transport, or land Bluefin Tuna. The list must be submitted not later than 30 days before the start of the season applicable to each such vessel.
An individual quota is allocated to each fishing vessel over 24 metres. The number of vessels and of fishing vessels flying the flag of a Member State may be submitted no later than 31 January each year.
the number of traps registered in an EU country and the number of boats registered in the EU may be determined in accordance with the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU).
The Member State with a quota must notify the Commission at least 3 days before any modification to the plan or the individual quotas.
Member States may decide to use a higher number of fish than their quota if they can demonstrate that their fishing capacity does not allow the use of its full quota. The Commission must inform the Member States of the reasons for this decision.
In addition, Member States must submit a report every 3 years on the implementation of the conservation plan. The report must include:
a description of the fish caught;
the species of fish caught and the methods used to capture and kill the fish;
measures taken by Member States to reduce the impact of the fishery on the marine environment;
an estimate of the annual fishing capacity of the vessels and the fishing fleet in the area covered by the Convention;
details of the number and type of fish that may be caught in the catch and landing obligation for the year 2018 and 2019.
A report must be prepared every 5 years by the Commission and submitted to the Council and the European Parliament, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on Fisheries and Aquaculture. It must also include a summary of the results of the monitoring and evaluation carried out in the previous 3 years.
Report
Each Member State is required to submit an annual report to the EU Parliament and the Council every 4 years on its implementation of its recovery plan. This report must cover:
the total number of bluefin fish caught, caught and landed;
fishing vessels registered in Member States;
catch and landing obligations for fishing vessels;
transhipment and transhipment of fish on board a fishing vessel to another fishing vessel;
harvesting of fish in farms or traps;
transfer and caging operations of live bluefin.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The Convention for Conservation and Migratory Species of Wild Fishes (ICCAT) has been amended several times.
For more information, see:
The International Convention of the Convention for:
Fishing and Fisheries (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bluefin: a species of tuna that is the largest fish in the world.
* Catching vessel: any fishing vessel with a maximum length of 24 metres or more.
Traps: any type of fishing device used to catch fish.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Delegated Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 21 December 2018 on the adoption by the European Community of a recovery plan pursuant to Article 3.3.2018 of the Regulation on the conservation, management and management of Atlantic Tuna (OJ L 347, 21.12.2018, pp. 1-24)

Summary 53:
Shipbuilding injurious pricing: combating the sale of ships below their normal value to non-market-economy countries (IPI Code)
Shipbuilding injurious pricing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/755 — combating injurious prices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 182/2011, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2011.
It aims to protect the EU industry against ship-purchase transactions where the price of a ship is below its normal value.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
ships built in non-EU countries;
ships imported into the EU and exported to the EU;
shipbuilding contracts concluded under the 1994 Anti-Dumping Agreement (ADA);
contracts for the construction of ships between contracting parties to the ADA.
Injunctive pricing
A builder of a vessel is liable for an injurious price charge if:
the normal value of the vessel is below the normal value;
the sale of the ship to a buyer other than the buyer of the country in which the vessel originates causes injury.
The builder must pay the charge within 5 years from the date of the sale.
If the builder does not pay within the applicable time limit, the shipbuilder may be subject to an investigation by the competent authorities of the contracting party.
Where appropriate, an investigation may also be initiated upon a written complaint by the authorities of a contracting party, in accordance with the conditions of the Shipbuilding agreement and under the conditions set out in Annex III to the agreement.
Procedure
An investigation may be initiated if the builder fails to comply with the regulation’s requirements.
A court decision may be taken on the application of the regulation.
Implementation
The Commission must take all necessary steps to ascertain, in the large conglomerates and holdings of third countries, the validity of accounting charges when the cost price structure needs to be estimated. The Commission may also take countermeasures if it considers that a shipbuilder has not paid the injurious charge within the time limit.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
It entered into force on 31 December 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat the practice of shipping in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Shipbuilding Injurious Pricing Code’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Shipbuilding: any vessel built in a non-European Economic Area (EEA).
* Contracting party: any person or company that is legally or operationally in a position to exercise restraint or direction over the buyer, which is presumed at a 25 % interest.

Summary 54:
Cableway safety: cableway installations
Cabling safety: cables and safety components
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on cableway safety
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the rules on the design, construction and operation of cableways in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the conditions for the placing on the EU market of cableway systems, subsystems and safety parts.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies in its entirety to new installations, including cable-operated lifts, whether vertical or inclined, permanently serving specific levels of buildings and constructions and not operating between cableway stations. It does not apply to installations where the users or carriers are waterborne.
It does not cover:
cable-operated ferries;
cables used for the transport of goods and passengers;
substances imported into the EU from non-EU countries;
safety components imported from outside the EU.
Importers must ensure that:
they have carried out the necessary conformity assessment* to ensure that their products comply with the regulation’s requirements;
they provide the competent national authorities with the necessary documentation;
the competent authorities have been informed of the conformity assessment results;
their products are available for inspection by the competent authorities.
Manufacturers must indicate on their products whether they are new subsystems or safety components.
EU countries must ensure the CE marking and documentation of their products is available to EU countries’ competent authorities for inspection.
in addition, EU countries must notify the European Commission of the names and addresses of any economic operators who have supplied them with a subsystem or safety component or to whom they have supplied a subsystem.
The CE marking, indicating the conformity of a cableway system or a safety component, is the visible consequence of a whole process comprising conformity assessment in a broad sense.
Safety analysis
The safety analysis must take into account the surrounding conditions, on the quality of the industrial goods supplied and on the way in which they are assembled, installed on-site and monitored during operation. It must also take account of the constraints linked to the operation of the installations, albeit not in such a way as to jeopardise the principle of free movement of goods for subsystems* or the safety of the cableway installation themselves.
Exclusions from the scope of the regulation
Cableways are subject to specific EU legislation and should not be excluded from its scope. However, the regulation does not extend to:
the installation of cables, cables and cables of any kind, including cables that are installed on the ground or in the air;
installations that are not intended for the use of ferries or for the transportation of goods or passengers, except for those that are intended for use by waterborne means of transport, such as waterborne ferries.
Scope and scope of authorisations
This regulation applies to the following:
new installations;
existing installations, installations requiring a new authorisation and modifications of installations requiring new authorisations;
systems and components which are new to the Union market when they are placed on it; that is to say, they are either new or second-hand, imported from a third country.
In addition, it covers:
subsystems that are in conformity with harmonised standards that are adopted in accordance with Directive 2000/9/EC for the purpose of expressing detailed technical specifications of those requirements, especially with regard to the design and construction of installations; and
safety systems and components that comply with those standards.
Evaluation of conformity assessment procedures
The European Commission must carry out a conformity assessment of the products covered by the regulation.
If a product is found to be in breach of the requirements of this regulation, it must inform the competent authority of the EU country concerned. The competent authority must then inform the manufacturer of the reasons for the non-conformity of the product and issue a certificate of conformity. If the manufacturer considers that the product is not in conformity, it may issue a recall. The manufacturer must also inform the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee of the measures taken to remedy the situation and inform the Commission of its decision to recall the product.
National authorities must inform EU countries of any non-compliance with this regulation and inform them of the remedies available to them.
Ensuring legal certainty
The Commission must:
ensure that the cableways covered by this regulation are not liable to endanger the health or safety of persons or property when properly installed, maintained and operated according to their intended purpose;
establish a system of authorisation for the installation, installation and maintenance of cables and cableways;
apply the rules laid down in this regulation to all cableways, installations and safety systems which are intended to be installed, installed or maintained according to the intended purpose.
Where an installation or safety system does not conform to the requirements set out in the regulation,



Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities
Assessment of the accessibility of products, services and e-commerce services for people with disabilities (Directive (EU) 2017/1564)
Assessing the accessibility requirements for products, service providers and ecommerce websites for the transport sector
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2017/1664 on the accessibility directive for the provision of goods and services to persons with disability
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the accessibility* of products* and services* for persons who have long-term physical, mental, intellectual or sensory impairments.
The directive applies to:
products and services offered by public sector bodies providing transport services, including urban and suburban transport services and regional transport services;
online and mobile device-based services including mobile applications developed or made available by operators of passenger transport services within the scope of this directive or on their behalf;
electronic ticketing services, electronic tickets and interactive self-service terminals.
It does not apply to audiovisual media services.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The legislation applies to all products or services offered in the European Union (EU). It does not cover, on an individual basis, single components with specific functions, such as a mainboard or a memory chip, that are used or that might be used in such a system.
Products and services covered by the directive include:
vehicles, aircrafts, ships, ships or rolling stock;
aircrafts;
motor vehicles, ships and rolling stock*;
aeronautomotive vehicles;
baggregional transport;
special volumes like comics, children’s books, art books and art books;
books and audio-visual media;
appliances and equipment;
mobile devices;
e-commerce websites;
services for the sale and sale of goods;
transport services for the supply of goods.
Accessibility requirements apply to all modes of operations of products.
Public sector bodies must ensure that their websites and mobile applications comply with the directive.
E-commerce sites must comply with this directive, as well as with the requirements of Regulation (EC) No 552/2014 on ecommerce.
Transport services must also comply with these requirements.
In addition, the directive sets out the obligations of the public sector body providing public transport services to ensure that:
their websites are accessible;
their mobile applications are accessible, and
their e-ticketing services are accessible and interactive;
the electronic ticketing service is accessible.
EU countries must also:
ensure that e-ticketing services and electronic tickets are accessible to persons who are visually impaired;
make sure that the information displayed on these services is clearly visible and understandable to users with disabilities;
provide users with the necessary assistance to enable them to use their assistive technologies;
set out the conditions for the use of these services and ensure that they are accessible in the event of an emergency.
Exceptions
Certain elements of transport services may not be covered by this directive when provided outside the territory of the EU, even where the service has been directed towards the EU market. These include the following:
the carriage of goods by road or rail;
public transport services provided by public transport bodies;
vehicle-based transport services which are provided by private transport companies;
passenger transport services that are provided in the EU;
certain types of aircrafts and ships which are not part of those vehicles or aircrafts.
Amendments to the directive
Amending Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 amends Directive 2017/1764 to take account of the fact that the directive has been amended several times and that it has not yet been incorporated into national law. This means that, as of 1 January 2019, it has to become law in all EU countries by 1 January 2020.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The accessibility directive was adopted by the European Parliament and the Council on behalf of the European Economic and Social Committee (Euratom).
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility Directive for Persons with Disabilities’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. ‘Accessible Products and Services for Persons With Disabilities Directive’ (OJ L 127, 1.2.2017, pp. 1-40)
Successive amendments to Directive 2017 / 1664 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 56:
Supply chain due diligence obligations of EU importers of minerals and metals from conflict-affected and high-risk areas (OECD Due Diligence Guidance for Responsible Supply Chains of Minerals from Conflict-Affected and High-Risk Areas)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on supply chain due-diligence obligations of Union importers for minerals or metals containing or consisting of tin, tantalum, tungsten or gold
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s raw materials supply chain is transparent and secure.
It sets out the obligations of all importers, including the EU smelters and refiners which import and process minerals and concentrates thereof.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all minerals, metals and copper imported into the EU. It does not apply to:
mineral-rich countries such as the Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC) and its neighbours in Central Africa;
conflict-affected countries in the Horn of Africa and the Central African Republic (CAR);
war zones;
non-EU countries whose annual import volume of each of these minerals and copper is below the volume thresholds set out in the regulation.
EU importers must:
ensure that their supply chain checks are carried out by independent third-party auditors;
keep a record of the results of these checks;
ensures that they report to the European Commission and the European Parliament and to the Council of the European Union (EU).
Imporporters must ensure that:
their annual import volumes of each mineral or metal concerned do not exceed the thresholds listed in the annexes to the regulation;
they report regularly to the Commission on the implementation of the regulation, including:
the number of minerals they have imported;
the volume of copper they have exported;
their number of tonnes of tin and tantalum imported.
The Commission must also:
report to the EU Parliament and the Council every 3 years on the measures taken to ensure the transparency of the supply chain;
identify the companies and individuals responsible for the supply of minerals;
draw up a list of the companies that have complied with its requirements.
In addition, the Commission must publish an annual report on the compliance of the EU's importers with this regulation. It must also publish a report every 5 years on how the regulation is being implemented.
Importer obligations
The EU must ensure:
that:
all minerals imported in the EU fall within the scope of this regulation and are subject to its requirements;
that the minerals are imported under the Combined Nomenclature Code (CNC Code);
that they are imported in quantities that are below the thresholds set in Annex I to this regulation; and
that there is no excess of any of these metals in the Union.
To ensure the proper functioning of the Union system while guaranteeing that the vast majority of the total volumes imported are covered by its requirements, this regulation should not apply in situations where the EU import volumes are below:
volume thresholds set at a level that ensures that the majority, but no less than 95 %, of the minerals imported by the EU are covered;
total volume thresholds are set at levels that ensure that no excess metals are added to the total volume of the metals imported. In addition, EU countries must establish national authorities responsible for monitoring the compliance with the CNC Code.
Due diligence schemes
EU countries must set up due diligence schemes for the import of minerals from conflict affected and high risk areas. These schemes must be effective and transparent. They must be:
based on the best available scientific and technical information available;
designed to protect the environment, human rights and the security of human beings;
coordinated with the United Nations and the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development;
contributing to the prevention of armed conflict and the financing of armed groups and security forces in resource-rich areas;
consistent with the principles of due diligence.
If an importer fails to comply with its obligations, it must inform the Commission, the EU countries and the World Trade Organization (WTO) of the reasons for the failure to comply.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
  The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve transparency throughout its supply chain. These include:
a ban on the export of certain minerals to the DRC and the CAR;
an obligation on EU countries’ competent authorities to monitor and report on supply-chain due diligence;
a requirement on the Commission to establish a register of companies that comply with the requirements of the Regulation.
For more information, see:
Mineral and metals (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Conflict-affected areas: areas where armed groups or security forces exploit minerals



Summary 57:
Customs legislation — modernisation of the customs control system
Customs Legislation — modernising the customs controls system
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated acts on customs legislation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets out the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by EU countries of the Commission’s exercise of implementing powers.
It repeals Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 February 2011 laying down the rules for the application of certain provisions of customs legislation to trade in EU goods between parts of the EU.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must apply the customs legislation as laid down in the implementing acts of the Code of European Union (COPE).
The Commission must adopt implementing acts relating to:
the harmonised application of customs controls, including the exchange of risk information and analysis, common risk criteria and standards, control measures and priority control areas;
the determination of the tariff classification of goods;
measures temporarily prohibiting the use of comprehensive guarantees.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that the information provided by economic operators is shared, taking account of the relevant data-protection provisions, between customs authorities and with other agencies involved in that control.
The European Commission must also ensure that EU countries’ experts and the business community are consulted in a transparent manner, and well in advance, when drawing up delegated acts.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts cover:
measures to ensure uniform application of EU customs controls;
common risk management framework and an electronic system for its implementation;
control measures
priority control areas
rules on the import and export of certain goods
measures and measures temporarily prohibiting comprehensive guarantees
rules concerning the release for free release of debt
rules for the release of certain types of goods.

The decision has applied since 31 December 2011.

Summary 58:
Enforcement of intellectual property rights by customs authorities: destruction of goods suspected of infringing copyright and patent law (EU rules on small consignments of goods) (Regulation (EC) No 1383/2003)
Enforcement OF INFROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC, No 1382/2003).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to goods suspected to infringe intellectual property (IP) rights. It does not apply to:
goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries;
good goods imported from outside the EU.
The goods concerned are liable to customs supervision or customs control, and to carry out adequate controls on such goods with a view to preventing operations in breach of the EU’s intellectual property laws.
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 amends Regulation (EEC) 2017/853 to extend the scope of the regulation to cover goods that are to be destroyed. It also introduces the possibility for goods which are to have been destroyed to be moved, under customs supervision, between different places within the customs territory of the Union.
In addition, the regulation introduces a procedure allowing the destruction of certain goods without there being any obligation to initiate proceedings to establish whether an intellectual property right has been infringed.
Customs authorities are responsible for:
the destruction of infringing goods;
the control of infringements;
immunity from liability of the infringer or other persons that might be considered liable under the legislation of the Member State where the goods were found.
the seizure of the goods and the confiscation of the infringing goods.
Objectives of the customs authorities
The purpose of the procedure is to ensure that the goods are destroyed and that they are destroyed in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
This regulation establishes standard forms to be used by the customs authority to identify infringements. These forms include:
a list of the names and addresses of the persons responsible for infringing the rights of the right-holder;
a description of the type of goods involved;
details of the method of transport and the mode or means of transport;
information on the nature of the infringement;
an explanation of the risk of diversion of such goods onto the market of the European Union (EU).
The procedure for destruction of these goods is governed by the national customs authorities’ national legislation. However, the European Commission may amend the regulation in respect of amending the non-essential elements of the definition of ‘small consignment’, such as the specific quantities set out in that definition.
Amendments to the regulation
The annexes to this regulation have been amended several times. The most recent amendment was adopted by the European Parliament and the Council in December 2018. The amendments to the annexes are as follows:
Annexes to the Regulation on the destruction procedure of goods are annexed to the original text. These annexes cover:
measures to be taken in the event of infringement of the laws of an EU Member State;
measures for the seizure of goods that have been found to be infringed;
examples of measures to be adopted to prevent the diversion of infringed goods on the market;
procedures for the destruction, confiscation, seizure and seizure of certain types of goods. The annexes also cover the following:
exceptions to the prohibition of the importation of certain medicines, including medicines that are manufactured by a person duly authorised to manufacture a certain quantity of goods, in excess of the quantities agreed between that person and the holder of the rights.
Exemptions from the prohibition on the import of medicines:
medicines which are manufactured for the first time in the European Economic Area (EEA) without the authorisation of that person;
medical devices manufactured by an authorised person, but placed on the EU market without his or her consent;
mixtures of medicines which have been manufactured in the EU without his/her consent, and which have not been manufactured as genuine goods and are manufactured in such quantities that they cannot be manufactured as such;
means of transport of medicines that have not yet been manufactured and which are only a portion of a complete journey beginning and terminating beyond the EU, with or without transhipment, warehousing, breaking bulk, or changes in the mode of transport.

Summary 59:
Customs 2020 programme
Customs programme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/241 establishing a new programme, the Customs programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a multiannual EU programme for cooperation in the field of customs.
It sets up a customs programme for the period 2021-2023.
KEY POINTS
The programme covers customs cooperation, but also provides support for the wider mission of customs authorities, as provided for in Article 3 of the European Parliament and of the Council’s Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU).
It aims to:
improve the efficiency of customs cooperation;
ensure that customs authorities have the necessary resources to carry out their mission;
contribute to the implementation of the customs union;
support the development of information and communication technology (ICT) systems;
facilitate the exchange of information between customs authorities and the private sector;
provide technical support to customs authorities.
The regulation sets out the objectives of the programme, including:
improving customs cooperation and the capacity-building of customs and customs control equipment;
encouraging customs cooperation with non-EU countries;
promoting customs cooperation between EU countries and developing customs cooperation within the EU;
strengthening customs cooperation at EU level;
developing customs cooperation among EU countries.
Implementing the programme
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It is assisted by the European External Action Service (OLAF) and the European Border and Coast Guard.
EU countries are invited to take part in the programme by providing technical support. The programme is open to the participation of EU countries covered by the EU Neighbourhood policy. The European Commission also invites the participation, if certain conditions are fulfilled, of acceding countries, candidate countries, potential candidates for EU membership, potential candidate countries and countries covered under the European Neighbouring Policy.
In addition, the programme is also open to participation of the following countries:
the United Kingdom (1), the United States (2), the European Economic Area (EEA) (3), the Republic of Ireland (4), the Czech Republic (5), Greece (6), Hungary (7), Croatia (8) and Romania (9).
The EU countries are also invited to participate in the Programme for the internal market, competitiveness of enterprises, including small and medium-sized enterprises, the area of plants, animals, food and feed, and European statistics (Single Market Programme), the Recovery and Resilience Facility (5) and Technical Support Instrument (TSIF).
Implementation
The Programme is implemented by the Commission. It covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2023. The Commission is assisted in its implementation by the OLAF, the European Customs and Border Guard Agency (ECBAA) and Europol.
A monitoring and evaluation framework is in place to monitor the implementation and the completion of the Programme. The interim and final evaluations, which should be performed no later than 4 years after the start of the implementation, respectively, should contribute to the efficient decision-making process concerning cooperation in customs under the next multiannually financial frameworks.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation has applied since 20 January 2021.

Summary 60:
European Social Fund Plus (ESF+) — 20 principles of the Pillar of the European Pillar of Social Rights and Social Innovation (PSTS)
European social fund Plus (ESSF+)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 establishing the European Social Fund+
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the implementation of the EU’s Pillar of social rights and social innovation (PSS) and the ESF+.
It sets out the 20 principles that guide the actions under the European social fund.
KEY POINTS
The ESF+ aims to:
promote equal opportunities and access to the labour market;
fair working conditions;
social protection and inclusion;
contribute to the modernisation of social protection systems with a particular focus on children and disadvantaged groups and with a view to promoting the accessibility of such systems, including for persons with disabilities.
It also aims to contribute to the eradication of poverty and social exclusion.
The 20 principles are structured in three categories:
equal opportunities and equal access to labour markets;
equal access to and quality of education and training to aid reintegration into education systems, promote social inclusion, facilitate access to healthcare for vulnerable persons and contribute to eradicating poverty;
equality of working conditions and social protection to improve the quality of life of workers and workers’ families.
In addition, it aims to promote:
the facilitation of reskilling and upskilling and employability, career transitions, geographic and sectoral mobility and supporting in particular low-skilled persons, people with disabilities and poorly qualified adults;
the promotion of self-employment and the social economy.
EU countries’ responsibilities
The European Commission may reimburse EU countries using simplified cost options, including lump sums.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The ESSF+ is one of the main instruments of the Social Fund for the European Union (EU). It is the main instrument for the financing of the social and economic policies and actions of the Union.

Summary 61:
European Solidarity Corps Portal for volunteering in humanitarian aid and humanitarian aid in third countries (European Union (EU) 2021/817)
European Union Volunteering Portal for Volunteering and Humanitarian Aid in Third Countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1288/2013 — European Solidarity Corporations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a single entry point for solidarity activities throughout the EU and beyond.
It aims to promote solidarity with victims of crises and disasters in non-EU countries and to raise both the awareness and the visibility of humanitarian aid, and volunteering in general, as a lifelong activity among EU citizens.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to encourage young people to take part in solidarity activities.
Volunteers can contribute to strengthening the EU’s capacity to provide needs-based and principled humanitarian aid. They can also contribute to enhancing the effectiveness of the humanitarian sector provided that they are adequately selected, trained and prepared for deployment so as to ensure that they have the necessary skills and competences to help people in need in the most effective way and that they can count on sufficient on-site support and supervision.
Participants in volunteering (‘volunteers’) can be young people from all over the EU.
Organisations are responsible for organising the volunteering activities. In a host capacity, hosting organisations should carry out activities in relation to receiving participants, including organising activities and providing guidance and support to participants during the solidarity activity, as appropriate.
The European Union countries should ensure that hosting organisations have the capacity to integrate the volunteering within the local context and to facilitate volunteers’ interaction with local humanitarian actors, the hosting community and civil society.
EU countries must ensure that participating organisations are able to fully cooperate with each other in the organisation of the solidarity activities and with the hosting organisations in the host countries.
In addition, EU countries must cooperate with the United Nations (UN) and the European Commission in the field of humanitarian aid, in particular with regard to the implementation of the UN Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities, as well as with other relevant EU policies.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLANTS APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has adopted a number of measures to encourage volunteering, including:
the creation of the European Union Youth Strategy 2019-2027 and the Erasmus+ programme;
the European Structural and Investment Funds and the EU programmes relating to migration, security, justice and citizenship, health and culture.
For more information, see:

Summary 62:
EU Structural and Investment Funds (ESF)
EU Structual and Investment Fund (ESGF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1046 establishing the European Social Fund Plus
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EGF, a flexible and special instrument outside the budgetary ceilings of the EU’s multiannual financial framework for 2021-2027.
It aims to provide assistance to workers who lose their jobs in large-scale restructuring events and to help them to find other jobs as quickly as possible.
KEY POINTS
The EGF is one of the main instruments of the European Pillar of Social Rights (PSR). It is designed to offer assistance in an anticipatory manner and in a reactive manner in the case of major restructuring events.
The main EU instruments to assist affected workers are the European social fund, which is established by a Regulation of the Parliament and of the Council and is designed as a proactive fund, and the European Investment Bank (EIB), which is designed for a reactive fund.
In addition, the European Monitoring Centre on Change (EMCC) assists the Commission and the Member States with qualitative and quantitative analyses in order to help in assessing trends of globalisation, technological change and climate change, as well as in the assessment of the impact of restructuring events on the labour market.
Eligibility criteria
Applicants must meet certain criteria, such as:
they must be nationals of an EU Member State,
they have to be employed in a Member State that is a member of the NACEA Member State
they belong to an EU country with a population of over 500,000
they are employed in an employer-owned business
they do not work in a non-EU Member State.
They must have a minimum annual income of €10,000 or €20,000 in the Member State concerned
they meet certain conditions, i.e. they must not have been unemployed for more than 6 months
they should not have lost their job in the previous 6 months.
Beneficiaries must be able to prove that they have the necessary skills and knowledge to find a job
they can demonstrate that they will not be affected by a restructuring event
they will not lose their job because of a lack of qualifications
they may not be unemployed because they do not have the skills to find another job.
EU Member States must ensure that:
the EGF does not discriminate between men and women
it is open to applicants from all Member States
it does not apply to applicants belonging to the same Member State and to applicants of the same gender.
Member States must inform the European Commission of the number of applications they have received and the reasons for the submission of the applications.
If the Commission considers that an application has not been received, it must inform Member States of the reasons why and inform the Commission.
An application must be submitted to the Commission within 6 months of receipt of the application. Member States may refuse to accept an application if they consider that the applicant does not meet the eligibility criteria.
Applications must be accompanied by:
a description of the specific needs of the applicant;
a summary of the measures to be taken to support the applicant and a description of how the measures will be implemented;
an estimate of the amount of the total amount of expenditure to be spent;
the number of Member States that will be involved in implementing the measures;
information on the number and duration of the expenditure;
details of the beneficiaries.
A report must be prepared by the Commission every 3 years on the implementation of these measures. The Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council on the results of the evaluation of the actions taken by Member States. The report must also include a report on the effectiveness of the implementation measures taken.
Report
The Commission must present to the Council, the Parliament, the EEC and the Economic and Social Committee a report by the end of each year on the use of the ESF+ and the EIGF. It must also present a report in the form of a communication to the public on the progress made.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European Social Fund plus (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  бараланска гракта 
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1046/2018 of 2 May 2018 laying down the rules for the establishment of a European Structural Fund (EGF) (OJ L 183, 1.5.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 63:
Ensuring transparency in EU policy-making and policy implementation — transparency register for interest representation (2014 agreement between the European Parliament and the European Commission)
Enensuring transparency and ethical interest representation at EU level — Transparency register for interested persons
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — conclusion of the 2014 agreement on cooperation between the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies in the field of interest representation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement establishes a joint framework for cooperation between EU institutions and bodies, bodies and agencies to encourage the registration of interest representatives* in the EU’s transparency register.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The aim of the agreement is to:
ensure that the transparency register is open to all interested persons who wish to participate in the formulation or implementation of EU policy or legislation;
promote transparency in the decision-making processes of the signatory institutions or other EU institutions;
encourage the participation of interested persons in the policy formulation, implementation or formulation of EU legislation.
Scope
This agreement covers activities carried out by interest representatives with the objective of influencing the formulation of policy or implementing legislation or the decision making processes of EU institutions or bodies. It does not cover:
making submissions to direct and specific requests from any of the institutions, their representatives or staff for factual information, data or expertise;
activities carried out in a strictly personal capacity and not in association with others;
spontaneous meetings, meetings of a purely private or social character and meetings taking place in the context of an administrative procedure established by the TEU or TFEU or legal acts of the EU.
Exemptions from the agreement
Applicants to the register must:
be able to demonstrate that they carry out covered activities and observe the code of conduct set out in Annex I (‘code of conduct’);
cooperate sincerely and constructively in accordance with the procedures laid down in Annex III.
Conditions and complementary transparency measures
The EU institutions agree to implement the coordinated approach with regard to the activities covered by this agreement and to set out those covered activities that they decide to make conditional upon registration in the register. They must also publish on their website the conditions and complementary measures that they put in place to encourage registration, such as dedicated mailing lists, the recommendation that certain decision-makers meet only registered interest representatives, or the publication of information on meetings between certain decision makers and interest representatives.
In the exercise of their respective powers of internal organisation, the signatories institutions delegate to the secretariat and the management board of the register the power to act on their behalf for the adoption of individual decisions concerning applicants and registrants.
They must ensure that these decisions are consistent with the agreement and that they reinforce the objectives of this agreement, namely, to set a high standard of transparent and ethical interests representation at Union level.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The 2014 agreement entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions have adopted a number of measures to encourage interest representation, including:
the establishment of the Transparency Register for organisations and self-employed individuals engaged in EU public policymaking and implementation;
the creation of a dedicated mailing list to facilitate the exchange of information between the institutions and the individuals involved in interest representation.
For more information, see:
EU transparency register (European Parliament and Council of Europe).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision (EU, Euratom, European Council, European Commission and European Parliament of 16 April 2017 on the conclusion of an Agreement on Cooperation between the Member States, the Secretaries-General of the Signatory Institutions and the Organisations of the Union in the Field of Interest Representation (2014 Agreement) (OJ L 127, 17.4.2017, pp. 1-14)

Summary 64:
Budgetary discipline and budgetary cooperation between the European institutions
Budapest Agreement 2021-2027 — budgetary discipline and cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on budgetary discipline
Decision (EU) 2021/2027 on the signing, on behalf of the European Parliament and of the Council, and provisional application of the Budgetary discipline agreement between the Council and the European Commission on budgetary matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to implement budgetary discipline, to improve the functioning of the annual budgetary procedure and to ensure sound financial management, and to implement cooperation and establish a roadmap towards the introduction of new own resources that are sufficient to cover the repayment of the EURI (European Union Recovery Instrument).
The decision approves the conclusion of the agreement.
KEY POINTS
Scope
This agreement applies to the multiannual financial framework (MFF) 2021–2027. It covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027. The annexes to this agreement form an integral part of the MFF.
It covers:
the general budget of the EU;
the financial framework for the European Union (EU);
the European Union Strategic Plan (CFSP) for the period 2021–2020;
fisheries agreements;
economic, social and territorial cohesion;
security and peace and stability;
emergency aid reserve;
environmental protection measures.
Rules for the budgetary procedure
The institutions must ensure that:
sufficient margins are left available under the ceilings for the various headings of the financial framework, except for the sub-heading "Economic, social cohesion" and
the social protection measures;
budgetary discipline;
climate spending targets are set out in the CFSPP budget chapter.
The Institutions must ensure as far as possible during budgetary procedures and at the time the general budget is adopted that sufficient margins remain under the ceiling for the different headings.
Cooperation between the Institutions
Where there is insufficient progress towards the climate spending target in one or more of the relevant programmes, the institutions will consult each other on appropriate measures to ensure that EU spending on climate objectives over the entire MFF 2021-2020 corresponds to at least the target.
Each quarter, the Commission must present to the Parliament and to the Council detailed information about the implementation of fisheries agreements in force and a financial forecast for the remainder of the year. The Commission may propose to mobilise the amounts corresponding to all or part of these amounts, in relation to a draft budget or a draft amending budget.
Where the conditions for mobilising the Solidarity and Emergency Aid Reserve are met, it must submit a proposal for a transfer from that Reserve to the corresponding budget lines in accordance with the Financial Regulation.
In the case of a new MFF or a revision thereof, the EU institutions must take any measure necessary to facilitate the adoption of a revised MFF, including:
an amendment of the budget;
a revision of the annex to the agreement;
procedures to be followed in the event of the withdrawal of funds from the reserve.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Agreement entered into force on 1 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The MFF is the EU’s third financial framework. It is the first financial framework to be adopted by the EU as a whole.
For more information, see:
Multiannual Financial Framework (European Commission).

Summary 65:
Economic and social support for the COVID-19 crisis: financial instruments and budgetary guarantees (until 31 December 2023)
Economic and Social Support for the crisis caused by COVID‑19
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 — financial instruments for the economic and social recovery of the Member States affected by the crisis of COVID‐19
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up a financial instrument to help Member States recover from the impact of the crisis in the aftermath of the devastating COVID–19 crisis.
KEY POINTS
The instrument is financed by the EU’s own resources (OJ L 168 of 26.5.2014).
It provides for non-repayable support, support through financial instruments or provisioning for budgetary guarantees and related expenditure by the European Union (EU) and the European Commission.
The amount of the instrument is up to an amount of EUR 750 million in 2018 prices on the basis of the empowerment provided for in Article 5 of the Own Resources Decision. This amount is divided into two parts:
€ 384 400 million for loans to Member States and € 5 600 million for the EU programmes referred to in Article 21(5) of Regulation (Euratom) No 1046 of the European Parliament and of the Council (the ‘Financial Regulation’);
€ 5.600 million for expenditure for support for specific EU programmes.
In view of the nature of the amounts available under the Instrument should not be automatically carried over beyond the respective end dates, except for commitment appropriations for technical and administrative assistance for implementation of the measures set out in the Instrument.
EU programmes
The EU programmes covered by the Instrument are:
the EU programmes for sustainable and resilient recovery of economic activity (e.g. tourism, agriculture and rural development);
the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) for rural areas;
the Economic and Social Fund (ESFAD) for the implementation of EU programmes in the field of rural development.
Funding of the Instrument
The Instrument is financed in accordance with the rules laid down in the Regulation on the use of the own resources of the EU. It is divided in two parts. The first part is used for loans for Member States, while the other part is external assigned revenue for the purpose of implementing the Regulation.
Financial instruments
The financial instruments provided for under the Regulation are the following:
a budgetary guarantee agreement between the Commission and the EU Member States for the financial support provided for by the instrument;
a financial instrument for the financing of the specific programmes covered under the instrument.
Budget appropriations for the different types of support, including:
loans for loans;
recipients’ contributions to the expenditure of the instruments;
guarantees for budgetary support;
commitment appropriations for expenditure;
budget appropriations to implement the measures.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  The COVID crisis has caused significant disturbances to economic activity which are reflected in a steep decline in gross domestic product and a significant impact on employment, social conditions, poverty and inequalities.
To prevent further deterioration of the economy, employment and social cohesion, an exceptional and coordinated programme of economic and economic support should be put in place, in particular for those Member States that have been particularly hard hit.
For more information, see:
‘Cohesion Fund’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 66:
Sanctions for Member States whose currency is the euro
Sanctions on Member States' excessive government deficits
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/97 — sanctions for Member State's excessive government deficit
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system of sanctions for the prevention and corrective parts of the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP).
It aims to ensure that Member States with a deficit over the reference value of the euro area’s currency are subject to sanctions.
It sets out the amount of interest-bearing deposits, of the non-interest-bearing deposit and of the fines provided for in this regulation.
KEY POINTS
Sanctioning
The regulation sets out:
the amount of the interest and the noninterest-bearing deposits and of fines for the Member States’ excessive deficit;
the minimum requirements for national budgetary frameworks, including macroeconomic imbalances, and enhanced financial market regulation and supervision;
a multilateral surveillance system organised by the European Systemic Risk Board (ESRB);
the procedure for preventing and correcting the excessive deficit as regulated by Article 126 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
The European Commission is responsible for assessing the effectiveness and adequacy of the sanctions and for conducting the investigations.
The Commission must submit a report to the Council every 3 years on the effectiveness of these sanctions. The Council adopts a recommendation to the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) on the implementation of the SGPP.
If a Member State fails to comply with the Council recommendation, the Commission must recommend that the Council, by a further decision, require the Member State to lodge with the Commission a deposit amounting to 0.2 % of its gross domestic product (GDP) of the preceding year. The Member State must lodge the deposit within 20 days of adoption of the Council's decision. If the Council considers that a Member States has not taken action in response to its recommendation, it may, by 20 days, require it to lodge a further deposit of 0. The amount of this deposit must be set in such a way as to ensure a fair graduation of sanctions in the preventive and corrective aspects of the GSP and to provide sufficient incentives for the EU countries whose currencies are the euro to comply.
In addition, the Council may adopt measures in accordance with the principles of subsidiarity as set out in Article 5 of the TFEU. These measures are designed to:
prevent and correct excessive deficits;
ensure that the medium-term budgetary objective is met;
provide incentives to Member States to adjust to and maintain the budgetary objective;
allow the Commission to carry out its investigations and to impose fines for infringements of the rules and regulations of the EU.
Penalties
The Regulation sets out detailed criteria for establishing the amount and duration of the fine and the procedures for conducting investigations. It also sets out how the fine is to be calculated. The Commission must inform the Council and the EEC of the results of its investigations. The decision requiring a lodgement of a deposit must not be adopted by the Council unless it is qualified by a qualified majority of the members of the Parliament or the Council.
Member States that fail to comply may be subject to fines of up to €500,000. The maximum amount of a fine may be reduced to €250,000 for each Member State that does not comply with its budgetary obligations.
A Member State whose deficit is above the Reference Value of the Euro Area is subject to a fine of €50,000 if it is found to have not complied with its obligations. The fine must be calculated according to the following criteria:
a Member State has not met its obligations under the GPP;
it has not adopted a decision to reduce its deficit to the Medium-Term Budget Objective (MBO);
it is not in a position to meet its obligations in respect of the adjustment and adherence to its medium-Term Fiscal Objective (MTOR);
its deficit is not exceeding the reference values of its Member States;
its debt is not above the medium term budgetary objective.
Where Member States do not meet their obligations under this Regulation, the fine must not exceed €50 000.
Sanitation of Member States
The Member State in question must lodge a deposit with the European Commission of 0% of its GDP in the following circumstances:
an exceptional deficit of more than 0.6 % of GDP;
an excessive deficit of over 0.8 % of the GDP; or
a deficit of less than 1% of GDP.
An exceptional deficit exceeding 0.5 % of gross domestic products (GBO) is considered to be excessive if it has not been reduced to the MBO;
in exceptional circumstances, an excess of a government deficit is considered exceptional within the meaning of the second indent of point (a) of Article 126(2) of The Treaty on European



Summary 67:
Population and housing statistics — common rules for the decennial provision of comprehensive data on population and housing (Regulation (EC) No 652/2008)
Population and Housing statistics — Common rules for collecting comprehensive data
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 608/2008 on the collection, transmission and dissemination of Community statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes common rules to ensure that the European Union (EU) Member States submit to the European Commission (Eurostat) data on the population, social and economic characteristics of persons, families and households, as well as on housing at a national, regional and local level.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the population* and housing* data collected by the Member States. It covers:
the population of the European Community (now the EU) as a whole;
the number of households and the number of people living in each Member State;
national and regional surveys of the population and of the housing at the national and regional levels.
Data sources and methodology
The Member States must submit to Eurostat:
a combination of register-based censuses, sample surveys and conventional censuses;
appropriate surveys with rotating samples (rolling censuses).
The data sources and methods used must meet the essential features of population-based and housing censuses as defined in Article 2(i) of the regulation.
Eurostat must assess the quality of the data transmitted and ensure that they conform with the principles of impartiality, objectivity, scientific independence and scientific independence, transparency, reliability, relevance, cost-effectiveness, confidentiality and statistical confidentiality.
The quality of data transmitted must be assessed by the European Statistical Programme Committee, assisted by the statistical programme committee.
In the event of a revision or correction of the statistics supplied under this regulation, the data must be sent to the Commission no later than one month before the release of the revised data.
Quality assessment
Member States must transmit the technical specifications of the topics as required by this regulation as and of their breakdowns, in accordance with the regulatory procedure referred to in Article 8(2). The Commission assesses the quality and consistency of the transmitted data and the metadata.
National and Community statistical authorities must take account of the principles set out in the European Statistics Code of Practice adopted on 24 February 2005 by the Statistical programme committee, established by Council Decision 89/382/EEC and attached to the Recommendation of the Commission on the independence, integrity and accountability of national and European statistical authorities.
Housing data
The Commission must collect detailed information on housing in support of various Community activities, such as the promotion of social inclusion and the monitoring of social cohesion at regional level, or the protection of the environment and the promoting energy efficiency. The Commission must ensure that these data are comparable between Member States and that they are comparable in terms of comparability, reliability and relevance. It must also ensure that their quality is assessed and that the data are transmitted to the national statistical authorities and Eurostat.

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure for Members of the European Parliament (Official Journal L 302 of 11.11.2019)
Rules of Procedures for Members Of The European Parliament
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/302 — Rules of Procedure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE RULES OF PROCEDURE?
The decision sets out the rules of procedure for the 9th parliamentary term of European Parliament, the assembly elected pursuant to the Treaties, the act of 20 September 1976 concerning the election of the members of the Parliament by direct universal suffrage and the national legislation deriving from the same act.
The rules apply to the members who are elected to the assembly for the duration of the term of office of the ninth parliamentary term.
KEY POINTS
Members of the assembly are referred to as:
‘members’ in Bulgarian, Bulgarian, Croatian, Czech, Estonian, French, German, Greek, Spanish, Hungarian, Italian, Slovenian, Portuguese, Romanian, Spanish and Slovenian.
They may also be referred to in writing as ‘Diputados al Parlamento Europeo’, ‘Poslanci Evropského parlamentu’ and ‘Medlemmer af Europa-Parlamentet’.
Members are elected for a term of 5 years. They do not hold any office incompatible with that of an EU Member of Parliament within the meaning of Article 7(1) or (2) of the Statute for Members.
Independent mandate
. They exercise their mandate freely and independently, shall not be bound by any instructions and shall not receive a binding mandate.
Member of Parliament:
has the right to participate actively in the work of the committees and delegations in accordance with the provisions of Parliament’s rules on the use of gender-neutral language in its documents;
is a laissez-passer of the EU allowing a Member to circulate freely in the Member States and in other countries which recognise it as a valid travel document;
has a right to inspect the institutions of the Member State in which they live and work;
may refuse to perform their parliamentary duties if they consider it difficult to do so;
must not disclose their personal or professional duties to the public;
can not be held liable for any damage caused by their failure to fulfil their duties.
When a Member resigns from his or her seat in Parliament, he or she must inform the committee responsible of the date on which that resignation is to take effect, which may not be more than 3 months after notification. If the committee considers that the resignation is in compliance with the act, a vacancy is declared with effect from the date indicated by the resigning Member in the official record, and the President must inform Parliament thereof.
In the event of the withdrawal of a Member's mandate pursuant to Article 13(3) of that Act, the President informs Parliament of the reason for the withdrawal. The President must also inform the Committee of the Regions and the European Economic and Social Committee, which is responsible for the implementation of the Act, of the reasons for the decision.
Duties of the Members:
perform their legislative duties;
participate actively in their committees, delegations and meetings of the Standing Committee on European Parliament and the Committee on the European External Action Service (EEA);
have the right, in writing, to inspect and inspect the bodies and bodies of the institutions and bodies responsible for carrying out their work, and to give their opinion on their performance;
make a statement in writing to the Committee and to the Parliamentary Bureau on the performance of their duties, in the absence of which they are not able to perform them;
provide a statement to the committee and the committee on the basis of which it is confident that they are able to carry out their duties and that their performance is not difficult to perform;
notify the committee of all the reasons why they do not perform their duties or that they would not be able to fulfil them.
If the Committee considers that a Member is unable to perform his/her parliamentary duties, it may, after hearing the Member's reasons for not performing them, order that the Member be informed in writing in writing of any reason why he/she would not perform them or that it would be difficult for him/her to perform these duties. The Member must also provide the Committee with a statement on the grounds that he or her performance is difficult or would be impossible to perform those duties. In addition, the Committee may order the Member to provide a statement for the Committee in writing concerning any reason which would make it difficult or impossible for him or her to perform the duties of the Committee or the committees.

European Data Processing Supervisor (EDPB) — Mission, duties and organisation
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/1725 establishing an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of personal data, are respected by the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies

ensuring that:

the protection of persons’ personal data is respected;
personal data are processed in accordance with the relevant EU legal acts.

The European Directorate-General for the Protection of the Fundamental Rights and Freedoms of Natural Persons, the European Ombudsman and the European Privacy and Civil Protection Bureau (EPDB).
It has a Director, a Director and a Director’s Deputy. The Director assists the European Director and the Heads of Units and Sectors and ensures strategic oversight of the work of the EDPBudget.

Summary 70:
System of own resources for the multiannual financial framework 2021-2027 (OJ L 183, 31.12.2027)
System of its own resources
SUMMARY OF:
Decision No 183/2027/EU on the establishment of a system of own resource
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a new system of EU own resources based on value added tax (VAT) to make it as simple and transparent as possible, to strengthen the link with EU VAT policy and the actual VAT receipts, and to ensure equal treatment of taxpayers in all EU countries.
It sets up a system for calculating the EU’s own resources.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the rules for the implementation of the new own resource system.
The new system is based on a uniform VAT base for each EU country. The VAT base must not exceed 1.40 % of all taxable supplies divided by the weighted average VAT rate calculated for the relevant calendar year.
EU countries’ share of the temporarily increased own resources ceiling is 0.6 % of their gross national income (GNI) for the period 2021–2027. This ceiling can be increased to 0.8 % if new own resources have been introduced after 2021 in accordance with Regulation (EEC, Euratom) No 1553/89 (see summary).
The own resource calls are announced by the Commission to EU countries in advance and should be strictly pro rata to the estimated budget revenue of each country, and should not exceed 50 % of the EU countries‘s GNI.
Member States may not exceed a uniform call for the following the adoption of this decision.
A uniform call must be made for each Member State for each of the following own resources:
the VAT base to be taken into account for this own resource;
the volume of waste generated by EU countries;
a uniform call to be made by the European Commission for the recycling of packaging waste generated in the Member States concerned;
an annual call for recycling of plastic and paper that is not recycled for the purposes of the European Union (EU);
a call for recycled paper and plastic that are not recycled in Romania, Bulgaria, Croatia, Greece, Hungary, Italy, Latvia, Lithuania, Malta, Poland, Romania, Slovakia and Slovenia.
Amounts not used for interest payments as foreseen will be used for early repayments before the end of the MFF 2021, with a minimum amount, and may be increased above this level provided that the new system has not yet been introduced.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
From 31 December 2027.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been working towards the introduction of other own resources, which may include a Financial Transaction Tax.
For more information, see:
‘The EU's own resources’ on the Commission's website.

Council Decision (EU) No 183 / 2027/Euratom of 31 October 2027 establishing the system of the own resources of the Union and repealing Council Decision 2014/335/EU (3)

Summary 71:
Tyre labelling
Tyre labels
Tire labels
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1369 on the labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on tyre labelling.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to tyres of all types, irrespective of their size and type. It does not apply to:
new tyres;
new vehicles fitted on new vehicles;
tires fitted on existing vehicles; and
new heavy-duty vehicles.
EU countries must ensure that tyre labels and product information sheets are accurate, reproducible and reproducible, and that tyre manufacturers are able to check that they comply with the regulation’s requirements.
The tyre label of a tyre which satisfies the minimum ice grip performance standards should show the ice grip pictogram set out in Annex I. The ice grip index values must be included on all technical promotional material and visual advertisements for specific tyre types, including where such material is made available on the internet. It must also be included in the product database established under Regulation (EU).
The information on fuel efficiency, wet grip and external rolling noise must be displayed on the tyre label. It should also appear on the product information sheet.
In addition, tyre labels must include the following information:
the fuel efficiency* and wet grip* values;
the rolling resistance* of the tyre;
a description of the tyres’ performance in actual use;
informative information on tyre characteristics;
how the tyres are abrasion-free tyres are treated during use.
the number of tyres available for sale in the EU.
instructions for tyre manufacturers, importers, distributors and tyre importers.
Importers and importers must provide the European Commission with a list of tyres covered by the regulation.
Distributors must ensure the information they provide to consumers and distributors is up-to-date and accurate.
Manufacturers must ensure their products comply with all the requirements of the regulation and that they provide the Commission with the information it requires in the form of a product database. They must also ensure that the information provided by tyre manufacturers is available to the Commission and to the end-users.
If a tyre manufacturer fails to comply with this regulation, it must inform the Commission, the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for this failure.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND

Summary 72:
Funding for customs control equipment
Funding of customs controls
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 establishing the Fund for Financial Support for Border Management and Visa Policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s Fund for Fiscal Support for Customs Control Equipment.
It aims to provide financial support to EU countries to improve the effectiveness of customs control at the external borders of the EU.
KEY POINTS
EU countries have been taking on an increasing number of responsibilities which are carried out at their external borders and which often extend into the security field. The need for adequate customs controls with equivalent results is ever more pressing, not only because of the traditional function of customs, which is to collect revenue, but also because there is a need to significantly reinforce the control of goods entering and exiting the EU's external borders in order to ensure both safety and security.
To achieve this, the Fund supports the development of modern, reliable and cost-effective equipment for customs controls.
The Fund is funded by the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Fund (ESF).
It covers the costs of equipment of which the primary aim is integrated border management, but will also allow that equipment to be used for additional purposes, such as border controls and security, as well as for the protection of the financial and economic interests of EU countries. The Fund has a maximum ceiling of €1.5 billion.
In addition, the Commission may grant up to €1 billion in grants, prizes and awards.
to EU countries for the best practice in the field of border management and border control.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has a customs union. Since the customs union is essential for the proper functioning of the internal market, and for businesses and citizens to benefit from it, continuous steps to strengthen it are needed.
For more information, see:
EU Customs Union (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs control equipment: the equipment used by customs authorities to monitor and control goods entering or leaving the EU and to ensure that they comply with the rules and regulations of the Union.
* Integrated border management: the system of checks and controls of goods crossing the external border of an EU country.

Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) 2018/922 of 21 December 2018 establishing the Instrument for Financial support for Customs control Equipment (OJ L 183, 31.12.2018, pp. 1-24)

Summary 73:
Electricity market liberalisation — rules for the internal market for electricity
Electricity markets liberalised— rules for electricity market liberalising
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/943 on the liberalisation of the electricity market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to liberalise the EU’s internal electricity market, which has been progressively implemented throughout the EU since 1999.
It recasts and repeals Directive 1999/36/EC.
KEY POINTS
The directive aims to:
ensure that electricity consumers have access to competitive electricity markets across the EU;
improve the transparency of electricity markets;
increase consumer participation and cross-border cooperation;
promote investment in new electricity generation and transmission networks.
The aim of the directive is to ensure that:
there is no distortion of market prices;
electricity prices are not artificially fixed;
there are adequate economic incentives to encourage investment in electricity generation;
the market is open and transparent;
market participants have the right to participate in the decision-making process and to be informed of the offers and savings available on the competitive market, in particular relating to dynamic electricity price contracts, and consumers are provided with assistance to respond to and benefit from market-based offers.
In addition, the directive sets out rules on:
transparency of the market and transparency of the wholesale electricity market;
rules on market participants’ rights and obligations;
public service obligations in the form of price setting for the supply of electricity;
requirements for the transmission and distribution of electricity.
the possibility for EU countries to impose public service obligations on electricity undertakings.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACTS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission Communication of 15 July 2015, entitled ‘Launching the public consultation process on a new energy market design’, highlighted that the move away from generation in large central generating installations towards decentralised production of electricity from renewable sources and towards decarbonised markets requires adapting the current rules of electricity trading and changing the existing market roles.
For more information, see:
‘Electricity’ on the European Commission's website.
