Summary 74:
Supercomputing and quantum computing: European High Performance Computing Joint Undertaking (EuroHPCJoint Undertaking 2021-2027)
Specialising in high-performance computing (HPC) and supercomputing, quantum computing and data capabilities, the EuroHPC joint undertaking aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) enjoys world-class supercomputer capabilities according to its economic potential, matching the needs of European users.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) 2021/694 of 27 May 2021 establishing a European High-Performance Computing and Quantum Computing Joint undertaking.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a joint undertaking for the purpose of implementing the strategic EU vision in High Performance Computers and Quantum Computers.
KEY POINTS
The aim of the joint undertaking is to:
contribute to the development of a European high performance computing and supercomputer ecosystem;
promote the use of supercomputers and data infrastructures, technologies and applications in the EU and worldwide;
ensure that the EU has a world-leading supercomputer and data infrastructure capable of meeting the demands of users;
support the development and deployment of high-performing systems of the highest quality;
cooperate with the private sector and the public sector in the field of research and innovation;
provide services and training to users and promote the creation of knowledge and skills;
facilitate the exchange of information and communication technologies and expertise in the area of High-Performing Computing;
encourage the sharing of best practices and best practices in the fields of research, innovation and technology development;
increase user access to high-performancy computing facilities and services;
develop and promote knowledge-based services and technologies;
raise awareness of the importance of the EU’s high-Performancy capabilities;
establish a network of national centres and centres of excellence for the provision of services and the training of key skills.
Objectives
The joint undertaking has a number of objectives, including:
promoting the use and development of supercomputer infrastructure;
creating a European ecosystem for the development, deployment and use of high performance Computing and quantum technologies and their applications;
improving the availability of high performing computing services and systems;
increasing the user-access to supercomputer facilities;
contributing to the creation and dissemination of knowledge, skills and knowledge;
raising awareness of user-oriented and application-driven capabilities; and
providing user-driven services and services to increase user access and use.
The Joint undertaking is funded by the European Commission under the multiannual financial framework for the years 2021 to 2027 (the ‘MFF 2021–2027’).
The financial contributions from non-EU countries are to be at least equal to 50% and should be able to reach up to 75% of the aggregated joint undertaking budgetary commitments.
In addition, the European Strategic Investment Facility (ESIF) and the Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) are to make financial contributions of up to €1.5 billion and €2 billion, respectively.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘EuroHighPerformanceComputing’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* High performance computing: the ability of a supercomputer to perform complex calculations at a very high level. It is the capability of a computer to process large quantities of data in a very short period of time. It can also be used to perform advanced scientific and technological tasks.
• Supercomputing: the capability to perform high-precision calculations at very low power. It has the potential to be used in medicine, transport and the environment, and to develop innovative solutions for medicine and transport.
** Quantum computing: a new generation of computing that is capable of performing supercomputer-like calculations. It consists of the ability to perform supercomputer operations at very high power and to perform quantum computing operations at extremely high power.

Summary 75:
Climate action and the EU’s transition to a climate-neutral economy by 2050 (European Green Deal)
Climate actions and the transition to an economy that is climate neutral
The European Green Deal sets out a new growth strategy that aims to transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy, where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use.
ACT
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of 11 December 2019 on climate action and a climate neutral economy.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE COMMUNICATION DO?
The Commission has, in its communication, in the framework of the ‘Green Deal’, set out a growth strategy for the European Union (EU) by 2050.
The strategy is based on the Paris Agreement on climate change and on the best available scientific knowledge. It aims to:
ensure that the EU is a global leader in the transition towards climate neutrality by 2050;
reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions to zero by 2050, while increasing the resilience of the economy to the adverse impacts of climate change.
KEY POINTS
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change, and to delivering on the implementation of the Paris agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’.
To achieve this, the EU countries must:
take action to reduce GHG emissions to no net GHG in the EU by 2050 and to a low-carbon economy;
make finance flows consistent with a pathway towards low greenhouse gas emissions and climate-resilient development, as set out in Article 2(1) of the agreement;
take the necessary measures to adapt to climate change;
encourage the development of a well-functioning internal energy market and the improvement of energy efficiency, while reducing energy poverty.
In addition, the Commission should facilitate sector-specific climate dialogues and partnerships by bringing together key stakeholders in an inclusive and representative manner, so as to encourage sectors themselves to draw up indicative voluntary roadmaps and to plan their transition towards achieving the EU's climate neutrality objective.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS COMMUNITY COMMUNITY COMMENT APPLY?
It has applied since 12 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The climate crisis is becoming more and more severe. The United Nations Climate Change Panel (IPCC) has issued a report on the impacts of global warming of 1.5 °C above pre-industrial levels and related global greenhouse gas emission pathways.
For more information, see:
‘Climate action’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 76:
Public health programme for the period 2018-2021
Public health Programme for the year 2018-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2018 establishing a European Union public health programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a programme to support the implementation of the European Union’s (EU) public health policy in the period from 1 January 2018 to 22 December 2020.
It sets out the specific objectives of the programme.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers:
health promotion and disease prevention;
preventing and responding to health emergencies;
improving mental health throughout the lifetime of an individual by addressing health risk factors, and health determinants;
enhancing access to sexual and reproductive healthcare, quality of life, workers’ health, productivity, competitiveness and inclusiveness, while reducing pressures on national healthcare systems and national budgets.
Programme objectives
In line with the ‘One Health’ and ‘Health in All Policies’ approaches, the programme is designed to:
promote health promotion;
improve mental health;
ensure access to quality of healthcare;
contribute to the attainment of the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs);
improve the quality of living and working conditions of workers;
reduce health inequalities and inequities;
encourage cooperation between EU countries;
increase the effectiveness of the internal market;
support research and innovation;
foster the development of new medicines and medical devices;
strengthen the monitoring and control of the supply chain of medicines;
provide information to the general public on the availability of medicines, medical devices and medical products;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice in the field of public health.
Specific actions
In order to achieve its objectives, the Programme supports actions in the following areas:
prevention and response to crisis prevention, preparedness and surveillance;
emergency response;
health literacy;
awareness-raising;
organ and blood donation;
reintegration of refugees and migrants into the labour market.
In addition, it supports the following actions:
information and awareness-raising activities;
exchange of best practice;
promotion and dissemination of information on the prevention, diagnosis and treatment of diseases;
education and training of health professionals;
training of healthcare workers and public health authorities;
cooperation and coordination among EU countries in the area of crisis prevention and preparedness;
the establishment of mechanisms for the efficient monitoring and needs-driven distribution or allocation of goods and services needed in times of crisis.
Implementation of the Programme
The Programme is funded by the EU Pre-accession Assistance III (PAA III) programme. It covers the period 2014-2020. This financial envelope comprises an amount of EUR 500 000 000 in 2018 and € 1.2 billion in 2019.
The budget is divided into the following allocations:
€ 500 000 for the general budget;
€ 1.1 billion for the specific budget; and
€ 552 million for the research and development budget.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
The regulation entered into force on 20 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Public Health Programme’ on the European Commission's website.

European audi-vocual-programme (EacD)2019-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Programme for the promotion of European cultural diversity
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE PROGRAMME?
It aims to promote the European cultural and creative sectors, in particular the film and television industries, by encouraging the creation, financing, producing and disseminating of works that are prominently displayed on different media, for example TV, cinema or Video On Demand, that are available and attractive to audiences in a more open and competitive market within Europe and beyond.
KEY POINTS
The programme is based on the freedom of artistic expression, the capabilities and competences of artists and cultural operators, the existence of flourishing and resilient cultures and the ability of these sectors to create, innovate and produce their works and distribute them to a larger and more diverse European audience.
It is funded by the European Union (EU) and the European Neighbourhood Policy countries and the EU’s strategic partners.
The budget is €2.5 billion.
EACDs are designed to:
increase awareness of the importance of the European film and TV industry;
promote the transnational circulation of artistic and cultural works, collections and products;
encourage dialogue and cultural exchanges;
facilitate cross-cutting activities supporting the news media sector.
In line with Articles 8 and 10 TFEU, the programme, in all its activities, should support gender mainstreaming and the mainstreaming of non-discrimination objectives and should, where applicable, define appropriate gender-balance criteria. It also aims to ensure fair remuneration of authors and performers, which are dimensions that should be taken into account across the programme.
Directive 2010/13/EU sets out the conditions for derogations from the obligation to fulfil the conditions set out in Directive 2010/15/EU.

the ‘European cultural diversity’ and ‘the European creative sector.’

Summary 78:
European Institute of Innovation and Technology (EIT) 2021-2027 — strategy and priorities
European Institute OF INNOVATION AND TECHNOLOGY (Eit) 2021 - 2027 — strategic priorities
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/819 — Strategic Framework Decision 2021/2027 on the European Institute of Innovation and Technology
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE STRATEGY?
The decision sets out the priority fields and the strategy for the EIT’s activities for the seven-year period from 2021 to 2027.
The strategic framework decision (SIA) sets out a framework for the activities of the institute and the Knowledge and Innovation Communities (KICs) for the period 2021–2027. The KICs are established and mature and their partnership agreements are to be terminated after 2024, in line with the maximum grant duration.
KEY POINTS
The SIA:
sets out the priorities and the strategies for the institute for the duration of the SIA.
These are based on the results of the research and innovation activities carried out under the Horizon Europe programme, as well as on the strategic planning of Horizon Europe, and are consistent with other relevant EU priorities and commitments, including those related to the European Green Deal, the Recovery Plan for Europe, the European strategy for data, the SME Strategy for a sustainable and digital Europe and the New Industrial Strategy for Europe.
the SIA also includes an estimate of financial needs and sources for the future activities of EIT.
is designed to ensure consistency with Horizon Europe's strategic planning.
It also takes into account the strategic plans for Horizon Europe to ensure that the institute's activities continue to be consistent with the objectives of the programme.
Innovation ecosystem and knowledge triangle activities
The EIT works closely with the knowledge and innovation communities in order to develop and implement revenue-creating strategies to maintain their innovation ecosystem and activities beyond the period covered by the grant agreements.
A number of KIC communities have been set up in the European Union (EU). These are based in different regions of the EU and are responsible for organising research, innovation and knowledge-based activities in the area of research and technology. They are also responsible for the coordination of research, development and the dissemination of results of these activities.
Research and innovation ecosystem
The institute is responsible for developing and disseminating research, technology and innovation-based research and development (R&D) programmes in the field of R&D, including:
the European Innovation Scoreboard (EIS) and the European Research and Innovation Network (ERIN);
the Research & Innovation Strategy (R & I Strategy) for Europe (RIS);
exchange of best practice and best practice;
the R&I Strategy and the R & I Strategic Plan (RSP);
research and innovation programmes for Europe;
exchanges of best practices and best practices in the areas of R & D and innovation;
research, development, and dissemination of knowledge and technology programmes;
participation of universities and research institutes in the EIS and RIS.
Knowledge and Innovation communities
The European Innovation Communities are organised by the European Commission and are composed of universities, research centres and research centres in different EU countries. They aim to:
promote the integration of the knowledge triangle in the innovation value chain;
encourage closer interactions between the actors of the know-how and innovation ecosystems;
facilitate the exchange of information and experience between the different actors involved;
support the development of innovative products, processes, services, businesses and societal initiatives;
promoting the exchange and dissemination, in particular of the results and results of research;
contribute to the implementation of the Green Deal and the recovery plan for Europe and to achieving a net-zero greenhouse gas economy by 2050 at the latest.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Decision applies from 1 January 2021. The SIA applies from 31 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European institute of innovation and technology (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Innovation ecosystem: a network of innovation centres, research and technological institutes, knowledge and technological communities, universities and other research centres, which are organised in the context of the European Innovation Strategy.
* Knowledge and innovation community: an innovation ecosystem in which the knowledge of the various actors involved in innovation is integrated into the innovation ecosystem.

Summary 79:
Aromatised wine products — rules on the production, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of certain aromatised wines (Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011)
Aromated wine products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition, description and presentation of wine products with a geographical indication*.
It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1601/91 and replaces it with this regulation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to wine products which are produced in the EU and which have an indication of a region, a specific place, or a country where a given quality, reputation or other characteristics of the product is essentially attributable to its geographical origin. It does not apply to wines produced in non-EU countries.
It covers the following categories:
aromated wines;
maromated wine-based drinks;
arromised wine-product cocktails.
The rules apply to the presentation and labelling of these products, save as otherwise provided for in this regulation, except for the sales denomination, which is set out in the annex to the regulation. The annexes to this regulation supplement the rules laid down in the regulation on the description of the wine products and the geographical indication.
General rules
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation and for ensuring that it is complied with by EU countries. It is assisted by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The Commission, which has powers to monitor and verify the compliance of EU countries with the regulations.
EU countries are allowed to adopt stricter rules than those laid down by the regulation, in so far as such rules are compatible with EU law.
Rules on geographical indications
The following rules apply:
the name of any wine product with a specific geographical indication;
the description and the presentation of aromatisation wine products;
a list of the specific characteristics of a wine product;
rules on the protection of the geographical indications and of existing geographical designations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMESSAGE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s wine sector is one of the most dynamic and dynamic in the world, with a large number of producers and a wide range of products.
For more information, see:
‘Aromatic wines’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Aromatic wine: a wine which has a specific flavour, aroma, colour, texture or flavour profile and which has been flavoured or added to the wine by means of the addition of alcohol. It may also be a drink which is based on one or more of the grapevine products defined in points 1, 2 and 11 of the annex, with the exception of:
wine-product cocktail: a drink made from wine-derived drinks, which are based on wine-related drinks, and which are composed of wine and/or wine products, which have been sweetened;
wines with a flavour, texture, colour or flavour consisting of wine, wine products or wine products that have been added to wine, which may be sweetened or which has an actual alcoholic strength by volume of not less than 14.5%.
* Maromatic wines: wines which have a specific aromatic flavour, a texture, a flavour or a flavour profile, which consists of wine grapes, wine based drinks, wine product cocktails and wine products. They may be classified into the following:
a wine with an aromatic flavour;
an aromatic drink;
wine based drinks; and
wine products with an alcoholic strength of not more than 14% vol. of wine by volume.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Decision 2014/852/EU of 20 December 2014 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the production and description, description of aromated wines and of certain aromatic wine products (OJ L 347, 31.12.2014, pp. 1-22)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, No 852/2014 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 80:
Management measures for European eel in the Mediterranean and the Black Sea (2020-2021)
Management measures concerning European eels in the Black Seas and the Mediterranean (2020 -2021).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the management measures for fish stocks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the total allowable catch (TAC) for certain fish stocks in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out:
the maximum allowable catch* for certain species of fish in the EU waters of the Mediterranean, Black Sea and Black Sea for the period between 1 August 2020 and 28 February 2021;
the target fishing mortality, in line with the ranges of FMSY defined in Article 2 of that regulation, is to be achieved as soon as possible, and on progressive and incremental basis by 2020 for the stocks listed in Article 1(1) of the regulation and shall be maintained thereafter within the ranges;
target fishing mortality for horse mackerel between ICES subarea 9 and ICES division 8c for 2020 and 2021, in accordance with Article 4 of that Regulation.
It sets out the maximum allowable fishing mortality (MSY) for European seabass between the sub-area 9 sub-divisions of the ICES and the divisions 8c and 8c.
For the period from 1 August 2021 to 28 February 2023, the maximum fishing mortality is set at 10 %.
In accordance with the Western Waters multiannual plan established by Regulation (EU 2019/472 of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary), the target mortality for the following:
fishing mortality (target mortality) for the fish stocks listed below the range of maximum sustainable yield (FMSY);
target mortality (maximum sustainable yield) for all other fish stocks within the range for which fishing opportunities are fixed;
maximum fishing mortality* for the species listed above the ranges for fishing opportunities;
total allowable fishing opportunities for the fishing stocks listed above and below the ranges defined in the regulation.
Fishing opportunities
Fisheries are fixed on the basis of available scientific advice, taking into account biological and socio-economic aspects whilst ensuring fair treatment between fishing sectors, as well as in the light of the opinions expressed during the consultation of stakeholders, in particular at the meetings of the advisory councils.
When the landing obligation in respect of a fish stock applies, fishing opportunities must be fixed taking account of the change from fixing fishing opportunities that reflect landings to fixing fishing opportunity that reflect catches.
The European Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the fishing of fish stocks;
ensuring that fish stocks are managed according to the conservation objectives set out in Council Regulation (EC) No 1100/2007;
establishing specific TACs for by-catches for those stocks.
Measures for recreational fisheries
The Commission must also ensure that:
measures for recreational fishing are maintained;
measures are in place to protect the stocks concerned;
there is no fishing closure period;
fisheries in which the species are being caught are allowed to continue to be fished, subject to certain conditions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 August 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation establishes the EU’s fisheries management measures.
KEY TERMS
* Maximum sustainable yield: the amount of fish that can be caught in a given year. It is the maximum yield of all the fish species caught in that year.
* Fishing mortality: the total number of fish caught over a given period.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. the regulation laying down the TAC of the EU Member States for the management of fishing opportunities relating to certain species in their respective waters.
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 81:
Emergency macro-financial assistance to Ukraine
Emergency macro–financial assistance for Ukraine
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/852 — emergency macro–economic assistance to the country of Ukraine
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
  
It authorises the European Union to provide a maximum amount of €1.2 billion to Ukraine for the period from 1 January 2022 to the end of the second quarter of 2022.
KEY POINTS
The EU’s assistance is based on economic policy conditions, to be set out in a memorandum of understanding (MOU) with the Ukrainian authorities.
The aim of the assistance is to:
support the economic stabilisation and substantive reform agenda of Ukraine;
improve the efficiency, transparency and accountability of the public finance management systems in Ukraine; and
ensure the sustainable financing situation for Ukraine, thereby supporting its economic and social development;
contribute to the development of a sustainable external financing situation;
provide the necessary financial resources for the implementation of the reform agenda;
promote the free movement of goods and services between the EU and Ukraine, in particular through the liberalisation of the internal market and the strengthening of the privatisation of state-owned enterprises (SMEs).
The European Commission is responsible for the provisioning of the EU macro‑financial assistance. The amount of provisioning must be consistent with the budgetary appropriations provided for in the multiannual financial framework for the EU.
A precondition for granting the EU assistance is that Ukraine respects democratic principles, human rights and respect for the rule of law. The European Commission regularly monitors the progress made by Ukraine in implementing the economic policy objectives of the MOU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLACEMENT APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCE?
The decision entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The economic situation in Ukraine has been affected by the recession in 2020, which was caused by the COVID-19 pandemic and by prolonged security threats at its border with Russia. The continuous build-up of uncertainty has resulted in a recent loss of confidence, negatively affecting the economic outlook, and, since mid-January 2022, in the loss of access to international capital markets.
For more information, see:
‘Economic and Financial Assistance to Ukraine’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 82:
Mediterranean swordfish: multiannual recovery plan for swordfish (Xiphias gladius) in the Mediterranean Sea (Mediterranean Swordfish Regulation)
MEDIAN FRAMEWORKFISH REGULATION (EU) 2017/2107 — implementation of the Multiannual Mediterranean Swordfish Recovery Plan (MedMRS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the implementation by the European Union of a multiannually-recovery plan for Mediterranean swordfish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
This regulation lays down general rules for the implementation of a Mediterranean Sea recovery plan adopted by the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT) starting from 2017 and continuing until 2031.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies in addition to the following Regulations or, where those Regulations so provide, by way of derogation therefrom:
Commission Delegated Regulation(EU) 2015/98 on the allocation of quotas for the fishing of swordfish in the European Sea (‘Mediterranean fishing quotas’);
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU)* 2017/2403 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the management of the Mediterranean fishing fleet (“Mediterranean fishery management measures”);
Delegated Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 1380/2013 on the application of the rules of Regulation (EC), Regulation (Euratom) and Regulation (EFSA) on the conservation of Atlantic tunas (see summary).
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that EU countries’ fishing efforts are commensurate with the fishing opportunities available to them.
Each EU countries must use transparent and objective criteria, including those of an environmental, social and economic nature, and endeavour to distribute national quotas fairly among the various fleet segments, giving consideration to traditional and artisanal fishing, and to provide incentives to EU fishing vessels deploying selective fishing gear or using fishing techniques with reduced environmental impact.
EU countries must ensure that the fishing effort of fishing vessels flying their flag is commensurately with the opportunities available in the EU.
Fishing effort
Each year, each EU country must submit an annual fishing plan to the European Commission. This plan must include:
the number of vessels in the fleet;
the type of fishing gear used and the number of by-catches caught;
fishing effort of vessels flying its flag;
a description of the methods used to catch swordfish.
The Commission must publish an annual report on how EU countries are implementing this regulation.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGUMENTS APPLY?
   
 
The regulations apply from 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Union is party to the International Convention for the Convention of the International Chamber of Commerce in the Law of the Sea (the ICCAT Convention) which aims to prevent the collapse of the stock of Mediterranean tunas.
For more information, see:
Mediterranean fish (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебарганска гранка

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework (MFF) for the period 2014-2020
Multiannional financial framework for the year 2014 to 2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1234/2014 on the multiannual Financial Framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the MFF’s budgetary ceilings for the years 2014-20.
The MFF covers the period from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The framework covers:
the general budget of the European Union (EU);
the Cohesion Fund;
the Structural and Investment Funds (SIFs); and
the European Globalisation Adjustment Fund (EGAF).
Cumulative budget appropriations
The general budget appropriations are:
€8.5 billion for 2014-2019;
€9 billion for 2020-2021; and
€10 billion for 2021-2022.
Special budgetary instruments
The European Stability Mechanism (SIGM), the Flexibility Instrument, the EGAF, the Contingency Margin, the specific flexibility to tackle youth unemployment and strengthen research, and the global margin for commitments for growth and employment, in particular youth employment.
In addition, the Commission may use special budgetary instruments, such as the emergency aid reserve, to cover short-term and medium-term budgetary needs.
Categories of budgetary appropriations
In order to ensure that the general budget does not exceed the ceilings set out in the regulation, budgetary appropriations are divided into the following categories:
general budgetary appropriations;
cohesion fund;
sIFs;
EGAFs;
contingency margin;
special budgetary instruments;
investment for growth, jobs and innovation;
research and innovation (R&I).
The General budgetary appropriations cover:
a maximum annual amount of €150 million (2011 prices);
a limit of €10 billion (2013 prices) for cohesion policy;
a ceiling of €7 billion (2014-2020) for investment for growth jobs and for youth employment;
an annual amount for research and innovation of €5 billion (2015 prices).
In accordance with Regulation (EC) No 2012/2002, the European Commission may also use the Emergency Aid Reserve (EAFR) to cover urgent needs arising from the end of the financial year.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
The Multiannual Fiscal Framework (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 84:
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFAF) 2021-2027 — priorities and eligibility rules
European Maritime & Fisheries Fund 2021-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 on the European Maritime and fisheries Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the priorities of the EMFAF, its budget and the specific rules for providing EU funding, complementing the general rules applicable to the EU’s Common Fisheries Policy (CFP).
KEY POINTS
The European Maritime Fund is one of the main instruments of the common fisheries policy (CPF). It aims to contribute to achieving the environmental, economic, social and employment objectives of the CFP, in particular the objectives of restoring and maintaining populations of harvested species above levels which can produce MSY, avoiding and reducing, as far as possible, unwanted catches and minimising the negative impact of fishing activities on the marine ecosystem.
It is funded by the European Union (EU).
The regulation sets out a simple architecture without predefining measures and detailed eligibility rules at EU level in an overly prescriptive manner. It does not apply to undertakings in the fishery and aquaculture sector.
The EMFF is managed under shared management by the EU countries. It is financed by EU Member States. The EMFF has a budget of €1.8 billion for the 2021-27 programming period.
Eligibility rules
Member States must ensure that the EMFF does not support overcapacity and overfishing in the fishing fleet, and that it does not contribute to illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing and to the introduction of new subsidies.
In addition, Member States are required to prohibit certain forms of fisheries subsidies which contribute to overcapacity, as well as to the prevention of unwanted catches, and to refrain from introducing new such subsidies. These include:
investments in selective fishing gear, in the improvement of port infrastructures and in the marketing of unwanted catch;
the purchase, installation and management of certain equipment for control purposes;
fishing fleet management and decommissioning of vessels;
investment in research and development of new fishing gear;
measures to reduce the risk of over-exploitation of marine life and to improve the management of the marine environment;
projects to promote the sustainable management of marine biological resources;
research and development activities in the fisheries sector by young fishers and the creation of new economic activities by young people.
Specific eligibility rules for certain other investments supported by the EMF are set out in the regulation. These cover:
the use of InvestEU (the European Union's investment programme for the sustainable development of the EU's fisheries sector),
community-led local development (CLLD) and for financial management.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The CFP is a key enabler for sustainable fisheries and the conservation of marine biodiversity, for food security through the supply of seafood products, for the growth of a sustainable blue economy and for the fulfilment of international commitments in the field of ocean governance.
For more information, see:
Fisheries policy (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еъраланска гаранда барга 
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 of 21 December 2019 laying down the priorities and specific rules of the European maritime and fisheries fund (the ‘EMFF’).

Summary 85:
Conservation and management measures for tropical tunas and bigeye tuna in the Pacific Ocean
Convention area of the Inter-American Tropical Tuna Commission (IATTC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2403 on the conservation and management of the fishery resources in the Convention area of IATTC
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the International Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish and Highly Migratory Fish Stocks (ICCST) and the measures adopted within the framework of that Convention.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels operating in the International Area of the Convention for the Conservation of Tropical Tunas (ITCST). This area is the part of the Pacific ocean bounded by the following lines: the 50°S parallel from its intersection with meridian 150°W until the intersection of meridian 130°W, and the 4°N parallel from the intersection with 150°S until the convergence of meridians.
It does not apply to:
purse seine vessels;
tuna fishing vessels; and
scientific research vessels.
EU countries must inform the European Commission by 15 June each year of which of the two closure periods from 29 July to 8 October, or from 9 November to 19 January, shall be applicable to their vessels. The Commission must assess and, where applicable, forward a request for a reduced closure period of 40 consecutive days the following year.
The Commission must forward the request to the IATCategories of fishing vessels:
Tuna:
bigeye tuna
tropical tunaspirate tunas
turtle mortality
sharks
white shark
shark mortality
white pelagic.
European Union fishing vessels may not operate within one nautical mile of a data buoy, provided that those vessels do not interact with the data buoy or deploy fishing gear, as set out in paragraphs 1 and 2.
Fishing closure periods
Each year, each EU country must notify the Commission of the fishing closure for tuna.
If a vessel has already observed a closure period in the same year during which the cause of the force majeure occurred, it must observe a reduced closed period for that year, to be notified to the Commission no later than 15 July of that year. If, however, the vessel has not observed a closed period during the previous year, it has observed one of the following years, it shall observe a reduction closure period:
40 consecutive days for tuna;
30 days for sharks
40 days for pelagic tuna
10 days for shark mortality.
In addition, EU countries must submit data on:
the number of sharks and pelagic sea mammals caught in the ITCST;
the total number of marine mammals caught and killed;
captures and transshipments of sharks, pelagic marine mammals and sea turtles;
dead or alive sharks and sea mammals;
fishing vessels that have surrendered their transhipments in the convention area;
transhipments of live sharks, sea mammals and marine reptiles;
sea mammals and seabirds that have been captured and transhipment of live sea mammals, marine reptiles and marine invertebrates;
all sea mammals that have not been caught or transhipmed in the ICTCST.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 15 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
The ICTCT is an inter-governmental organisation established by the United Nations Convention of 10 December 1982 on the Law of the Sea and the Agreement for the Implementation of That Convention relating to the Conservation, Management of Staddling and Highly Migration Stocks.
For more information, see:
Inter-American tropical tuna Commission
Tropical tuna (Inter-Tuna Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Tuna: a species of tuna caught by a purse seine fishing vessel.
* Tropical tunas: a fish that can be caught by the vessel, its owner or its operator.
Turtle mortality: the mortality of sea turtles.
Black sea turtle: a sea turtle that is killed by a vessel that has been caught and transhipped.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/2301 of 15 June 2018 on the protection of the marine life of the European Union (OJ L 347, 15.6.2018, pp. 1-2)

Summary 86:
Better law-making in the European Union: Joint declaration of the European Parliament, the Council, the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (Joint declaration on annual interinstitutional programming)
Better Law-Making in the EU: Joint Declaration on annual and multiannual programming
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852/EU — Joint declaration on the EU’s annual programming
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE JOINT DECISION?
The decision establishes a framework for closer cooperation between the three European Union institutions (European Parliament, European Council, European Parliament and European Commission).
The joint declaration sets out the principles and objectives of the agreement.
KEY POINTS
The three institutions agree to reinforce the EU's annual programming in line with Article 17(1) of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) which entrusts the Commission with the task of initiating annual programmes.
The Commission work programme will include major legislative and non-legislative proposals for the following year, including repeals, recasts, simplifications and withdrawals of existing legislation. The initiatives will be accompanied by an impact assessment. The Commission will also consult the public and stakeholder bodies before adopting a proposal.
In addition, the three institutions will set up, at the latest by the end of 2017 and in close cooperation, a joint functional register of delegated acts, providing information in a well-structured and user-friendly way, in order to enhance transparency, facilitate planning and enable traceability of all the different stages in the legislative process.
the Commission will inform the European parliament and the Council of its plans for the implementation of delegated or implementing acts.
Every year, the institutions will exchange views on initiatives for the coming year and agree on a joint declaration, to be signed by the Presidents of the three Institutions. This joint declaration will be updated in good time before adopting or revising its guidelines concerning their tools for Better Law-making (public consultations, impact assessments and ex-post evaluations).
Each institution will ensure that the Commission adopts a proposal in an open and transparent way, ensuring that the modalities and time-limits of those public consultations allow for the widest possible participation.
When the Commission submits a proposal for a delegated act, the institution concerned will be informed of the detailed reasons, and will provide, where appropriate, an analysis of possible alternatives and respond to any issues raised by the institutions. The European Parliament will take full account of the Commission's impact assessments when considering Commission legislative proposals.
As a general rule, the Commission will carry out its role as facilitator in facilitating the legislative dialogue between the institutions and will, where possible, include in that planning their requests for in-depth evaluation of specific policy areas or legal acts. It will also act as a consultative body for the public in the preparation of its proposals for delegated acts and for the adoption of its own work programmes. The institutions will also cooperate on the preparation and implementation of Commission work programmes and joint declarations.
A Joint Political Declaration of 27 October 2011 on explanatory documents will be adopted on the basis of this joint declaration.

Summary 87:
Procedures and conditions for citizens’ initiatives (Regulation (EC) No 596/2004)
Procedues and conditions of citizens‘ initiatives (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EC) no 596 / 2004 on the procedures and conditions required for a citizens' initiative
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedure and conditions to be followed by the organisers of citizens' initiatives (Citizens' Initiatives) in order to promote a European Union (EU) law.
Citizens’ Initiatives are the means by which the European Commission invites citizens to submit a proposal for a legal act of the EU for the purpose of implementing the Treaties similar to the right conferred on the European Parliament under Article 225 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union and on the Council under Article 241 TFEU.
The aim of the regulation is to ensure that CIs are organised in such a way that they are transparent and that the information they provide to the Commission is up-to-date.
KEY POINTS
Organisers of CIs must:
register the initiative with the Commission, providing the information set out in Annex II, in particular on the subject matter and objectives of the initiative;
provide, for the register and where appropriate on their website, regularly updated information on the sources of support and funding for the proposed CIs;
ensure that the online collection system used for the purposes of a CIs complies with the requirements of this regulation;
collect the necessary statements of support from citizens;
present the initiative at a public hearing at EU level.
In order to ensure coherence and transparency in relation to CIs, it is mandatory to register such initiatives on a website made available by the Commission prior to collecting the necessary information from citizens. The organisers must complete the forms as indicated in Annex III prior to initiating the collection of statements of supporting from signatories. They must also provide the Commission with, where appropriate, information on:
the sources of funding;
the number of signatories;
a description of the CIs’ objectives.
Organisations must also ensure that the data they collect are not retained for a maximum of 2 years after the end of the period of collection.
EU countries must recognise the certificates issued by the competent authorities of other EU countries.
When the Commission receives a proposal from an initiative supported by the requisite number of signatures, the organisers are entitled to present that initiative at an EU level public hearing.
Where the Commission considers that the initiative does not meet the conditions laid down in the regulation, it may adopt a decision to withdraw the proposal.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
 
CITIZENS’ INITIATIVES
Citizen’s initiatives are the tools by which EU citizens have the right to participate in the decision-making process of the Commission.
For more information, see:
European Citizens’ Initiative (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* CITIZENATIVE: a citizen initiative which aims to promote an EU law, policy or initiative.
* ARTICLE 225: the right of the Member States to legislate on behalf of the Union and to adopt a law or a policy implementing that law or policy.

Summary 88:
Statistical statistics on the placing on the market and use of pesticides
Statistical Statistics on the place on the markets and use thereof
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 223/2009 — statistical statistics on pesticides
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common framework for the systematic production of EU-wide statistics on pesticide sales and use.
It aims to provide up-to-date statistics on sales and the use of pesticide products in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers substances listed in Annex III contained in pesticides which are plant protection products, as defined in Directive 2009/128/EC.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with reports on the quality of the data transmitted as referred to in Annexes I and II of the regulation, as well as on the burden on businesses, agricultural holdings and national administrations and the usefulness of these statistics in the context of the Thematic Strategy on the Sustainable Use of Pesticides in particular with regard to the objectives set out in Article 1.
Quality report
Each EU country must supply Eurostat with a quality report indicating:
the methodology used to collect data,
relevant aspects of quality according to the methodology used,
a description of estimations, aggregations and exclusion methods used, and
the usefulness of the statistics for statistical purposes.
The data are used by national authorities and by Eurostat exclusively for the statistical purposes, in accordance with Article 20 of Regulation (EU) No 1166/2008 on farm structure surveys and the survey on agricultural production methods.
the confidential data are published on the Internet.

Summary 89:
EU rules on plant protection products — harmonised rules for active substances and placing on the market (Regulation (EC) No 1272/2008)
EU rules for plant protection substances — harmonized rules for the approval of active substances
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2016/852 on the harmonisation of rules on the authorisation of plant protection* products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise EU rules on:
the authorisation, monitoring and control of plant-protection products;
the application of the regulation;
records and information on the use of these products.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
active substances* and adjuvants* intended for the protection of plants or plants’ health and the environment;
safeners* and synergists* intended to enhance plant protection;
plants used for minor uses.
It does not apply to micro-organisms.
Authorisations
EU countries must grant authorisations to active substances, safeners or synergists. The regulation sets out a procedure for granting these authorisations. It also lays down rules for:
granting a parallel trade permit;
grants authorisations for products placed on the EU market.
The authorisation procedure is based on the precautionary principle. It is designed to ensure that active substances* do not adversely affect human or animal health or the environment.
In exceptional cases, EU countries may also apply the comparative assessment when granting authorisation.
the results and costs of tests and studies on animals should be shared between EU countries.
animal health and animal welfare rules
the rules on record-keeping and information about plant protection must be kept for at least 5 years.
EU Member States must ensure that:
there is no duplication of authorisations between Member States;
there are no restrictions on the import and export of products from one Member State to another.
Member States have the possibility of recovering the costs associated with the application of this regulation from those seeking to place, or placing, active substances.
Where identical plants or products are authorised in different Member States, a simplified procedure is provided for in this regulation, in order to facilitate the trade between them of such products. This procedure is known as the ‘parallel trade permit’.
Exemptions
Certain substances which are not predominantly used as plants protection products may be of value for plants protection, but the economic interest of applying for approval may be limited. These substances are excluded from the scope of this Regulation. The Commission may, however, grant authorisation to these substances if they are of value to plant protection.

Summary 90:
Internal market information system (IMI)
Internal market Information System (IME)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 on the establishment of the Internal Market Information System
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a communication tool for administrative cooperation between competent authorities of the European Union (EU) Member States and between the European Commission and the European Economic Area (EEA).
It sets out the rules and procedures for the exchange of information, including personal data, between EU countries and the Commission.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
administrative cooperation between the competent authorities* of the Member States;
the Commission and EU countries’ competent authorities in the field of the implementation of EU acts in the area of the internal market.
It does not apply to administrative cooperation* between the EU countries or between the Commission and non-EU countries, unless a Union act provides for a role for the Commission in such cooperation.
Exchange of personal data
Personal data may be exchanged as part of administrative cooperation within IMI, unless the Commission has access to personal data.
The processing of data is subject to all relevant safeguards, including the protection of the personal data of the data subjects and the confidentiality of confidential information.
EU countries must ensure that:
personal data is processed only for the purpose for which it was originally collected and for the purposes for which the information was exchanged;
data subjects have the right of access to their personal data;
information exchanged is processed in accordance with the security and confidentiality requirements of the EU acts;
all personal data are protected.
National coordinators
Each Member State appoints a national coordinator to act as a coordinator for IMI. The coordinator is responsible for:
coordinating IMI activities;
ensuring the proper functioning of IMI;
monitoring the use of the system by IMI coordinators;
preparing and disseminating information on administrative cooperation or mutual assistance in the EU;
organising training and awareness-raising initiatives;
providing information to the general public on the IMI system.
Implementation of the regulation
The Commission must adopt an implementing act to determine which EU acts are subject to a pilot project and on the modalities of that pilot project, in order to ensure that the pilot project reflects the needs of the users and that the rules on processing of the information exchanged are fully complied with.
In addition, the Commission must monitor the performance of Member States regarding the effective application of this regulation based on statistical data from IMI and any other relevant data. It must also report to the European Parliament and the Council every year on IMI’s functioning.
Role of the Commission
The European Commission may appoint one or more coordinators for each Member State to carry out IMI tasks. These coordinators must be independent from the national coordinators and must:
carry out the tasks of the national coordinator;
act as liaisons between the coordinators of the different Member States, and with the Commission;
notify the Commission of any changes to IMI procedures and procedures;
coordinate the work of the coordinator and the national IMI coordinator.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  IMI is the main communication tool used by EU countries to exchange information on the EU internal market, which is not open to the public. In particular, IMI helps competent authorities to identify their counterpart in another Member State, to manage the information exchange on the basis of simple and unified procedures and to overcome language barriers.
For more information, see:
‘Implementing Regulation (Euratom)’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Administrative cooperation: administrative cooperation in the context of an EU act, i.e. the cooperation of EU countries, between the authorities of those countries, and between national authorities and the EU Commission, to implement that EU act.

Summary 92:
Unitary patent protection in the EU: European patent with unitary effect (until 31 December 2020)
Unitary Patent protection in Europe: European Patent with Unitary Effect (until31 December 2023)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2012 on the creation of unitary patent protection
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to create uniform patent protection throughout the European Union (EU).
It establishes the European Patent Organisation (EPO).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to patents granted by EU countries.
The EPO decides whether a patent is to be granted as a national patent or as a European patent.
EU countries must ensure that the EPO grants a patent to a patent applicant with the same set of claims as national patents.
A European patent is granted in its entirety and in all EU countries, regardless of the applicant’s place of residence or place of business. The EPO determines the number of EU countries covered by the regulation.
In the pre-grant phase, the applicant must submit a request for patent protection to the EU country in which the patent was granted. This request must be submitted within 1 month of the date of publication of the mention of the grant in the European patent Bulletin. The applicant must provide a copy of the patent application and the relevant documentation.
Where a patent has been granted in a non-EU country, it is considered not to have taken effect on their territory as a patent, so as to avoid any duplication of patent protection.
If a patent does not take effect on the territory of an EU country, the patent may be licensed in return for appropriate consideration.
Patent proprietors must pay a single annual renewal fee for a patent. The amount of the renewal fee is determined by the EU countries’ laws on patents. The distribution of the fees is based on:
the number of patents granted in the participating EU countries;
the frequency of patent applications; and
the level of patenting activity in each participating EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Patent Convention (EPC) sets out the rules for the application of the EPC to patents. It also provides for the establishment of a Unified Patent Court to hear cases concerning the patent, to ensure consistency of case-law and hence legal certainty, and cost-effectiveness for patent proprietors.
For more information, see:
‘Unitary patents’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* European patent: a patent granted by one EU country to one patent applicant in another EU country for the purpose of conferring patent protection on the same object of property (i.e. the same product or service).
* Unitary patent: any patent granted in accordance with the rules of the EU Patent Convention.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  наскабрака гарка
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1160/2013 of 21 December 2012 on the establishment, on the basis of Article 5 of the Treaty on European Union, of the unitary protection of patents and repealing Regulation (EC) No 652/2006 (OJ L 347, 24.12.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1160 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 93:
European Observatory on Infringements of Intellectual Property Rights (EOSI)
European Observatory On Infringings of Intellectual Product Rights (OOSI)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2011 establishing a European Observatory on infringements of intellectual property rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes the European Observatory (OESI) which is responsible for collecting, analysing and disseminating relevant objective, comparable and reliable data on the value of intellectual intellectual property (IP) rights and infringements, identifying and promoting best practices and strategies to enforce IP rights, and raising public awareness of the impact of infringements.
KEY POINTS
The OESI’s tasks are to:
organise, administer and support the gathering of experts, authorities and stakeholders assembled under the name ‘the Observatory’;
facilitate the activities of national authorities, the private sector and the European Union institutions relating to the enforcement of IP rights and in particular their activities in the fight against counterfeiting and piracy.
The Observatory is composed of:
representatives from the public administrations, bodies and organisations in the EU countries;
private-sector representatives.
In addition, the Observatory organises meetings consisting of representatives of national trade mark offices and the internet intermediaries.
It also organises, in consultation with the Member States, programmes for the provision of technical assistance to third countries as well as developing and delivering specific training programmes and events for officials from third countries who are involved in the protection of IP.
Assembled by the Observatory, the OOSI collects, analyses and disseminates relevant objective and comparable data on:
the value of IPs;
the infringements that occur; and
the impact of these infringements on the economy and society.
Each year, the Office adopts an annual report on the implementation of its tasks. The report is based on the information it has collected and on the opinions of the experts and the stakeholders it has consulted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect intellectual property. These include:
a legal basis for the involvement of the Office for Harmonization in the Internal Market (Trade Marks and Designs) in enforcement-related activities;
a European anti-counterfeiting and anti-piracy plan.
For more information, see:
‘The European Observatory for Intellectual Property Enforcement’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Intellectual property rights: the legal rights that make it possible for a person, organisation or business to make a product or service available to the public, including trademarks, trade marks and designs.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU) No 550/2011 of 1 January 2011 establishing a Community Observatory on infringement of intellectual Property Rights and amending Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 652/2010 (OJ L 127, 1.2.2011, pp. 1-18)

Summary 94:
Safeguarding the EU’s economic interests in international trade agreements
Safer trade agreements: measures to safeguard the EU's economic interests
Sovereignty of the European Union
The European Union (EU) has adopted a series of measures to protect its economic interests against breaches by non-EU countries of international trade rules which affect its interests.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 of 22 December 2006 on the protection of the Union’ s economic interests under international trade agreement safeguards
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a procedure for the European Commission to adopt implementing acts determining the appropriate commercial policy measures in the event of breaches of the rules of the International Trade Agreement on Safeguards (ITA) and the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT).
It aims to ensure that the EU is able to react swiftly and in a flexible manner in the context of the procedures and deadlines set out by the agreements which it has concluded.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
any country or separate customs territory which applies a safeguard measure* to goods imported from the EU;
any EU country which is a party to the WTO Agreement on Trade in Goods and Services (TISA);
any contracting authorities, entities or categories of contracting authorities or entities which have a commercial presence within the EU, i.e. that country and in the territory of which the legal person is engaged in substantive business operations;
services provided through a commercial relations with a third country;
the imposition of a mandatory price penalty on tenders of suppliers of goods or services originating in the third country concerned.
It does not apply to the following:
the import of goods originating in and operating in the EU from a non-member country, or a contracting authority or entity that has a direct link with the EU country concerned; or
the provision of a service provided by a service provider to a third party.
Exceptions
The Commission may adopt measures in cases where:
there is a breach of the WTO agreement on trade in goods and services;
a modification of the trade-enhancing measures adopted by a WTO Member State;
an extension of the validity of the safeguard measures adopted under the TISA;
where there is an increase in the level of nullification or impairment of the benefits accruing to the EU under an international agreement.
Where action is necessary, the Commission adopts an implementing act determining the measures to be taken. These measures may include:
a suspension of the application of a safeguard and a proportionate adjustment of the measures;
suspension or withdrawal of the concessions of the Member State concerned;
restructuring concessions or other obligations in the trade relations with third countries, when the treatment accorded to goods from the Union is altered in a way that affects the Union's interests;
implementing measures to ensure the maintenance of a substantially equivalent level of concessions and other obligations between the exporting Member States and the importing Member States, which would be affected by such a measure.
Implementing acts are adopted by the Commission no later than 3 years after the first instance of its implementation or 5 years from its date of entry into force, whichever is the earlier. The Commission may review the scope, functioning and efficiency of the regulation, including possible measures relating to intellectual property rights and additional measures concerning services.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
International trade agreements and safeguards (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* International trade agreement: any trade agreement between the EU and any country or a customs territory.
* Safeguard measures: measures adopted to protect the EU against breaches of international rules.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Егародна на барканска ганка (OJ L 347, 23.12.2006, pp. 1-11)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 552 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 95:
Safeguarding the EU banana sector
Safer measures for the protection of the EU’s banana sector from serious harm from imports from Central America (Agreement on the stabilisation of trade between the EU and Central America)
Agreement of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2012 on the establishment of a European Union (EU) Banana Stabilisation Mechanism.
ACT
Council Decision 2012/852/EC of 29 June 2012 on implementing the Agreement on the implementation of the Stabilization of Trade between the European Union and its Member States, of the one part, and the countries of the Central American Economic Area (CARA) on the other part.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE AGREEMENT DO?
The agreement establishes a mechanism to prevent serious harm to the Union banana growing sector which is of great importance to the agricultural producers of many of the outermost regions in the EU.
The aim of the agreement is to:
ensure that the Commission adopts appropriate safeguard measures in the event of an increase in imports of bananas from the CARA;
establish a mechanism for the temporary suspension of the preferential customs duty established for bananas;
implement measures to prevent a negative impact on the EU market for bananas.
KEY POINTS
The Commission must:
monitor the observance by Central America of the social and environmental standards laid down in the agreement;
monitor imports from CARA countries of bananas and those sectors to which monitoring has been extended;
assess the impact of increased imports on the banana sector;
make a determination of the existence of a threat of serious injury, based on verifiable facts and not merely on an allegation, a conjecture or a remote possibility.
Importers must provide the Commission with all information it considers necessary to make a determination with regard to the conditions set out in Article 2(1), and, where appropriate, verify that information.
An investigation must precede the application of any safeguard measure, subject to the Commission being allowed to apply provisional safeguard measures, in critical circumstances as referred to in Article 106 of the Agreement.
In the event that there is a surge of imports concentrated in one or several EU countries, or in an outermost region, the Commission may also initiate a proceeding, provided that the conditions for initiation are met, in accordance with Article 5(5). The Commission must present an annual monitoring report to the EU Parliament and to the Council on updated statistics on imports from Costa Rica, El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras, Nicaragua, Guatemala and Panama.
If the Commission considers that the import of the product concerned is likely to cause serious injury to the sector, it must adopt definitive safeguard measures. These safeguard measures are based on a number of factors, including:
the rate and amount of imports;
the share of the domestic market taken by increased imports and changes in the level of sales, production, productivity, capacity utilisation, profits and losses, and employment.
Provisional safeguard measures may be adopted in the case of a surge in imports from one or more EU countries or in the outerest regions, or if there is sufficient prima facie evidence that they are likely to have a serious adverse effect on the situation of the sector. These measures must be adopted within 1 month of receipt by the Commission of a request or information pursuant to paragraph 1.
Stabilisation mechanism
The stabilisation mechanism for bananas is based on the following principles:
an advisory procedure must be used for the adoption of surveillance and provisional measures given the effects of those measures and their sequential logic in relation to the adoption.
It must be possible for the Commission to adopt implementing acts for temporary suspension or elimination of preferential customs duties for bananas, or to determine that such temporary suspension is not appropriate. It must also be possible to suspend or eliminate the tariff on imports originating in a non-EU country.
Where the Commission determines that the measures are not appropriate, it may adopt a decision to terminate or eliminate a tariff on the import or export of a product originating in an EU country. The decision must be communicated to the other EU countries and the EU countries’ competent authorities.
A non-confidential file must be kept in a secure location. The Commission shall, as soon as the necessary technical framework is in place, ensure password-protected online access to this file.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU banana industry is one of the most important agricultural producers in the world and is particularly vulnerable to serious damage caused by imports from the region.
For more information, see:
Bananas (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
  i.e. Implementing Regulation (EC) No 852/2012 on the adoption by the European Commission of implementing acts pursuant to Article 106(1) of the Agreements on the Implementation of the Stability of Trade within the Community of the Member States



Summary 96:
European Account Preservation Order (EACPO)
European Account Protection Order (ECO)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2009 on the European Account Preservation order
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has a uniform procedure for the preservation of bank accounts and debtors’ assets.
KEY POINTS
Creditors can apply for a ‘preservation order’ to prevent the transfer or withdrawal of funds held by a debtor in a bank account maintained in an EU country if there is a risk that, without such a measure, the subsequent enforcement of their claim against the debtor will be impeded or made substantially more difficult.
The procedure for a creditor wishing to secure the enforcement of a later judgment on the substance of the matter must be established prior to initiating proceedings on the matter and at any stage during such proceedings.
A creditor may apply for an EACPO if he is a consumer domiciled in one EU country and the bank account concerned by the order is maintained in another EU country.
Where a creditor applies for a preservation order, jurisdiction to issue the order lies only with the courts of that EU country, even if the creditor applies at the same time for a Preservation Order which concerns an account or accounts maintained in a non-EU country. The court dealing with the application for the order must be located in the same EU country as the court issuing the order.
In addition, the creditor must provide security so that the creditor can be compensated at a later stage for any damage caused to him by the preservation order. The amount of the security must be determined by the court in the absence of specific evidence as to the amount of potential damage.
Liability of the creditor
The creditor is liable to the creditor where the damage caused is due to fault on the creditor’s part. However, the EU countries may maintain or introduce in their national law grounds for liability other than those specified in this regulation.
Implementation of the regulation
EU countries must ensure that:
the order is transmitted to the court of the EU country of origin and served on the debtor;
the debtor is served with the order by the competent authority of the country of enforcement;
any amendments to the order are transmitted as soon as possible;
all documents and information transmitted by the creditor are translated into the language of the applicable EU country;
there is no obligation for the creditor to provide any additional information.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission launched a consultation in 2006 on the need for a uniform European procedure for preserving bank accounts.
For more information, see:
‘Preservation Order’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EC), No 653/2009 of 21 December 2009 on the application of the European Bank Preservation Order to the Court of Justice of the Member States in respect of the attachment of accounts (OJ L 127, 24.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Council Regulation (EEC, No 654/2009 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 97:
Border surveillance of the sea borders by border guards operating under the coordination of the European Border Surveillance System (Eurosur) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 — coordination of border surveillance operations at sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union Agency for the Coordination of Border Surveillance of the Sea (EASA).
It sets out the rules for border surveillance at sea.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
border surveillance operations conducted by EU countries and Eurosur;
operational cooperation between EU countries, Eurosur and non-EU countries.
It does not affect the responsibilities of search and rescue authorities, including for ensuring that coordination and cooperation is conducted in such a way that the persons rescued can be delivered to a place of safety.
Operational plan
The operational plan must include specific information on the application of the relevant jurisdiction and legislation in the geographical area where the joint operation, pilot project or rapid intervention takes place, including references to EU and international law regarding interception, rescue at sea and disembarkation.
EU countries and the Agency must comply with norms and standards at least equivalent to those set by EU law.
The Agency may cooperate with the competent authorities of third countries, in particular as regards risk analysis and training, and should facilitate operational cooperation between Member States and third countries. The Agency may also cooperate with non-European Union countries, such as the United Kingdom (United Kingdom (1) and Switzerland.
Rights of migrants and asylum seekers
The European Union countries must respect human dignity, fundamental rights and the rights of migrants, including the principle of non-refoulement and the right to an effective remedy.
In addition, the Agency is responsible for:
the protection of personal data,
the right to asylum and to protection against removal and expulsion,
non-discrimination, and
the rights of the child.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
It entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The purpose of the regulation is to strengthen the information exchange and operational cooperation on border surveillance.
For more information, see:
Border surveillance (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Operational surveillance: a border surveillance operation in which border guards are deployed to monitor the borders of an EU country to prevent illegal border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
* Interception: the interception of a person attempting to cross an irregular border.
