Summary 98:
EU funding for the area of freedom, security and justice (2014-20) (European Union specific regulation)
EU funding to the area for the development of freedom and security (2014 - 20)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012 laying down general rules necessary for the implementation of specific Regulations relating to EU funding in the field of home affairs for the period 2014-20
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out general rules for implementing the specific regulations relating to the EU’s home affairs programme for the 2014 - 20 period.
It aims to ensure that EU funding is more efficient and better targeted if it is based on strategic multiannual programming, drawn up by each EU country in dialogue with the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland and the European Economic Area (EEA).
Eligible actions
Each EU country must submit to the Commission a national programme describing how it aims to achieve the objectives of the relevant specific regulation for the EU funding period 2014 -20. It must also provide a description of the measures it intends to take to achieve these objectives.
The European Commission must review the programme every 2 years and, if necessary, issue a report to the European Parliament and the Council on the progress made in achieving the programme objectives. The Commission must also review the implementation and the quality of the programmes every 3 years.
EU countries must ensure that:
the national programmes are managed and implemented by the competent authorities;
the information and communication measures are available to the public and the general public;
there are regular audits carried out by the Commission, the European Court of Auditors and European Anti-Fraud Office (EAFO).
The Commission has powers and responsibilities to verify the effective functioning of the management and control systems and to require Member State action to correct any infringements of EU or national law.
Specific Regulations
The Regulation sets out the general rules on the eligibility of EU funding for certain specific actions. These include:
emergency assistance for actions which were incurred before the application for such assistance was made, but not before 1 January 2014, in accordance with Article 18(4) of the Schengen acquis (‘OLAF’) which allows such assistance to be announced as well as unannounced;
measures to prevent, detect and respond to illegal immigration and border control.
In order to encourage financial discipline, it is appropriate to define the arrangements for decommitment of any part of the budget that is not in line with the principle of subsidiarity.
National programmes
Each year, the Commission and the EU countries must submit national programmes describing how they intend to implement their national programmes and the measures they will take to ensure the proper functioning of their programmes. The national programmes must be audited by the national authorities and the Commission in order to ensure their reliability and quality of information provided.
Each national programme must also contain detailed rules relating to information, communication measures and technical characteristics of such measures. These rules must be published on the Commission's website.
An annual accounting system must be in place for the annual clearance of accounts across the EU. This system must ensure the consistency of the accounts and the level of controls to be carried out.
Member States must:
ensure that the information they provide is accurate, reliable and up-to-date;
establish a website or website portal with the necessary information;
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to assess the effectiveness of the national programmes;
report regularly to the Council and the Parliament on the programme and its implementation.
If the Commission considers that the national programme is not being implemented correctly, it may require Member States to adopt measures to ensure its proper functioning.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  The European Union has adopted a number of specific regulations, including:
Directive 2014/20/EU establishing a European Union home affairs strategy (see summary);
Directives 2014/21/EU, 2014/22/EU and 2014/23/EU laying down specific rules for the application of the specific regulation;
Regulations (EU) No 852/2014, 2014-19/EU on the European Asylum and Migration Fund;
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom and Council Decision 1999/352/EC, ECSC and Euratom on the specific rules applicable to these specific regulations.
For more information, see:
European Union funding for home affairs (European External Action Service).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. Title V – Specific Regulation (Euratom, EEA) No 999/2012 of the Council of 21 December 2012 laying down the general provisions of the European Union specific regulations applicable to the Union funding programme (OJ L 347, 31.12



Summary 99:
EU Asylum and Migration Fund (EASF) (2013-2020)
EU asylum and migration Fund (2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 439/2010 establishing the European Asylum and Migrations Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU)-wide fund to support Member States in the field of immigration and asylum.
It aims to:
support Member States subject to particular pressure on their asylum systems;
contribute to the implementation of the Common European Asylum System;
provide financial support to voluntary burden-sharing operations agreed between Member States and consisting of the transfer of beneficiaries of international protection and of applicants for international protection from one Member State to another.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EASF supports Member States’ efforts to improve their asylum and integration policies and systems.
The Fund is designed to provide financial support for the following policy areas:
integration measures;
reintegration of asylum seekers and their families;
transit assistance;
refugee resettlement programmes;
the transfer of refugees and displaced persons from one EU country to another;
cooperation with the European Union Agency for Migration and Integration (Eurojust);
the implementation of a Union Resettlement Programme.
Funding
The fund’s main objectives are:
to contribute to the effective functioning of the common European asylum system, which encompasses measures relating to policy, legislation, and capacity-building, while acting in cooperation with other Member States, EU agencies and non-EU countries;
in cooperation with the EASObligations (e.g. Eurojust).
to provide financial assistance to Member States for the integration of refugees, their families and their extended family members;
to help Member States to cope with the impact of the influx of irregular migrants.
In addition, part of the available resources under the Fund should also be distributed for specific actions which require cooperative effort amongst Member States. These include:
the establishment of a European Asylum Support Office;
supporting Member States' efforts to integrate refugees and asylum seekers into the labour market;
helping Member States deal with the effects of the refugee crisis;
assistance to the European Commission and the European Border and Coast Guard in the event of a mass influx of displaced persons.
Cooperation
Member States are encouraged to cooperate with each other in order to develop, implement, monitor and evaluate in general all immigration and integration strategies, policies and measures for third-country nationals, including EU legal instruments. Member States are also encouraged to take the necessary measures to protect the integrity of their immigration systems, in particular by:
ensuring that asylum-seekers are returned to their countries of origin;
promoting cooperation between the national authorities;
encouraging the exchange of information and best practice;
developing, implementing, monitoring and evaluating the integration measures of Member States;
exercising the capacity of national authorities to integrate asylum seekers.
Transfer of beneficiaries and applicants for protection
The European Union is committed to providing financial assistance for Member States that are subject to a heavy influx of refugees or displaced persons, as well as Member States which are under heavy pressure in the area of migration. The Fund also provides financial assistance in the case of mass influxes of displaced people. It is also important to ensure that the application of emergency assistance is consistent with and, where relevant, complementary to the EU's humanitarian policy and respects humanitarian principles as set out in the European Consensus on Humanitarian Aid.
Exemptions from the Fund
The Commission may, where appropriate, exclude certain Member States from receiving financial assistance under their national programmes, such as Cyprus and Malta, from the allocation of the Fund. The Commission may also exclude certain countries from receiving the Fund under its national programmes from the amount of financial assistance allocated to them under the EU Migration Fund. In addition, Member States may not exclude national authorities from receiving certain amounts of financial support under their respective national programmes for specific integration actions.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has adopted a number of measures to support the migration and asylum policy of the EU, including:
establishing the European Migration Fund;
creating an Asylum and Integration Fund for the period 2013-2020.
For more information, see:
European Asylum Fund (European Commission).

1.1.2011

Summary 100:
Funding for the EU’s fight against transnational crime and terrorism: Multiannual Security Instrument (SIC)
Funding towards the fight against cross-border crime and terrorist threats
SIC
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 establishing a multiannual security instrument for the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union instrument to combat transnational threats to the EU.
It aims to support the work carried out by EU countries’ competent authorities in order to protect people and goods from increasingly transnational security threats and to strengthen the capacity to react to security-related incidents or newly emerging threats.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument applies to matters that are covered by the Justice programme, as set out in Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1382/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council. It does not apply to matters covered by Article 4 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) annexed to the TFEU.
The instruments are designed to:
support actions of particular interest to the Union (‘EU actions’);
provide emergency assistance and technical assistance;
contribute to the development of new technologies, transnational projects, networking and exchange of best practices;
monitor the implementation of relevant EU law and EU policies and actions in relation to and in non-EU countries;
encourage cooperation between Europol or other relevant EU bodies and EU countries.
promote the exchange of information and best practice;
develop cooperation between police cooperation, preventing and combating crime, crisis management and crisis management;
ensure the timely and appropriate transmission of relevant documents between EU countries and the European Commission.
Funds
The European Union budget allocated to the instrument is divided between the following areas:
emergency assistance
security-related actions
transnational projects
security measures
transborder projects
trans-national projects.
Implementation
The EU budget is divided into the following categories:
EU actions:
measures to combat terrorism and transnational organised crime;
measures in the field of police cooperation;
security actions to prevent and combat crime.
Transnational projects:
projects designed to protect the EU and its citizens from transnational terrorist threats;
projects in the area of transnational cooperation.
In addition, the instrument provides financial support for:
the establishment of a network of experts and experts in the fields of crime prevention and crime control;
the development of trans-border projects;
exchange of information on transnational transnational criminal threats and measures to protect EU citizens and goods;
cooperation between national authorities and Europol and other relevant European Union bodies and Member States.
EU countries are encouraged to use a part of the global resources available for the national programmes to address the strategic EU priorities set in Annex I to this regulation.
Monitoring
The Commission monitors the instrument, in accordance with the relevant provisions of the Regulation, with the aid of key indicators for evaluating results and impacts.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  The Internal Security Strategy for the Union adopted by the Council on 8-9 November 2010 aims at tackling the most important serious and organised criminal threats in the EU in a coherent and methodical manner through optimum cooperation between the relevant services.
For more information, see:
‘Fighting transnational terrorism’ on the European Council's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Transnational criminal threat: transnational threat: a threat to the safety and security of people, goods or property of an EU country or of a Member State.

  i.e. the instrument for combating transnational crimes and terrorism (OJ L 127, 1.1.2015, pp. 1-32)

Summary 101:
Non-sustainable fishing of stocks of common interest: EU action against non-EU action against countries allowing non-Sustainable fishing
Non-Sustainability of fisheries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 596/2011 — measures to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing in fisheries involving a common interest stock
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries do not allow countries to fish the stocks of the common interest (i.e. stocks within which the size of a stock can replenish itself with high probability while allowing high yield fisheries on it).
It sets out the type of measures that may be taken with regard to countries allowing such fishing.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fisheries involving a fishery involving a stock of interest to that country and to the EU;
fishing activities that jeopardise the sustainability of that stock, and fails to cooperate with other countries and the EU in its management;
non-EU countries with an interest in the fishery.
It does not apply to fishing in the waters of the Mediterranean, Black Sea or the Black Sea.
National authorities must notify the country concerned of the intention to identify it as a country allowing non sustainable fishing. The European Commission may adopt implementing acts determining whether the measures adopted in respect of the country are to apply to the country.
Implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the conditions laid down in the regulation. The measures must be based on objective criteria and be equitable, cost-effective and compatible with international law.
The Commission must also establish an appropriate system for their enforcement by competent authorities.
Examination procedure
A committee composed of representatives of the Member States and chaired by the Commission is responsible for the implementation of the implementing acts adopted pursuant to this regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
The common fisheries policy of the European Union (now the EU) is based on the principle of ‘common interest’. This means that the EU and its Member States are required to cooperate for the conservation and management of marine resources. This cooperation may be established in the framework of regional fisheries management organisations (RFMOs) or, where RFMOs have no competence for the stock in question, by means of ad hoc arrangements among the countries having an interest.
For more information, see:
Common fisheries policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Common interest: a stock within which there is a high probability of the stock replenishing itself with a high yield.
* Non-EU country: a third country, including territories enjoying self-governing status and endowed with competencies in the area of conservation, management of living marine resources, that allows, without due regard to existing fishing patterns or the rights, duties and interests of other countries or the EU, fisheries activities that could jeopardise its sustainability.

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements between the EU and non-EU countries: transitional rules for the movement of capital between EU countries and non EU countries
Bilateral investments between the European Union (EU) and third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the application of Article 3(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU to bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on the implementation of the TFEU’s Article 3 (bilateral investment agreement) and Article 9(1)(e) of Title V (the Treaty on European Union).
It aims to facilitate the negotiation, signing and conclusion of investment agreements with third countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that have signed a bilateral investment agreement with a non-European Economic Area (EEA) country between 1 December 2009 and 9 January 2013.
It does not apply to countries which have not yet entered into an agreement with the EU.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any agreement they wish to maintain in force or permit to enter into force by 8 February 2013. The notification must include a copy of those agreements.
The notification must be transmitted at least 5 months before formal negotiations are to commence.
If the Commission considers that the agreement does not comply with the requirements of the regulation, it may authorise the EU country to amend an existing or to conclude a new agreement.
Authorisation to amend or conclude an agreement is granted by the Commission on the basis of a proposal from the country concerned. The proposal must be accompanied by a legislative proposal.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
The agreement entered into force must be notified to the Commission at least 8 months before the date on which it is due to come into force. The Commission must publish in the Official Journal a list of the agreements notified pursuant to Article 2, Article 11(6) or Article 12(6).
The Commission may decide to suspend, amend or terminate an agreement if it considers that it does not conform with the rules of Article 9 and/or V of the European Economic Area.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The EU has exclusive competence with respect to the common commercial policy of the EEA countries. The regulation sets out transitional provisions for such agreements which have now come under the EU‘s exclusive competence.
For more information, see:
Investment agreements (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bilateral Investment Agreement: an investment agreement between an EU country and a third country.
* Economic Area: an economic area in which a country is a party to an economic agreement with another EU country. It is an area of mutual interest for both parties.

Summary 103:
Tariff preferences for sustainable development and good governance (SDRIVE FORWARD OF THE SCHEME
Trade preferences
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 establishing a general tariff preferences scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a general arrangement for preferential trade preferences for developing countries.
It sets out the conditions under which the European Union (EU) grants tariff preferences to developing countries to help them diversify their trade with the EU.
KEY POINTS
The general arrangement applies for a period of 10 years from the date of application of the preferences provided for in this regulation, except for a special incentive arrangement for the least-developed countries, which should continue to be applied without any expiry date.
Beneficiaries of the general arrangement are:
low-income or upper-middle income countries;
high-income countries with per capita income levels allowing them to attain higher levels of diversification without the scheme’s tariff preferences.
Eligible countries
Countries that fulfil the eligibility criteria for the scheme can benefit from the additional tariff preferences if, upon their application, the Commission determines that the relevant conditions are met. A list of eligible countries is established in Annex I of the regulation.
The European Commission must report to the European Parliament and to the Council every 5 years on the effects of the scheme under this regulation and assesses the need to review the scheme. It must also take into account the situation of the beneficiary countries and take account of the fight against terrorism and the situation in the EU industries. The Commission may decide to suspend the temporary withdrawal of certain tariff preferences, taking account of international standards on transparency and exchange of information in tax matters.
Temporary withdrawal of tariff preferences
The tariff preferences may be temporarily withdrawn if a beneficiary country does not respect its binding undertaking to maintain the ratification and effective implementation of international conventions or to comply with the reporting requirements imposed by the respective conventions, or if it does not cooperate with the monitoring procedures as set out in this Regulation. The temporary withdrawal may also be suspended if the beneficiary country:
does not comply with its obligations under the relevant international conventions;
is not complying with the requirements of the relevant monitoring bodies established under those conventions; or
has not complied with the relevant reporting requirements of those conventions.
Implementation of the tariff preferences is governed by delegated acts adopted by the Commission. The regulation sets out a procedure for the adoption of implementing acts on suspension from the preferential preferences of certain GSP sections and on the initiation of a temporary withdrawal procedure.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 November 2008.
BACKGROUND
The General Trade Preferences Scheme (GSP) was established by the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) in 1979, under which WTO Members may accord differential and more favourable treatment to developing and developing countries in order to encourage trade in goods and services between them.
For more information, see:
‘Tariff Preferences’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* General trade preferences: a preferential trade arrangement which allows the EU to grant preferential treatment to certain countries, such as developing countries, in the form of a quota or quota-based system, for goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries. These preferences are based on a number of factors, including:
the level of protection of intellectual property rights;
the quality of the products imported; and
the quantity of the goods imported.

Summary 104:
Export authorisation and import and transit measures for firearms, their parts and essential components and ammunition for the purpose of implementing the United Nations Protocol against the Illicit Manufacturing of and Trafficking in Firearms, their Parts and Components and Ammunition (the UN Firearms Protocol)
Export authorisations and import & transit measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 428/2009 — rules governing export authorisation, and import, brokering and transit of dual-use items for firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the export of firearms and their parts, essential components, ammunition and ammunition to and from the EU.
It aims to implement Article 10(3) of the UN Protocol against Illicit Manufacture of and trafficking in Firearms.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
firearms manufactured after 1899;
deactivated firearms*;
antique firearms* and their replicas as defined in accordance with national legislation, provided that antique firearms do not include firearms manufactured before 1899.
Exports
The exporter is the person who has the power for determining the sending of the item out of the customs territory of the EU to or through non-EU countries. If no export contract has been concluded or if the holder of the contract does not act on its own behalf, the exporter must be the one of the persons responsible for sending it out.
Authorisation
An authorisation is required for:
the export of all firearms, parts and ammunition listed in Annex I of the regulation;
the transfer of firearms for civilian use within the EU,
the import of firearms to and through the EU and
the transit of firearms from non-European Union countries.
Importation
Importers must apply for authorisation from the competent authorities of the country where they are established and must submit a written application to the EU country in which the item is being exported. The application must be accompanied by:
a copy of the export contract;
a statement of the reasons for the authorisation;
procedures to be followed in the event of any objection to the granting of authorisation.
EU countries must consult the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council before granting authorisation to an exporter.
The Commission may decide to issue an authorisation in writing or by electronic means. The Commission must inform the EU countries in writing of any objections it or they may have to granting such authorisations, which must bind the Member State which has granted it.
In addition, the Commission must:
inform the Member States in writing and in electronic form of the notification of the decision to grant authorisations;
ensure that the authorisations are valid for a period of 5 years;
notify the Commission of any amendments to the annexes to the regulation.
Transit
Imports and transit are subject to the same conditions as exports, except for the following:
transit must be authorised by the competent authority of the exporting Member State;
exports must not be carried out without the prior authorisation of the importing Member State.
If a Member State objects to the export or transit of a firearm, it must submit an objection within 20 working days of the import or transit. The exporter shall consult the other Member States and inform them of the reason for the objection. The Member States must also provide the Commission with all the relevant documents and information concerning the export and transit.
Member States must ensure that the consignee of the consignment is accompanied by a written declaration of the origin, destination and destination of the shipment. The consignment must also contain:
all the necessary documents and documents for the shipment;
all necessary documentation for the transit and export of the firearms;
any necessary documents, documents and technical documentation relating to the shipment of the firearm and the ammunition.
Annex II sets out the list of the items covered by the export authorisations. The regulation does not apply to the following items:
parts and ammunition;
firearm components;
ammunition;
non-firearm parts;
amphibious firearms.
Ammunition is subject to authorisation if it meets the following conditions:
it is in good condition;
it meets the requirements of the relevant national legislation;
there is no traceable traceability or traceability of the parts or ammunition; and
it does not contain any traceability, traceability and traceability measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 3 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
The EU’s export control system (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Firearms: a firearm which has been rendered permanently unfit for use by deactivation, ensuring that all essential parts have been rendered inoperable and incapable of removal, replacement or modification that would permit the firearm to be reactivated in any way



Summary 105:
EUROSUR framework for border surveillance and cooperation with the European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation (European Border and Coast Guard)
EurosUR framework: border surveillance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 establishing a framework for the exchange of information and cooperation in the field of border surveillance within the framework of EUROSUR
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework to improve the situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the Member States of the Union (‘external borders’) for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any legal or administrative measure taken once the responsible authorities of an EU Member State have intercepted cross–border criminal activities or unauthorised crossings by persons of the EU’s external borders. It does not apply to any measures taken in the context of operational cooperation coordinated by the Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms (EPHA).
EU countries must establish national coordination centres and communication networks to improve information exchange and cooperation on border surveillance between them and with the Agency.
The European situational picture and the common pre-frontier intelligence picture are essential for the establishment and application of surveillance tools.
Each Member State must establish a national coordination centre to exchange information and to exchange situational pictures of the borders of its territory.
National coordination centres must store sensitive information and communicate with each other and with other Member States.
A communication network must be set up to exchange and exchange information in a secure manner and to store sensitive sensitive information.
Member States must ensure that the communication network is secure and that national situational pictures are stored in a non-classified manner.
In addition, Member States must provide the Agency with the necessary information and tools to improve their situational awareness, reaction capability and situational awareness at their external borders for the prevention, detection and combating of illegal immigration.
Cooperation with neighbouring countries
The Member States should cooperate with neighbouring non-EU countries, particularly in the Mediterranean region, because well-structured and permanent exchanges of information, cooperation with those countries, in particular in the area of the Schengen region, are key factors for achieving the objectives of the EUROSU framework.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Border surveillance’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* External borders: the external border of a Member State, i.e. the border between the country of origin and that of the State of destination.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Eurosur) No 550/2006 of 20 December 2006 establishing a European framework for cooperation in border surveillance (OJ L 183, 31.12.2006, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EurOSUR) No 555/2006 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 107:
Protection of designations of origin and geographical indications
Protection and labelling of protected designations and traditional specialities guaranteed
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 596/2006 on the protection of protected designs of origin* and protected geographical indications of agricultural products and foodstuffs
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that designations* and specialities* guaranteed* are protected in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the conditions for the registration of these names.
KEY POINTS
Protected designations
The regulation applies to:
designations* of origin (e.g. ‘traditional speciality guaranteed’) and ‘protected geographical indications’*;
guaranteed products* (i.e. products that have been produced in the EU for a significant period of time and are guaranteed to have a high level of quality;
traditional products guaranteed (i).
The European Commission may adopt guidelines on optional quality terms, such as ‘mountain products’ or ‘tourist specialities’, which may give rise to confusion or mislead consumers.
The Commission may also adopt guidelines concerning the use of the ‘EU symbol’ on the labelling and the information and promotion of these indications.
Traditional products guaranteed are protected under Regulation (EU) No 652/2006.
Protective designations are protected against the misuse, imitation and evocation of the registered names on goods as well as on services.
Registered names
They are protected for a maximum period of 10 years and may be cancelled if they are found to be in conflict with a prior trade mark.
National authorities must ensure that:
the registered names are used fairly and in order to prevent practices liable to mislead consumers;
the protection of the protected names is extended to the misuse of the names;
there is a system that verifies compliance with the product specification;
they are subject to market surveillance relating to the marketing of products;
their use is protected against counterfeiting.
In addition to the general rules on labelling, Directive 2000/13/EC on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to labelling (see summary), the regulation sets out specific rules on:
protection of the designations, geographical indications and traditional products guaranteed;
information and promotion on the use, presentation and advertising of these designations;
use of the EU symbol and the indication on the packaging of protected products.
Guaranteeing the quality of traditional products
To guarantee to the consumer the specific characteristics of geographical indications, operators must be subject to a system to verify compliance with a product specification.
To ensure that names of genuine traditional products are registered under the scheme, the criteria and conditions for registration of a name should be adapted, in particular those concerning the definition of ‘Traditional’ and the scope of the term ‘guaranteeed’.
A register of registered names must be established. The Commission may decide to extend this register to cover non-traditional products. It must also establish a system for the verification of the compliance of protected names with the specification and for the promotion of the product.
It must be possible to associate the Union symbol with the designation of origin or the traditional speciality guarantee on the label of a protected product or to associate it with the indication.
Exemptions from protection
The protection of a designation or a geographical indication may be refused on the grounds of conflict with prior trade marks. It may also be refused if it does not comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation or if it is incompatible with a national law.
Implementation of the regulation
In order to facilitate the application of the directive, the Commission must adopt guidelines. These guidelines must be adopted in the light of the fact that the regulation does not apply to certain categories of products, including:
mountain products, which are not protected under the regulation;
tourists’ specialties guaranteed, which do not bear the designation or the geographical indication;
mammoth products, for which there is no EU symbol or indication; and
traditional specialities, which bear the indication or the designation.

Summary 108:
Phasing out of single-hull oil tankers and banning the transport of heavy grade oil in single-Hull vessels
Phasing-in of the application of the requirements of MARPOL 73/78 for the phasing-out of the use of heavy-duty oil tanker design
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2006 — phasing out and banning of the transport to or from EU ports of heavy category oil
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the application by EU countries of the European Maritime Organisation’s (IMO) rules on the design and construction of double hulled ships.
It aims to reduce the risk of accidental oil pollution in European waters.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
oil tankers of 5 000 tonnes deadweight and above:
which fly the flag of an EU country;
irrespective of their flag, which enter or leave a port or offshore terminal or anchor in an area under the jurisdiction of a EU country.
No oil tanker shall be allowed to operate in ports or offshore terminals under the EU country of its flag, nor shall any oil tanker, irrespective of the flag, enter into ports under the territory of a non-EU country, which are not under its jurisdiction.
EU countries must ensure that:
any oil tanker flying their flag must comply with Regulation 20 of Annex I to MARPOL 1973/78 as amended in 2004 by Resolution MEPCP 117(52) adopted by the IMOJoint Committee on the Marine Environment and Maritime Safety (MEPCPolicies of the EU;
any tanker flying its flag is not allowed to enter into or leave an EU port or an offshore terminal under its own jurisdiction unless it complies with Regulation 19 of the same regulation;
no oil tanker is allowed to anchor in any EU port under its flag unless it is a double-HULL oil tanker.
Exemptions
Annex I to the regulation allows for an exception for Category 2 and Category 3 oil tanker to continue to operate, under certain circumstances, beyond the time limit of their phasing* until the end of 2026.
Implementation of the regulation
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of the Regulation. This report must include:
the number and type of oil tanker;
the age of the tanker; and
the type of heavy oil it carries.
In addition, the Commission must publish an annual report on its implementation of this regulation. It must also publish a report on the progress made by the EU countries in implementing the regulation.
Repeal
The Regulation repeals and repeals Regulation 20/78/EEC as of 1 January 2008.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to ensure the safety of its maritime transport sector, including:
a ban on the import of heavy crude oil from the United States of America;
a prohibition on the export of crude oil to and from the EU.
For more information, see:
Heavy crude oil (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Heavy crude oil: oil tanker: a tanker of more than 5,000 tonnes.
* Single hulled: a ship of less than 10 metres in length.

Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 653 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 109:
Supervision of financial institutions in the European Union (ESFSF)
Supervisory arrangements in the Euro area
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1092/2010 establishing a European system of financial supervisory authorities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes the European System of Financial Supervisory Authorities (ESFSA).
It aims to improve the quality and consistency of national supervision, strengthening oversight of cross-border groups and establishing a single rule book applicable to all financial institutions operating in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The ESFS is an integrated network of national and EU supervisory authority, leaving day-to-day supervision to the national level. It is responsible for:
supervision of banks and credit rating agencies;
supervising the prudential supervision of insurance and reinsurance companies;
monitoring the financial stability of the EU’s financial system.
The European Systemic Risk Board (ESRB) forms part of the ESFS for the purposes of the tasks as specified in this regulation.
Supervision powers
The ESCFSA is empowered to:
suspend or restrict certain financial activities that threaten the orderly functioning and integrity of financial markets or the stability of financial systems in the Union in the cases specified and under the conditions laid down in the legislative acts referred to in the regulation;
make recommendations to the European Commission on how the ESCFS could play a strong role in crisis situations;
ensure that decisions taken by ESCFS authorities do not impinge on the fiscal responsibilities of EU countries.
It also has the power to adopt delegated acts.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
In November 2008, the Commission mandated a High-Level Group chaired by Jacques de Larosière to make recommendations on how to strengthen European supervisory arrangements with a view to better protecting the citizen and rebuilding trust in the system. The crisis exposed shortcomings in the areas of cooperation, coordination, consistent application of EU law and trust between national supervisors.
For more information, see:
European supervisory system (European Commission).

Summary 110:
Mobility of labour within the EU: mobility of workers, employment and vocational training (codified version)
Mobilities of labour, employment, vocational training and mobility of teachers and students
Mobility OFLIFE OF LIVES AND WORKERS
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/97 — mobility of labour
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC, No 553/96) No 652/96.
It aims to facilitate the movement of workers and their families within the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to nationals of all EU countries, regardless of their nationality.
Eligibility for employment
Nationals of the EU countries must be able to apply for employment in any undertaking, branch of activity or region, or at a national level, where they do not reside in the territory of an EU country.
They do not have to be subject to conditions of registration with employment offices or impede recruitment of individual workers, where persons who do not live in that country are concerned.
The European Coordination Office (ECO) is responsible for coordinating the coordination of the mobility of EU countries’ labour market services.
EU countries must send to the ECO a list of services which specialise in certain occupations or specific categories of persons. These services must cooperate directly with each other.
Workers’ families are entitled to the same social and tax advantages as nationals of the other EU countries.
Children of a national of a European Union country who is or has been employed in another EU country are admitted to that country’s general educational, apprenticeship and vocational education and training courses under the same conditions as the nationals of that EU country, if such children are residing in its territory.
Education and training
The Commission must forward to the Commission the list, drawn up by common accord, of services referred to in paragraph 1 and the Commission shall publish such list for information, and any amendment thereto, in the Official Journal of the Union.
In addition, the Commission must send the list to the specialist services of each EU country to provide as much practical information as possible on the various aspects of mobility, and the state of the labour market as is likely to be of guidance to workers from the other countries. The Commission must communicate these facts to the technical committee of the European Economic and Social Committee and to the specialised services of the Member States.
Information concerning living and working conditions
Each Member State must send a report to the Technical Committee on the living conditions of workers from other Member States and the State of the Labour Market. The report must summarise the information it has received and the data arising out of studies and studies carried out pursuant to the studies and analyses carried out by the technical committees. It must be communicated to the competent authorities in the Member State concerned and, where necessary, to the European Commission.
Vacancies and applications
The details of vacancies and applications for employment must be circulated according to a uniform system to be established by the European coordination office in collaboration with the European coordinination office. The services responsible for border regions must also set up cooperation and service structures to provide:
users with practical information on mobility;
management and labour, social services (in particular public, private or those of public interest) and all institutions concerned, with a framework of coordinated measures relating to mobility,
official employment services which are specialised in certain occupation or specific category of persons, with cooperation directly with one another.
Where necessary, the services territorially responsible for cross-border regions shall also establish cooperation structures to:
provide users with practical advice on mobility as possible;
contribute to the development of a harmonised system of mobility for workers, their families and employers;
cooperate with the specialisation services in the fields of education, training, vocational education, vocational and employment;
carry out joint studies and analysis of labour market developments;
coordinate the implementation of joint measures at European level with the technical coordination office.

Summary 111:
Ban on the use of hydrochlorofluorocarbons in the manufacture, import and export of products and equipment containing or relying on ozone-depleting substances (ODS)
Ban onthe use of ozone depletion substances (ODS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2037/2000 on the ban on use of substances and mixtures that Deplete the Ozone Layer
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) does not use any substances that deplete the ozone layer.
It recasts and repeals the regulation of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 July 1991 concerning the placing on the market of substances that deplete the ozone Layer.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the production, import, export and use of products or equipment containing, relying on, or being used for the maintenance or servicing of refrigeration and air conditioning equipment;
the import, transit, storage, customs warehousing and the free zone procedure of products, equipment or mixtures containing ODS.
Exemptions
The ban applies to certain substances such as methyl bromide, chlorinated biphenylsulfate, perchlorate, trichloroethylene and perchlorohexane. It does not apply to the following the entry into force of the Montreal Protocol on Substances that depleting the ozone (see summary) and to certain other substances.
the ban on the export of certain products, including:
mixtures containing and relying on:
perchlorate;
methanol;
perfluoromethane;
pyrrolizidine;
pentane.
The annexes to the regulation supplement the Protocol with decisions of the parties to the protocol.
Implementation of the regulation
In order to ensure compliance with the Protocol and to improve their practical application, the Commission is empowered to modify the reporting requirements for EU countries and undertakings.
EU countries are required to report to the Commission every 5 years on the substances they use and the quantities of substances they have used.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules on the labelling, labelling and packaging of substances, as well as on the disposal of waste.
Monitoring
The Commission is responsible for the monitoring of the substances used in the EU and for their disposal. It is also responsible for assessing the impact of these substances on human health and the environment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Ozone depletion’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Ozone depletion substance: a substance that depletes the atmosphere of ozone by burning certain organic compounds, such as sulphur hexafluoride.

  i.e. the regulation on the prohibition of the use, importation, export or use of certain substances and of mixtures of substances classified as ODS (Recast) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2002, pp. 1-8)

Summary 112:
European Union Agency for the Protection of the Environment (EURatom) (European Union agency for the protection of the environment) (EC) No 1049/2001)
European Union Environment Agency (Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2001 on the European Union’s Environmental Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Environmental Agency (EPA).
The purpose of the EPA is to:
ensure that the public is properly informed about the state of the European environment;
provide the necessary technical and scientific support.
KEY POINTS
   
The Agency is responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on the state and future state of Europe's environment.
Its main tasks include:
monitoring and analysing the quality of the EU's environment and the pressures on it;
ensuring that the environment is taken into account in the formulation of EU environmental policy;
assisting the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the European Committee of the Regions in the preparation of environmental policy.
EU countries must keep the Agency informed of the main component elements of their national environment information networks, especially in the priority areas, including any institution which in their judgment could contribute to the work of the Agency, taking into account the need to ensure the fullest possible geographical coverage of their territory.
The European Parliament and the Council designate, as national focal points, a ‘national focal point’ for coordinating and/or transmitting information to the Agency and the information to be supplied at national level to the institutions or bodies forming part of the Network, including the topic centres referred to in Article 4 of the regulation.
Each year, the Agency adopts its multiannual programme on the basis of a draft submitted by the Executive Director after consulting the European Commission and receiving its opinion.
A management board is set up by the Agency. It is composed of representatives of the Member States, the Commission and the Management Board. It meets at least twice a year. The Executive Director is assisted by a scientific committee. The committee is composed, in particular, of members particularly qualified in the field of environmental protection, and is chaired by a Member State representative.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 30 April 2001.
BACKGROUND
 
  The EPA is one of a number of agencies responsible for the implementation of the environmental policy of European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘The European Environment Agency’ on the EPA's website.

Summary 113:
Eco-management and audit scheme (EMAS) for organisations in the environment and sustainable development
Eco–management and auditing scheme (EASA) for companies in and outside the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 761/2001 establishing an eco-management* and audit* scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes an eco–management* scheme for organisations whose activities have an environmental impact.
It aims to improve the environmental performance of companies in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all companies whose activities affect the environment, whether or not they are located in the EU.
Organisations must:
make publicly available periodic environmental statements providing the public and other interested parties with information on their compliance with applicable legal requirements relating to the environment;
ensure that their environmental policy and environmental management systems are in place;
have an environmental programme, objectives and targets;
meet the deadlines for achieving the environmental objectives;
provide an annual report on their environmental performance.
An organisation’s environmental statement and updated environmental statement must be reliable, credible and correct and meet the requirements of this regulation.
Accreditation bodies and competent bodies must ensure that the environmental statements and environmental statements are validated and that they are available to the public.
The European Commission must produce sectoral reference documents in the area covered by this regulation following a priority programme.
EU countries may grant incentives to registered organisations such as access to funding or tax incentives in the framework of schemes supporting the performance of industry, provided that the organisations are able to demonstrate an improvement of their environmental quality.
EMAS is open to all organisations, including those located in non-EU countries, that have an impact on the environment.
Participation in EMAS is voluntary and may be made on a voluntary basis and may gain added value in terms of regulatory control, cost savings and public image.
Recognition of accredited bodies
The Commission must establish an accreditation body and a competent body for each EU country. The accreditation bodies are responsible for monitoring and verifying the compliance of organisations with the environmental requirements of EMAS. The competent bodies are appointed by the European Commission and must be independent of national authorities. They must also ensure that organisations comply with the rules of the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEA).
The competent bodies have the power to issue accreditation and supervision licences to organisations that have been accredited under EMAS and to supervise their environmental statements.
In addition, the Commission must publish sectoral references for the areas covered by the regulation. These refer to the EU countries’ national reference documents on the environmental aspects, environmental impacts and environmental performance related to an organisation's activities, products and services.

Summary 114:
Rules on the production, description, presentation, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks and the protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks (Regulation (EC) No 510/2006)
Rules on:
the definition, description*, presentation*, labelling*, production*, and presentation
the protection of certain geographical indications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition* and presentation*, description*, description**, presentation, labelling* and production*, as well as on the protection* of geographical indication*.
It does not apply to spirit drinks produced in the EU for export.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks:
from any agricultural raw material listed in Annex I to the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU);
from raw materials of agricultural origin other than cereals and/or potatoes.
It applies to:
vodka;
wine;
spirits;
bourbon;
pomace;
malt;
cocoa;

coffee;
vegetable spirits.
The rules apply to the following categories:
cognac, sparkling wine, sparkling cider, rum, liqueur, rum and liqueurs;

The regulations also apply to spirits produced on their own territory and in particular for geographical indications registered in Annex III or for the establishment of new geographical indications.
Labelling
Each spirit drink must bear the following information on its label:
type of spirit drink;
name and description;
description of the spirit drink and its presentation;
the geographical indication;
type and description of the geographical indication (Annex II);
the minimum alcoholic strength of the product in category 41 in Annex II.

be sweetened to correspond to particular product characteristics and according to Annex I(3) to this regulation and taking into account the particular legislation of Member States.
General rules on the quality of the products produced and their labelling
Member States may adopt rules stricter than those laid down in this regulation on production, describing, presentation*, presentation andlabelling. They must apply a quality policy to ensure a high level of quality and diversity in the sector. These rules must be adopted in accordance with the rules laid down by the European Commission.
Exceptions to the above rules
The following products are excluded from the scope of the regulation:
alcoholic spirits (e.g. rum and cider);
wine, cider or cider-based spirits (i.e. those with a maximum alcoholic strength greater than 0.5% alcohol);
veterinary spirits (vodka and rum-based drinks);
coppernauts (cider and cider).
Exemptions from the regulation include:
wine-based spirit drinks which are produced on the territory of a non-EU country;
spirits which are made from raw materials other than those listed in the annex to the regulation;
drinks which are not made from agricultural raw materials.

Summary 115:
Common rules for safeguarding civil aviation (common basic standards on aviation security) - Regulation (EC) No 2320/2002 (see summary)
Common rules on the safeguarding of civil aviation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 3232/2002 on common rules and common basic standards for aviation security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes common rules for the protection of persons and goods travelling by air within the European Union (EU).
It seeks to simplify, harmonise and clarify the existing rules and the improvement of the levels of security.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following:
all airports or parts of airports located in the territory of an EU country that are not exclusively used for military purposes;
all operators, including air carriers, providing services at airports referred to in point (a);
all entities applying aviation security standards that operate from premises located inside or outside airport premises and provide goods and/or services to or through airports;
account consignors* who originates cargo or mail for its own account and whose procedures meet common security rules and standards sufficient to allow carriage of that cargo on all-cargo aircraft or mail on an all-mail aircraft.
It does not apply to:
passenger aircraft;
airside baggage;
cabin baggage*;
baggage intended for carriage in the cabin of an aircraft; and
aircraft security search.
EU countries must draw up a national civil aviation security programme.
The European Commission may use the urgency procedure for the adoption of the common rules.
Measures for monitoring compliance
Each EU country must:
ensure that:
airport security officers are specially selected and trained, taking into account the required security and safety aspects on board an aircraft,
in-flight security officers have the necessary training and experience;
security measures are in place to detect and prevent unlawful interference with civil aircraft that jeopardises the security of the aircraft. These measures must be implemented in accordance with the rules of the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA).
The Commission may adopt general measures, designed to amend non-essential elements of the regulation by supplementing them, to ensure that it is complied with.
In addition, the Commission may decide to grant derogations to non-EU countries if they consider that these measures are equivalent to those of the EU countries.
Exemptions from security controls
A number of EU countries are exempt from the requirements of this regulation. These include:
the United Kingdom (1);
Ireland (2);
France (3);
Germany (4);
the Netherlands (5);
Romania (6);
Spain (7);
Italy (8);
Iceland (9);
Sweden (10);
Austria (11);
Bulgaria (12);
Norway (13);
Turkey (14);
Yugoslavia (15);
Syria (16);
Zimbabwe (17);
United Arab Emirates (18);
Vietnam (19); and
United States of America (20).
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFORCE IN THE MEDIARY
EU NAVFOR MED IRINI
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 on a European Union military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) military operation (EUVFOR MEDINI) in Libya.
It aims to prevent the illicit export of petroleum from Libya, to capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy, and to disrupt the business model of human smuggling and trafficking networks.
KEY POINTS
The operation is designed to:
contribute to the implementation of the UN arms embargo on Libya with aerial, satellite and maritime assets;
provide situational awareness in the area of operation and in the Area of Interest;
support the detection and monitoring of trafficking networks through information gathering and patrolling carried out by aerial assets above the high seas, in the agreed Area of Operation.
In addition, the operation contributes to the prevention of the export of weapons of mass destruction to Libya, as well as to the disruption of trafficking in human beings and drugs.
EUNAFOR MEDIRINI may, in particular in the course of inspections carried out in accordance with UN Security Council Resolution (UNSCR) 2292 (2016), collect and store evidence related to the carriage of items prohibited under the arms embargo.
As a secondary task, and in line with UNSCR 2240 (2015), the operation is to support the training of Libyan coast guard and navy personnel.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLYEARNING THE OPERATION
The decision has applied since 31 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been involved in a number of operations in Libya since 19 January 2020, including:
Operation Medirini (EUAFRICOM (European Union Maritime and Coast Guard Mission in Libya);
EUFOR MEDICOM (EU Maritime and Coastal Guard Mechanism in Libya).

Summary 118:
Fraudulent travel documents and false travel documents: FADO (European Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation) system
Frictionless application of the laws of the European Union (FADO)* system
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 on the establishment, operation and management of the Facility for the Analysis of Authentic Documents and False Travel Documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the establishment and operation of the facility for the analysis of authentic travel, identity, residence and civil status documents, driving licences and vehicle licences and on other related official documents, in particular those used in support of applications for official documents issued by third parties, such as third countries, territorial entities, international organisations and other entities subject to international law, and on false versions thereof.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (Joint Action 98/700/JHA) on document and identity fraud.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The purpose of the regulation is to contribute to the fight against document-related fraud by sharing information on the security features of, and potential fraud in, authentic and false documents between the EU countries’ competent authorities.
EU countries are obliged to provide the Agency with information on authentic documents which they issue, including on security features thereof, and the false versions of such documents in their possession. The Agency will enter that information in the system and guarantee the uniformity and quality of the information.
The Agency will also provide the general public with different levels of access to the system depending on their needs and the sensitivity of the data concerned. It will not be possible to search for any elements of personal data in this system.
In addition to the Agency’s role, the system will also be used by the European Commission, the Agency, the European Border and Coast Guard (BAR) and the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA).
The system will be used for the following purposes:
the exchange of information between EU countries, the EU and non-EU countries;
the sharing of information on methods of falsification of authentic documents and the identification of persons involved in such falsification;
identification of persons who have been identified by means of the system;
exchanges of information and recommendations on effective ways of detecting and preventing document fraud;
implementation of measures to combat document fraud by EU countries and international organisations.
Exchange of information
The European Agency will be able to exchange information on:
authentic travel documents,
driving licences and
vehicle licences;
travel, identity and residence documents;
driving licence and vehicle licence applications;
passports and visas;
visas and travel documents issued in the EU;
other related documents used in the context of the Schengen acquis.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
The Facility for Authentication of Documents and false Travel Documents was set up in 2003. It was designed to facilitate information exchange between the competent authorities of EU countries on authentic and falsified travel documents. It has been in operation since 2004.
For more information, see:
Document and Identity Fraud (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Document and Identity Document: an official document issued by an EU country.
* False travel document: a document that has been altered or falsified in order to facilitate the entry into the EU of a non-authorised person.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 of 21 December 2018 establishing the framework, operation, management and control of the Functioning of the Agency for Legal Aid and Enforcement of the Law of the Member States, the Union and third parties in the area of document fraud (OJ L 347, 28.12.2018, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) No 347 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 119:
Access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions: proportionality and transparency (Mutual Evaluation Process)
Access to, orthe pursuit of: regulated professions
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2005/36/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the access to and pursuit of regulated professions (mutual evaluation process)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to harmonise the requirements on access to a regulated profession or to the pursuit thereof laid down in European Union (EU) law.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the following professions:
medicine and pharmaceuticals;
health and social care;
lawyers and accountants.
It does not apply to:
professional organisations or bodies;
professional societies and bodies.
EU countries must assess the proportionality of the measures they introduce, and the additional criteria which are relevant for the regulated profession being analysed.
Proportionality
The measures must be applied in a non-discriminatory manner; be justified by public interest objectives; be suitable for securing the objective which they pursue; and not go beyond what is necessary in order to attain that objective.
If a measure does not meet the above criteria, it should not be considered to be suitable.
Where the independence of a profession cannot be adequately guaranteed by other means, EU countries could consider the application of safeguards, such as limiting the shareholding of persons outside the profession or providing that the majority of voting rights are to be held by persons practising the profession, as long as such safeguards do not constitute an overriding reason in the public interest.
Scope
The regulation applies to all professions in the EU. It does not cover:
medical products and services;
medical devices and equipment;
legal advice and training;
employment and social security schemes;
education and training.
Exemptions
Certain professions are excluded from the scope of the directive. These include:
the legal profession of a Member State;
the profession of an Accountant or Accountant;
a Member State’s professional organisation or body;
an organisation that is entrusted by the State with safeguarding the legitimate practice of the profession;
membership of a professional organisation, or organising or supervising continuous professional training; and
members of professional societies or bodies that are responsible for the proper functioning of the internal market.
In addition, Member States may exempt certain professions from the obligation to undergo continuous professional development. For example, certain professions may be exempt from this obligation if they meet certain conditions.
Quality of service
Member States must ensure that the quality of the service provided is high and that it is accessible to the public. Member States must also ensure that:
there is a high level of transparency in the supply chain;
there are adequate safeguards to protect the health and safety of patients;
procedures are in place to ensure that medicines are safe and that they are easily accessible to consumers.
The Member State concerned must also take into account the impact of the regulation on the free movement of persons and services within the Union, on consumer choice and on the quality and quantity of the services provided.
Mutual evaluation procedure
The European Commission has launched a mutual evaluation process to assess the measures that Member States have adopted in relation to access to regulated professions. It is based on a number of criteria, including:
proportionality;
quality of service;
availability of information;
impact on the public;
effects on the economy.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directives 2005/35/EC and 2006/34/EC have applied since 1 January 2005.
DirectIVE 2006/24/EC has applied since 31 December 2006.
Amending Directive 2006/28/EC entered into force on 1 January 2007 and had to become law in Member States by 31 December 2008.
Regulation (EC) No 596/2009 has applied from 1 January 2009.
For more information, see:
‘Medicine’ on the European Commission's website.

Management of fishing stocks and their fisheries in North Sea, Baltic Sea and Black Sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the implementation of the common fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a plan for the management of fishing opportunities in EU waters of the north and Baltic seas.
It sets out the objectives, quantifiable targets, conservation reference points, safeguards and technical measures designed to avoid and reduce unwanted catches.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing opportunities for all stocks of species to which the EU has a landing obligation;
fisheries in the Baltic Sea, the Black Sea and adjacent waters, as well as to demersal stocks;
the North Sea.
Stocks of species covered by the plan

a range of fish species and their fishing opportunities;
measures to prevent, reduce and eliminate unwanted catches;
target ranges and safeguards;
timeframes, targets, time frames and margins;
safeguards.

it must:
ensure that fishing opportunities are fixed in accordance with the objectives;
contribute to the sustainable management of mixed fisheries, i.e.
Safeguards and measures

measures designed to reduce the risk of overfishing;
monitoring measures to ensure that the plan is implemented;
implementing technical measures to protect the marine environment.
Monitoring measures
EU Member States must submit joint recommendations to the Commission by 6 August 2023 on the application of the plan. The Commission must assess the adequacy and effectiveness of this regulation based on scientific advice.
In addition, Member States may adopt temporary cessation measures that have been adopted in order to attain the plan’s objectives. These measures must be submitted to the European Commission within 6 months of the end of the 5-year period.

Summary 121:
Fishing for straddling fish stocks in the South-East Pacific Fishery Management Area (SPFMO Convention Area)
Fishing in the south-east Pacific Fisheries Management Area
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — management, conservation and control provisions relating to fishing for fishing for fish species in the SPRFMO convention area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for fishing in the southern part of the South–East Pacific Maritime Forests Management Area.
It aims to ensure the long-term conservation and sustainable use of fishery resources by the application of the precautionary approach to fisheries management and an ecosystem-based approach.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing vessels operating in the convention area;
third country fishing vessels transhipping fishery products harvested in the area; and
third countries fishing vessels upon requesting access to, or being the object of an inspection in, EU ports and carrying fishery product from the area.
Seabird mitigation measures
All EU fishing vessels using longlines shall be subject to the seabird-mitigation measures set out in this regulation. These include:
a ban on discharging offal during shooting and hauling;
the use of gillnets;
a prohibition on the use of nets, which is more than 2.5 kilometres in length;
an obligation to inform the Commission of any significant adverse impacts on seabirds during the normal course of fishing operations.
the level of observer coverage dedicated to recording seird.
The Commission must transmit the annual scientific report covering the previous year.
Fisheries are subject to a ban on the dumping of offal into the sea.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
seabird data are reported to the Commission by the 20th day of each month;
fisheries vessels are informed of any adverse impacts to the marine ecosystems caused by their fishing activities;
seafarers are informed in advance of any changes in the conditions of the sea;
EU countries are required to report the results of their fishing operations to the European Commission every 2 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Union has signed the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea, which contains principles and rules with regard to the conservation and management of the living resources of the seas.
For more information, see:
The EU’s fisheries policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Seabird: any marine animal or plant which is threatened by significant adverse impact resulting from physical contact with bottom gears in the normal process of fishing activities, including reefs, seamounts, hydrothermal vents, cold water corals or cold water sponge beds.
* Fishing vessel: any fishing vessel flying their flag and fishing in an area covered by the Convention.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еграканска барка

Summary 122:
Exchange of information on movements of cash within the European Union (EU) (Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005) (SUMMARY)
Exchange, storage and exchange of data on cross-border movements of certain types of cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to prevent and deter the movement of cash* within the EU.
It aims to prevent money laundering and terrorist financing.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
natural persons entering or leaving the EU;
cash couriers;
travel agents;
transport companies.
Controls
EU countries must:
impose all requisite controls on persons, their luggage, the means of transport used to cross the external borders and any unaccompanied consignment or receptacle crossing that border which may contain cash;
ensure that controls are based primarily on a risk analysis, with the purpose of identifying and evaluating the risks and developing the necessary countermeasures.
The competent authorities collect the declarations and perform controls under this regulation.
Where the competent authorities register a failure to declare or disclose cash or where there are indications of criminal activity, they should promptly share that information with other EU countries through appropriate channels.
Information must be exchanged between the authorities and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and Europol, as well as between the EU countries participating in enhanced cooperation pursuant to that regulation and with the United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime (UNODC).
The information must be recorded in a standardised format. It should be made available to the Commission, to the European Public Prosecutor’s Office and to Europol.
Exceptions to the obligation to declare and disclose cash include:
individuals who have been convicted of a criminal offence;
persons who have committed a serious offence in another EU country;
those who are suspected of being involved in a terrorist organisation.
In addition, the regulation allows EU countries to restrict movements of capital that cause, or threaten to cause, serious difficulties for the operation of economic and monetary union or under Articles 143 and 144 TFEU as a result of a sudden crisis in the balance of payments.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Movement of cash’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Movement of cash: any form of highly-liquid store of value, such as cash couriers, travel agents and travel agents, that can be moved by means of means other than a passport or visa.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 89/2005 of 21 May 2005 on the obligation of natural persons entering and leaving the Union to declare cash (OJ L 127, 23.5.2005, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Council Regulation (Euratom) no 1889/05 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
