Summary 123:
Energy Union: national energy and climate plans (Regulation (EC) No 517/2014) (summary)
energy union: national and regional energy plans (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2018/852 on the Energy Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the energy policy objectives and targets for the EU and its Member States for the period 2018-2023.
It aims to:
ensure a resilient energy union with an ambitious climate policy at its core;
promote market integration, increased competition, the attainment of climate and energy objectives and the gradual shift towards a sustainable low-carbon economy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers five dimensions:
energy security;
the internal energy market;energy efficiency;energy decarbonisation;research, innovation and competitiveness;
energy poverty;
research and innovation.
The objectives of the energy union are:
a secure, sustainable, competitive and affordable energy market for consumers and businesses;
a share of renewable energy consumed in the EU of at least 27% by 2030;
electricity interconnection of 15% of the EU’s electricity market;
an increase in the share of energy efficiency;
decarbonisation.
In order to achieve these objectives, Member States must:
improve the efficiency of energy production and distribution systems;
increase renewable energy consumption;
reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by at least 30%;
percentage of energy poverty in the Member States;
carbonise the energy economy;
by 2030, at least 25% of electricity interconnections;
improve energy efficiency in the European electricity market.
National energy plans
Member States should:
prepare an integrated national energy plan for each of the five dimensions of the Energy union;
inform the public of their national plans;
draw up public consultations on the objectives of their plans.
Public consultation
The Member States should ensure that the public is fully involved in the preparation of the national energy plans. They should also ensure the involvement of social partners in preparing the national plans, and aim to limit administrative complexity when fulfilling their obligations with regard to public consultation.
When carrying out public consultations, and in line with the Aar Convention, the public should be able to access all the information they need to make their views known, including:
the number of households in energy poverty, taking into account the necessary domestic energy services needed to guarantee basic standards of living in the relevant national context, existing social policy and other relevant policies, as well as Commission indicative guidance on relevant indicators, including geographical dispersion, that are based on a common approach for energy poverty.
Annex II to the regulation sets out a timetable for the preparation and publication of the first draft and final integrated national plans. The plan must be adopted by 31 December 2018. The Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the progress made by Member States towards achieving the objectives set out in the energy Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  The Energy Union aims to give EU consumers, including households and businesses, secure and sustainable energy.
For more information, see:
The Energy Union (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy Union: an energy policy framework for Europe.
* Renewable energy: a renewable energy target for the European Union for 2030 of 32 % of the total energy consumed, including a review with a view to increasing the Union-level target by 2023 to 30%.
* Decarbonisation: a transition to a low carbon economy that reduces greenhouse gas emissions by as much as possible.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. implementing Regulation (EEC) no 652/2018 of the European Central Bank and of the Council of 21 December 2018 establishing a framework for the implementation of a resilient Energy Union and amending Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 852/2014 (summary).
* Regional energy cooperation: a European energy cooperation framework for North, South and West.

Summary 124:
Eliminating persistent organic pollutants (POPs) in the environment: Regulation (EC) No 1907/2006 and Regulation (EU) No 528/2012 (Recast)
Elimination of persistent industrial pollutants in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1906/2006 on the control of POPs
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to eliminate the production, manufacture, placing on the market and use of persistent organometallic pollutants (‘pOPs’) and to reduce their release into the environment.
It recasts and repeals the previous regulation (Regulation No 1907 / 2006/2006/EC).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out rules on POPs, as well as on the listing of substances and the monitoring of waste containing POPs.
EU countries must:
ensure that POPs which are unintentional by-products of industrial processes are identified and reduced as soon as possible, with the ultimate aim of elimination, where feasible;
monitor and report regularly to the European Union Agency for Safety and Health at Work (Euratom).
Control of the production and manufacture of substances listed in Annex II, whether on their own, in mixtures or in articles, must be restricted, subject to a concentration limit of 500 mg/kg. The maximum concentration limit is set by the European Commission and the European Agency for the Protection of Life and Health of the Environment (EPHA).
The European Commission must review that concentration limit in the light of scientific and technical progress and, where appropriate, and adopt a legislative proposal to lower it to 500mg/kg by 16 July 2021 and, in any event, not later than 16 July 2023.
In addition, EU countries are required to:
report regularly, in standardised form, to the EEA on POP inventories, notified stockpiles and the manufacturing and placing on market of restricted substances;
keep a record of all POPs that have been manufactured, manufactured or placed on the EU market;
report to the Commission, the European Parliament and the Council regularly on the implementation of this regulation.
The Commission may adopt measures concerning waste management and the minimum information to be provided by EU countries in monitoring the implementation.
Evaluation of the regulation’s implementation
In order to ensure uniform conditions for the implementation, the Commission has established the Information Platform for Chemical Monitoring. It is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation and monitoring of the Regulation;
assisting the Commission in drawing up, implementing and updating its implementation plans;
coordinating with the European Environment Agency (EEA) and the national authorities of EU countries on waste management;
ensuring that waste is not disposed of as hazardous waste.
Monitoring of waste and monitoring measures
The EEA and the EU countries must ensure that waste and waste-related measures are implemented in accordance with the rules laid down in Directive 2008/98/EC on hazardous waste (see summary).
Waste management and monitoring
The waste management system should be based on a standardised reporting system. It should include:
the reporting of waste to the Agency and the Commission;
the monitoring and reporting of the quantities of waste that are disposed of;
reporting of the total amount of waste;
a system for monitoring and monitoring POPs and waste containing waste; and
a monitoring system for the monitoring and control of waste-containing substances.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2008/96/EC has applied since 16 July 2006. The regulation had to become law in the Member States by 16 June 2007.
BACKGROUND
The EU is seriously concerned by the continuous release of persistent organic pollutant (POCs) into the EU. It has signed the Protocol and Convention on the Elimination of Persistent Organic Pollutants and the Convention on Biological Diversity.
For more information, see:
‘Persistent organic pollutants’ on Euratom's website.
KEY TERMS
* POPs: organic pollutants: any substance that is a by-product of an industrial process.
* Concentration limit: the maximum concentration of an organic substance in a kilogram of solid, liquid or powder.
ACT
Council Regulation (EURatom) no 1906/06/2006 of 16 June 2006 laying down the rules for the control, manufacture and use, and the list of substances (OJ L 127, 16.7.2006, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (e.g. Annex II) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products: reducing their impact on the environment and human health (Directive 2013/34/EU)
Single-usable plastic products (Directives 2013/35/EU and 2013/36/EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It aims to reduce the quantity of plastic waste generated in the European Union (EU) and to tackle the problem of marine litter.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all plastic products that are found the most on beaches in the EU as well as fishing gear containing plastic and products made from oxo-degradable plastic.
It does not apply to:
glass and metal beverage containers;
plastic fishing gear;
single-use drinking containers and lids made of plastic;
conformity with the EU’s legislation on the labelling and labelling of foodstuffs;
non-toxic plastic packaging materials.
Producers’ responsibility
EU Member States are required to ensure that producers of certain single-use products cover the costs associated with their production and disposal. They must also take responsibility for the environmental impact of their products and ensure that they are properly disposed of.
They must also ensure that their products are reusable and that they can be reused and recycled.
Labelling requirements
Beverage containers, lids and plastic fishing gear are subject to labelling requirements. The labels must be clearly visible, legible and clearly visible. The packaging must also bear the following information:
the number of times they have been used;
the quantity of waste they have generated and disposed of
the amount of litter they have caused.
the total amount of plastic that has been thrown into the environment.
The packaging must be designed to be reusable, reusable or recyclable.
If a product is not reusable, it must bear a marking indicating that it has been reused, reused or recycled. The label must also indicate that it can be recycled or reused.
Manufacturers must ensure that the packaging they make conforms with the requirements laid down in the directive and that it meets the requirements of the European Strategy for Plastics (see summary).
The European Commission must review the directive in the light of the relative properties of different packaging materials, including composite materials, on the basis of life cycle assessments, addressing in particular waste prevention and design for circularity.
In addition, Member States must take measures to prevent the spread of plastic litter in the marine environment and to reduce its impact on human health and the economy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directive 2012/35 has applied since 1 January 2013. It had to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle plastic pollution, including Directive 2008/98/EC on plastic waste. These have been amended several times, most recently by Directive 2014/13/EU.
For more information, see:
‘Plastic pollution’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
  барканска накова грака   глаксанда
Council Directive 2013/33/EU of 21 December 2013 on the reduction of plastic pollution (OJ L 183, 1.1.2013, pp. 1-18)

Summary 126:
EU trade mark protection — rules for goods and services (codified Regulation (EU) No 608/2013)
EU Trade mark protection rules — codified Regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 652/2013 on the registration of trade marks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the registration and protection of EU trade marks.
It aims to open up unrestricted economic activity in the whole of the internal market for the benefit of undertakings.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to trade marks which are governed by a uniform EU law directly applicable in all EU countries. It does not apply to national trade marks or trade names.
Applicants for protection must submit an application for protection to the European Commission. The application must be accompanied by a declaration of the goods or services for which the trade mark is sought.
The Commission decides whether the protection is sought only as a national trade mark in one or more EU countries, or only as an EU trade mark, or both.
EU trade marks are granted exclusive rights for the duration of their protection. The proprietor of a trade mark does not have the right to prohibit the use of signs or indications by third parties which are used fairly and thus in accordance with honest practices in industrial and commercial matters.
Rights conferred on the proprietor
The proprietor has exclusive rights to:
use a sign in comparative advertising where such comparative advertising is contrary to specific EU legislation;
the use of a sign or indication in advertising where comparative advertising does not conform with the EU legislation.
Use of the trade marks in the form in which it is registered should not alter the distinctive character of the mark;
use of a mark in a form that differs in elements which do not alter its distinctive character.
Enforcement of the rights conferred by an EU Trade Mark
The trade mark may be enforced against any third party that uses it in a manner which is contrary, in principle or in practice, to the rules of the European Union (EU).
The rights conferred on proprietors must be enforced without prejudice to the rights of proprietors acquired prior to the filing or priority date of the registration.
Application procedure
The applicant must apply for protection in writing to the competent authorities and economic operators of the EU countries in order to enable them to determine the extent of the protection applied for. The applicant must provide the Commission with the information necessary to determine whether the application is valid. The Commission may reject the application if it considers that the application does not comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
If the Commission considers that an application is not valid, it must inform the applicant of the reasons for rejecting the application.
In addition, the Commission must ensure that the applicant is informed of the procedure for granting or revoking the protection granted to the applicant.
Where the applicant fails to comply with this procedure, the applicant may be subject to administrative proceedings before the European Court of Auditors.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world’s largest single market for trade marks and the Commission has adopted a number of measures to open it up to competition.
For more information, see:
Trade marks (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: a trade name used to describe a product or service.
ACT
Commission Implementing Regulation (Euratom), Euratom, of 21 December 2013 laying down rules concerning the registration, protection and enforcement of EU Trade Marks (Codified version).

Summary 127:
Access to published works for persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled
Access to Published Works for Persons who are Blind, Visually Impaired or Otherwise Print Disabled
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the implementation of the Marrakesh Treaty to Facilitate Access To Published Works For Persons Who Are Blind, Visual Impaired, Or Print Disabled (OJ L 115, 17.4.2017)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the availability of accessible format copies for beneficiary persons in all of the EU countries and the circulation of such copies within the internal market.
The regulation applies to the following:
the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other protected subject matter;
the import and use of these in the EU and in non-EU countries; and
the exchange and making available of information between authorised entities established in an EU country.
It does not apply to:
public institutions or non-profit organisations that provide the same services to beneficiary persons as one of its primary activities, institutional obligations or as part of its public-interest missions;
authorised entities that are a party to the treaty.
KEY POINTS
EU countries may:
make, communicate or make available, for non-commercial purposes, an accessible format copy of a work or other subject matter that has been distributed, communicated or made available to beneficiaries persons or to authorised entities, by an authorised entity established in a country that is a party of the treaty;
export, obtain or access, in accordance with the national legislation adopted pursuant to Directive (EU), an accessible-format copy of the same subject matter, from a third country, for the benefit of beneficiary persons or authorised entities in the same EU country;
use, for commercial purposes, the same conditions as those applicable to the import and import of these same subject matters from the same third country under the same national legislation.
Authorised entities may not:
distribute, communicate, make available available to the same beneficiary persons, or to an authorised body established in another EU country for the same purpose; or
make available to a person who is blind or visually impaired, or who is otherwise print disabled, a printed work that has not been distributed or communicated to them.
An authorised entity may also:
export or use, for a commercial purpose, a non-accessible-format version of the work or subject matter for the purpose of the exchange of information;
exchange and make available information on this subject matter to the beneficiary persons and authorised bodies established in other EU countries.
In addition, the regulation provides for the following exceptions to copyright and related rights:
for the making, dissemination, making available, importing and using of accessible formats of the works and subject matter covered by this regulation, and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
Exceptions and limitations to copyright, related rights and exceptions to certain exceptions to the protection of personal data:
exceptions or limitations to certain rights that are harmonised by Directive 2001/29/EC and Directive 2017/1664 (see summary);
exclusions or limitations of certain rights and rights to certain subject matter contained in Directive 2002/679/EC, as well as in Directive (EC) No 552/2004 on the protection and protection of the personal data of persons with disabilities, and repealing Directives 1/46/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council, and Directive 1/58/EC on the Protection of the Personal Data of Persons with Disabilities, and Directives 2001/30/EC or 2001/37/EC concerning the transmission and transmission of personal communications, and on the processing of such communications.
Amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 128:
Enforcement of EU consumer protection legislation
En enforcement of Union consumer protection legislations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/849 on the enforcement of consumer protection law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the effectiveness of the EU’s anti-fraud legislation by strengthening the powers of EU countries’ authorities to detect, investigate and prohibit cross-border infringements.
It also aims to ensure that EU countries can cooperate effectively and in a timely manner.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods or services that are traded in the EU and that have a European dimension. It does not apply to:
distance contracts;
contracts with third parties;
travel arrangements;
online services.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of suspected infringements and the competent authorities of all EU countries, through their single liaison offices, of any infringement with a Union dimension.
If the Commission has a reasonable suspicion that a widespread infringement has occurred, it should notify the EU countries and the single liaison office concerned by that infringement, stating the grounds which justify a possible coordinated action in the notification.
The competent authorities should coordinate their investigation and enforcement measures in order to tackle effectively the widespread infringement or widespread infringement with an EU dimension and to bring about its cessation or prohibition.
Where appropriate, the outcome of the investigation and the assessment of the infringement should be set out in a common position agreed among the EU country authorities.
Penalties
The European Commission has the power to impose penalties for infringements covered by the regulation. These include:
a ban on the sale of the goods covered by this regulation;
a prohibition of the intra-EU infringement.
In addition, the Commission may decide to impose fines on the trader responsible for the infringement. It may also decide to grant the trader additional remedial commitments for the benefit of consumers that have been affected by the infringement, or to seek to obtain commitments from the trader to offer adequate remedies to the consumers.
Cooperation between EU countries
The Commission and EU countries must ensure that:
the competent authorities have the necessary means and powers to cooperate with each other;
the Commission and the national authorities have access to the information and evidence they need to detect and stop infringements;
all EU countries have the right to set up a coordinated action to tackle infringements with a EU dimension, and to send a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the implementation of this regulation.
Consumers’ rights
EU citizens’ organisations, and where appropriate trader associations, should be allowed to notify competent authorities in their EU countries of infringements, to share with them the information they have received and to provide them with the information needed to detect infringements or to stop them. They should also be able to provide the Commission with information on infringements that have occurred in the digital environment.
When a trader is found guilty of an infringement, it must inform the authorities of the country in which the infringement took place and inform them of the reasons for that infringement. The competent authorities must also inform the European Court of Justice of the European Union (ECJ) of the case and the Court of Auditors of the ECJ of the Member States concerned. The ECJ will decide whether to hear the case on the basis of the common position adopted by the EU Member States.
Implementing powers
The implementing powers of the regulation comply with other EU and national law, including with applicable procedural safeguards and principles of the fundamental rights. They also comply with Directive 2000/31/EC on personal data protection and with Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 on the protection of consumers’ information.
Exemptions
The EU countries may exclude from the regulation the following from the scope of the powers conferred on them by the Regulation:
an obligation to inform the Commission or the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) of any suspected infringement;
any obligation on a trader to notify the competent authority of a Member State of a suspected infringement or to inform OLAF of any other Member State in which an infringement has taken place;
requests to be made by a consumer organisation or a trader association to inform an EU country of an alleged infringement; and
any obligations on a consumer association or trader association, or any other person concerned, to provide information to the Commission, the OAF or to the Member State concerned of any infringements they have committed.
References
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in Member States

Directive 2001/36/EC

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices: safety and quality requirements
In vitro diagnostics: quality and safety requirements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on the regulation of in vitro diagnostic Medical Devices and their accessories
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the placing on the market and putting into service, as well as the monitoring and control of these devices.
It aims to ensure a high level of safety and health for patients and users.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all medical devices manufactured and used only within health institutions, including hospitals, laboratories and public health institutes that support the health care system and/or address patient needs, but which do not treat or care for patients directly. It does not apply to devices manufactured in series production.
Manufacturers must ensure that the devices they manufacture comply with the general safety and performance requirements and other legal requirements, such as those relating to quality and risk management, laid down in this regulation. They must also have a quality management system and a post-market surveillance system in place.
EU countries may decide on a case-by-case basis whether a specific product, category or group of products falls within the scope of the regulation.
Importers must notify the European Commission and the European Union Agency for Harmonised Quality Assurance (EQA) if they believe that a device does not conform to the requirements of this regulation or that it poses a risk to public health and safety.
The Commission may decide that a product is not covered by the regulation if it meets certain conditions.
In addition, the Commission may, on its own initiative or at the duly substantiated request of an EU country, consult the Medical Device Coordination Group (MDCG), decide whether or not a specific device falls under the regulation’s scope.
Equal treatment of all products
The MDCG is responsible for the harmonisation of the rules on the qualification of medical devices. It is assisted by the European Medicines Agency (EMAA).
The EQA assists the Commission in ensuring that the regulation is applied uniformly throughout the EU and that it is implemented in line with EU law.
Exemptions from the regulation
The Regulation does not cover:
medical devices manufactured or used only in health institutions (e.g. hospitals);
general laboratory products and products for research use only;
general medical devices, general laboratory products, products for testing only.
For manufacturers who are not established in the EU, the authorised representative plays a pivotal role in ensuring the compliance of the devices produced by those manufacturers and in serving as their contact person.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
 Â  Â
     
  ICD-10 has been the subject of a number of amendments since its entry into force. Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 (see summary) has been amended several times, most recently by Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2014.
This regulation is the first to establish a harmonised regulatory framework for in vitro-diagnostic medical devices (IVMDs).
For more information, see:
In Vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* In vitro diagnostic device: a medical device that can be used to diagnose, test and monitor the presence or absence of a biological substance or biomarker (i.e. a substance that is present in healthy subjects or in patients).
* General laboratory product: a laboratory product that can detect, test for, or demonstrate the presence of biological substances or biomarkers.
* Software: a software that is specifically intended by the manufacturer to be used for one or more of the medical purposes set out in the definition of an IVMD.
** Software intended for well-being purposes: software for general purposes, even when used in a healthcare setting, or software intended for health-related purposes.

Summary 130:
Ensuring that appliances and fittings comply with the new approach to technical harmonisation and standards (Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012)
Ensembles and fitting — ensuring that they comply with Directive 2009/142/EC
Importers and distributors must ensure that they only make available on the market appliances or fittings which are in conformity with this regulation.
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 — rules on the conformity assessment of appliances and fitting
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the technical requirements for the sale, importation and putting into service of appliances* and fitting* in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the essential requirements and conformity assessment procedures to be followed by EU Member States.
The regulation applies to:
imports and imports of appliances from non-EU countries, whether new or second-hand, imported from a third country;
manufacturers and importers of appliances or fitting imported from outside the EU.
It does not cover risks caused by appliances in the case of incorrect installation, maintenance or use.
KEY POINTS
Scope of the regulation
It applies to all types of gas used in the manufacture, manufacture, operation, testing and installation of appliances, including:
natural gas;
biogas;
combustion products;
electrical appliances;
conformity assessment procedures.
EU Member States must take the necessary measures to ensure that:
the appliance or fitting complies with the requirements laid down in the regulation;
the manufacturer or importer has carried out a conformity assessment;
an appliance or a fitting has been placed on the EU market by the manufacturer or the importer;
it has been certified by the competent authority of the Member State in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on conformity assessment.
Member States must:
ensure that the manufacturer, importer, distributor or any other economic operator who has supplied them with an appliance or with a fitting does not keep the information required for the identification of other economic operators who have either supplied them or to whom they have supplied an appliance;
make available to the public the information to identify all the economic operators involved in the supply and distribution chain;
take the necessary precautionary safety measures in relation to the health and safety risks related to combustion products and the need for the public to be made aware of these risks.
In addition, Member States should ensure that the gas supply conditions do not constitute barriers to trade and that appliances that are compatible with the local gas conditions are put into service.
Consumers must be able to easily identify the type of gas and the corresponding supply pressures used on their territory and certain essential requirements. They should also be informed of the type and quantity of the gas that is supplied to them.
Manufacturers, importers, distributors and any economic operators that are involved in supplying the appliance or the fitting must ensure the information they provide is accurate and up-to-date. They must also inform the Member States of the quality of the supplied gas in the event that it does not remain within the already communicated quality range. The Member States are required to inform the European Commission, the European Atomic Energy Agency (EIA) and the Committee of the Regions of the technical characteristics of the gaseous fuel used in their territory.
National authorities must ensure:
that the appliance and the fitting are accompanied by a CE conformity assessment and that the appliance bears the CE conformity marking indicating that it meets all essential requirements;
that they have carried out the necessary conformity assessment, and that they have notified the Commission of the results of the assessment and notified the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
The appliance and fitting must be accompanied by an electronic label affixed to it showing that it conforms to the regulation. The label must be affixed in a language easily understood by end-users and must bear the following information:
type of gas supplied;
number of hours of operation;
pressure of the supply;
volume of use;
frequency of operation.
If the appliance does not conform to the requirements, it must bear a CE marking affixed on it.
Where a Member State considers that an appliance is not complying with the regulation, it should inform the Commission and the EIA of the reasons for this. The Commission must inform the EEA of the reason for its decision and inform the Council and the other Member States as soon as possible.
Directive 2009/28/EC requires Member States to introduce in their building regulations and codes appropriate measures in order to increase the share of all kinds of energy from renewable sources in the building sector. These measures must progressively reduce energy consumption in different areas, including in buildings.
This regulation lays down the rules for the harmonisation of the rules on energy efficiency and the use of renewable sources of energy.

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash by road: rules for professional cash-in-transit (CIT) companies
Cross-Border transport of Euro cash — rules for professionals
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 on the professional cross-border Transport of Euro Cash by Road between participating Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the rules for the transport of euros by road by professional cash handlers in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the security of euro-denominated euro cash and to harmonise the rules on the application of these rules.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cross-border CIT deliveries/pick-ups;
point-to-point transport;
the transport of cash in transit;
categories of CIT vehicles.
Crossing-border cross-Border CIT licence
The CIT vehicle must have a valid licence issued by the competent authority in the Member State of origin of the CIT company in which it is established. It must be accompanied by a copy of the national CIT security certificate.
The driving licence must be valid for a minimum of 5 years.
CIT security staff must be able to carry out the following tasks:
transport euro cash;
carry out euro cash deliveries;
deliver euro cash to, from, to, to or through a CIT cash centre;
take euro cash from, or to a cash point to, a cash centre in another Member State during the same day;
make euro deliveries to, or from, the same CIT centre in the same Member State;
collect euro cash at the same time in the host Member State.
exchange of information between CIT companies.
Minimum protection rules
Minimum rates of pay, including overtime rates, must be guaranteed for the duration of the whole working day in order not to impose an unnecessary administrative burden on the operators.
In addition, minimum protection rules must be applied to the minimum levels of language skills and the level of security of the staff involved in CIT transport.
Exemptions
The rules on minimum protection apply to the following the implementation of Directive 96/71/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on the minimum protection of workers in the transport sector (see summary).
The Regulation also applies to the general rules on information cooperation between the Member States.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Euro cash transport (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* CIT: a professional cash handling company that offers the best price and/or service and is able to take advantage of the cash services of the nearest national central bank (NCB) branch or cash centre, even if it is located in another EU country.
* Cross-border cash transport: the transport by road of euros in transit between Member States of the euro area.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. the regulation establishing the rules concerning the professional crossing-border Cross-Border Transport of euro Cash Transport by Road of professional CIT Companies (OJ L 183, 1.7.2009, pp. 1-18)

Summary 132:
Food information: mandatory nutrition declaration for foods and non-prepacked foods (Regulation (EC) No 552/2006)
Food information : mandatory nutrition designation for foods
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2016/852 laying down the general principles and requirements of food law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common rules for the provision of food information to consumers.
It aims to streamline and modernise food information law in order to ensure easier compliance and greater clarity for stakeholders and to take account of new developments in the field of nutrition information.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all foods in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
alcoholic beverages;
foods such as meat, fish and fishery products;
prepackaged foods;
meat and fish;
vegetables;
eggs and poultry;
fruit and vegetables;
fish and beef;
animal feed.
Foods that may be exempted from the obligation to provide information on ingredients and nutrition must be labelled in a language easily understood by the consumer.
EU countries must ensure that the declaration is legible and legible.
The declaration must contain:
a description of the composition of the food;
an indication of the amount of the product to be consumed;
a list of the ingredients and the quantities to be added to the declaration.
a statement of the quantities of the products to be taken from the product.
In addition, the declaration must state:
the amount of food to be eaten;
the quantity of food in the form of the declaration;
how the product is prepared and packed.
the number of grams of food and the quantity of portion-based declarations.
Exemptions
Certain foods may be exempt from the declaration, such as alcoholic beverages and certain prepacked foods.
However, alcoholic beverages may be subject to a declaration on ingredients or nutrition information to alcoholic beverages. This declaration must be in the same form as that for foods such as beer, wine, cider and wine-based drinks. The declaration must also state that the product has been frozen and later defrosted and that the freezing and defrosting of certain foods limits their possible further use and may also have an effect on their safety, taste and physical quality.
National authorities must:
ensure that the statement of ingredients and of the amounts of food ingredients are clearly visible on the label;
check that the information is clearly legible;
assist the food business operators and enforcement authorities in complying with the requirements of the regulation;
report to the European Commission within 3 years of the entry into force of this regulation concerning the application of the requirements to the disclosure of certain information. The Commission should, taking into account the need to ensure coherence with other relevant EU policies, produce a report within 3 year of the application.

Summary 133:
Issuance of euro coins and euro banknotes
Issuation of euros and euro banksnotes
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the introduction of the euro
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the issuance of coins and banknotes in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure uniform rules for euro coin and banknote issuances.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro.
EU countries may issue two types of euro coin: circulation coins and collector coins.
The following definitions apply:
circulation coins: euro coins intended for circulation, the denominations and technical specifications of which are laid down in Regulation (EC), No 975/98;
collector coins, which are intended for collection that are not issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
Collector coins: coins that are intended to be put on the market at or above face value. They must not have a shaped edge with fine scallops or a ‘Spanish flower’ shape.
There are limits on the number of 2-euro commemorative coins that can be issued per issuing EU country per year and per issuing country for the total number of issues of such coins. These limits must not exceed 0.1 % of the cumulated total net number of 1- and 2-cent coins put into circulation by EU countries up to the beginning of the year preceding the end of the calendar year.
Consultation prior to the destruction of circulation coins which are not euro coins unfit for circulation within the meaning of point (b) of Article 2 of Regulation (EU) No 1210/2010 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 December 2010 concerning authentication, handling and handling of euro notes.
In addition, EU countries must consult each other via the relevant subcommittee of the Economic and Financial Committee and inform the mint directors of the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 1998 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
The euro has been the subject of a number of legislative acts, including:
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) No 552/2008 on the euro and its legal tender status;
Council Delegated Regulations (EC, Euratom, EEC, EEA, EFS, ESI, ESM and ESI).
For more information, see:
‘The euro’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 134:
European Social Fund (ESF) — missions and scope of activities, including the Youth Employment Initiative (YEI) and the types of expenditure eligible for assistance.
European social fund (ESSF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1081/2006 establishing the missions of the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the ESF’s missions and the scope of its activities.
The ESF is the European Union's (EU) social fund. Its main objective is to support EU countries in pursuing the priorities and headline targets of the EU strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth (the ‘Europe 2020 strategy’).
It also aims to promote employment, improve access to the labour market and support voluntary labour mobility.
KEY POINTS
The objectives of ESF activities are to:
promote employment and social inclusion;
fight against poverty;
improve the quality of education and training;
ensure the protection of human health and the protection against social exclusion;
contribute to the sustainable recovery of the economy.
EU countries are responsible for the implementation of the Europe 2020 strategy. The ESF contributes to this by:
providing financial support to the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
contributing to the development of regional and local policies in the fields of education, training and skills upgrading;
promoting social inclusion and the fight against poverty.
Funding
The regulation sets out the specific objectives of the ESSF, including:
the creation of jobs and training opportunities for young people;
the improvement of social protection systems;
support for the social inclusion of disadvantaged people and the reintegration of female victims of violence into society.
Scope
The European social fund is financed by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and its Member States. The EIB is responsible for:
supporting Member States’ efforts to achieve the objectives set out in Article 9 TFEU (the European Pillar of Social Rights);
financing the activities of the regional development fund (EIF);
support the European Network of Employment Services (EURES) in relation to recruitment and related information, advice and guidance services at national and cross-border level;
fostablishing the European network of employment services (ENES);
fostering cross-sectoral and territorial partnerships.
Eligible expenditure
The EIF is funded by the EIB, the EIF and the EU countries’ regional development funds. It may also be financed by other sources, such as the EU budget.
It may be used for the following purposes:
to:
finance research and development (R&D) projects;
provide technical assistance;
facilitate the exchange of best practice and best practice in the field of training and education;
assist Member States in developing and implementing training policies and programmes;
encourage the creation of networks of experts in the areas of employment, social protection and training.
In addition, the regulation establishes a common set of output and result indicators based on the results of the actions supported by ESF. These include:
participation of young people in employment and training initiatives;
recruitment and training of workers;
employment of workers in the social economy;
employability of workers, enterprises and entrepreneurs;
transnational geographical mobility of workers.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
The purpose of the regulation is to set out the missions, the scope and the type of expenditure that may be financed.
For more information, see:
‘The European Social fund’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1108/2006 of 21 May 2006 establishing the mission and scope, the specific provisions and the categories of expenditure to be made available for assistance under the ESI (OJ L 127, 28.5.2006, pp. 1-8)

Summary 135:
Demographic statistics at European level: data on population and vital events (2013-2018)
Demography statistics at EU level: information on population
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1059/2003 on European demographic statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union (EU) Member States’ obligations to provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the total population at national level at the reference time.
It sets out the data and metadata exchange standards for the European Statistical System (ESS).
KEY POINTS
EU Member States must provide Eurostat with the data on their usually resident population. The data cover population by age, sex and region of residence.
The data must cover:
live births by sex, month of occurrence, live birth-order, mother’s age, mother's year of birth, country of citizenship and mother's region or country of residence;
deaths by age and sex, year of residence, region or region, country, citizenship and country of nationality;
vital events, such as births, deaths and births of children, marriages, divorces, deaths of unmarried couples and deaths of children under the age of 18.
Eurostat must transmit the data to the Commission within 8 months of the end of the reference year. The first reference date is in 2013 and the last reference date reference period referred to in 2018, as appropriate.
Member States must report to Eurostat on reference metadata, using the European statistical system (ESSS).
The Commission must keep the Commission informed of any changes to the data provided by the Member States.
National statistics must be based on the same concepts as those provided by Eurostat. The Commission must use scientific-based and well-documented statistical estimation methods, where appropriate, and use the NUTS classification.
In order to ensure uniform conditions for the implementation of this regulation, implementing powers should be conferred on the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE?
The regulation applies from 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for the collection, transmission and analysis of national statistics.
For more information, see:
‘European demographic statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Total population: the number of people living in a given Member State and the total number of deaths in that Member State.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1024/2003 of 21 December 2003 establishing the European System of Territorial Units (NUTS) (OJ L 218, 31.12.2003, pp. 1-8)

Textile fibre names and related labelling and marking of fibre composition of textile products (Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 on the use of textile fibre names, rules concerning the labelling or marking of textiles containing non-textile parts of animal origin and rules concerning quantitative analysis of binary and ternary textile fibre mixtures, with a view to improving the functioning of the internal market and to providing accurate information to consumers.

Summary 138:
Insurance investment products for retail investors
Insurance Investment Products for retail investor
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the regulation of investment opportunities to retail investors (PRIIPs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes uniform rules on transparency at EU level for the marketing, distribution and sale of insurance investment products* to consumers.
It aims to ensure that the PRIIPs covered by the regulation are available to all retail investors.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all insurance products that are manufactured by the financial services industry for the purpose of providing investment opportunities, where the amount repayable to the retail investor is subject to fluctuation because of exposure to reference values, or subject to the performance of one or more assets which are not directly purchased by the investor.
This regulation does not apply to:
individual and occupational pension products;
individual insurance accumulation or saving products that do not offer investment opportunities;
deposit products solely exposed to interest rates.
The key information document (KIC) must be written in a language easily understood by retail investors, and should be in a standardised format which allows retail investors to compare different products, since consumer behaviour and capabilities are such that the format, presentation and content of information must be carefully calibrated to maximise understanding and use of information.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
the PRIIP manufacturer uses clear and understandable language;
the description of how the investment targets are achieved, including the description of the financial instruments used, avoids financial jargon and terminology which is not immediately clear to investors;
there is a comprehension alert to alert retail investors if the product is not simple and may be difficult to understand.
PRIIP manufacturers must inform the European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority (EIOPA) and the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESAs) of any amendments to the KIC and the key information documents they have prepared.
EU countries must designate the competent authorities responsible for that supervision.
In exceptional cases, the competent authority or the EIOPA may impose a prohibition or restriction before an insurance-based investment product has been marketed, distributed or sold to consumers, in order to protect the interests of the consumer and to ensure the proper functioning of the EU’s financial services market.
EIA and ESAs must:
ensure that the KICO and the KEY INFORMATION DOCUMENTS comply with the relevant EU rules;
make available to the public the information they have drawn up in accordance with this regulation;
provide the public with a list of the authorised persons authorised to act in the field of insurance and the information that they have provided to the EIA and to the ESAs;
take all necessary measures to prevent, investigate and prosecute any infringements of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Insurance investment product: an investment product that is designed to provide investment opportunities.
* Key information document: a document containing information on the investment objectives of the investment product, the investment strategy and the risks associated with those objectives.

Summary 139:
Resolution of failing banks within the EU
Resolving banks within EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/59/EU on the resolution of banks
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2013 on the establishment of the Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE AND THE REGULATION?
The directive establishes the European System of Supervision Mechanism, which aims to ensure effective resolution decisions for failing banks in the European Union (EU).
The regulation lays down the rules for the establishment and operation of the Supervisory System of the Member States (SSM).
KEY POINTS
The SSM is the EU’s centralised system of banking supervision and resolution. It is designed to prevent and resolve bank failures across the EU.
The resolution procedure is carried out by the European Bank Resolution Board (EBA), which is composed of the European Central Bank (ECB) and the national authorities of the participating Member States. The ECB is the supervisor of the SSM. The EBA is assisted by the supervisory board of the ECB and the European Financial Stability Facility (EFSA).
In the event of a bank failure, the resolution proceedings are handled by the competent national authorities. The resolution procedure must be coordinated by the EBA and the EFSF. The European Stability Mechanism is financed by bank contributions raised at national level and should be pooled at EU level in accordance with an intergovernmental agreement on the transfer and progressive mutualisation of those contributions (the ‘Agreement’.
EU countries will assume the obligation to transfer to the Fund the contributions that they raise at national levels.
A resolution decision may be made by the ECB or by the national competent authorities of a Member State of the EU country.
National authorities may take unilateral action to ring-fence banking operations, for instance by limiting intra-group transfers and lending, or by imposing higher liquidity and capital requirements on subsidiaries in their jurisdictions of potentially failing parent undertakings. However, national authorities may not take unilateral actions to resolve directly any credit institution of a participating Member State in order to avoid asymmetries within the internal market in respect of the treatment of failing institutions and creditors during a resolution process.
In addition, national supervisors have strong incentives to minimise the potential impact of bank crises on their national economies by adopting unilateral action, for example by limiting cross-border banking operations or imposing higher capital requirements for subsidiaries of a potentially failing Member State.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directives 2014/58/EU and 2014/61/EU have applied since 1 January 2015.
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 1096/2013 and (EU), No 1024 /2013 have applied from 1 January 2016.
Directions 2014/60/EU, 2014/65/EU (EU banking supervisor) and 2014 /65/EC have applied as of 31 December 2014.
Implementing Regulation (EU):
DirectIVE 2014/57/EU sets out the rules relating to the resolution procedure of banks across the Union and establishes a European Supervisory Mechanism and a European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) for the implementation of the resolution procedures.
Delegated Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 sets out rules on the application of the Resolution Procedure and the Supervision of the System of Banks (EU resolution procedure).
DeLegated Regulation(EU) 2018/852 amends Directive 2014/55/EU concerning the establishment, operation and management of the Solvency II Fund (EU financial stability fund) (EU bank resolution procedure) (see summary) (Directive 2018/1052/EU) (delegated regulation (EU and Euratom).
Commission Delegated Regulations (EC, EFSA) No 852/2014, 2018/1152 and 2018/1452 have applied to the European Banking Supervision Fund (ESM) as of 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to strengthen its financial stability. These include:
the establishment of a centralised supervisory system of supervision for banks;
the setting up of a European Financial Supervision Facility (ESMF);
the creation of a resolution procedure for banks.
For more information, see:
Resolution procedure (European Commission).

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at European Union airports (Airports Regulation (EU) No 1234/2006)
Noise - related operating restrictions
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (Euratom) No 112/2006 on the application of Regulation (EC) No 852/2006 in the field of noise-related operational restrictions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to reduce the noise impact from aircraft at EU airports.
It sets out the procedure for the introduction of operating restrictions.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must designate one or more competent authorities responsible for the process to be followed when adopting operating restrictions* at their airports. These authorities must be independent of any organisation which could be affected by the noise.
The regulation applies to all airports in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to airports in non-EU countries.
Airport authorities must ensure that:
noise assessments are carried out on a regular basis in accordance with Directive 2002/49/EC (see summary);
measures are selected according to objective and measurable criteria common to all EU countries;
the stakeholders are consulted in a transparent way on the intended actions;
no measures are adopted and sufficient notification is provided for;
implementation is implemented;
dispute resolution is ensured.
In addition, EU countries must notify the European Commission, in a timely manner, of the name and address of the designated appeal bodies and the arrangements for ensuring that an appeal body is appointed.
the names and addresses of the appeal bodies.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
From 1 January 2007.
  
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The Regulation applies to:
airports located in EU countries in which an airport as referred to in Article 2 of Article 2 is located;
airport authorities in the EU countries where the noisiest aircraft are located.
For more information, see:

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings: rules on the application of Regulation (EC) No 596/2006
Cross-Border insolvencies: rules applying to the administration of proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the application by the European Union (EU) Member States of the regulation on cross-border judicial proceedings.
It sets out the rules applicable to proceedings which are triggered by situations in which the debtor faces non-financial difficulties, provided that such difficulties give rise to a real and serious threat to the debtor’s actual or future ability to pay its debts as they fall due.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to proceedings concerning a legal person or company which has been opened in a Member State other than that of its registered office. It does not apply to proceedings that are subject to publicity in order to allow creditors to become aware of the proceedings and to lodge their claims, thereby ensuring the collective nature of proceedings, and to give creditors the opportunity to challenge the jurisdiction of the court which has opened the proceedings. The regulation does not extend to proceedings where the debtor has moved from one Member State to another.
Creditors have the right to be heard in proceedings in the Member State in which they are located. The proceedings may also be referred to the Court of Justice of the European Economic Area (CEA) in the case of a person who has moved to another Member State. Creditors are entitled to a hearing before the Court in the new Member State where the proceedings have been opened.
The creditor has the right of access to the court of the new State where proceedings are opened. The creditor may also have a right to access the court in the country in which has jurisdiction over the proceedings in which it has been moved.
In addition, the creditor may be able to have a hearing in a Court of the same Member State as that of the debtor in the original Member State, if the debtor is carrying out an economic activity with human means and assets in that State, in accordance with the case-law of that State.
Appointments
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the Regulation. The report concludes that the regulation is functioning well in general but that it would be desirable to improve its application. It therefore calls on the Member States to adopt measures to ensure that proceedings are effective and efficient.
Amendments to the regulation
The Regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendments to the Regulation have been incorporated into the original text. These amendments concern:
the application of the annexes to proceedings in relation to the establishment of a new State;
the introduction of measures to preserve the assets of a debtor; and
the establishment of proceedings for the preservation of the assets belonging to a debtor.

Summary 142:
Carriage of passengers and their luggage by sea: liability of the carrier and the passenger’s luggage
Carriage by sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the liability of carriers for the carriage by sea of passengers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the liability rules for carriage of passengers, their luggage and their baggage by sea carriers.
It applies to international transport only.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
passengers and their luggage on board ships of Classes A and B of the European Union (EU) and to ships of Class C and D under Article 4 of Directive 98/18/EC;
ships of Classes C, D and E of the EU, and to bareboat charter-out registration.
Liability of carriers
The liability of a passenger and his or her luggage carrier is governed by the Athens Convention relating to the Carriage of Passengers and Their Luggage by Sea, 1974 and the Protocol of 2002 to the Convention.
In the event of loss of, or damage to, mobility equipment or other specific equipment used by a passenger with reduced mobility, the carrier is liable for damages. It is not refundable, except in the cases set out in Article 3(1) or Article 6 of the Convention or Appendix A to the IMO Guidelines, or where the person who received it is not the person entitled to damages.
The carrier and performing carrier must provide passengers with at least the information contained in a summary of the provisions of this regulation prepared by the European Commission and made public.
Compensation in respect of damage caused to passengers
The passenger is entitled to compensation for:
the loss of mobility equipment and/or damage to his/her luggage;
the damage caused by the loss or damage of the passenger's luggage or the loss of the baggage or the damage to the cabin of the ship;
damage caused to the ship or to the baggage of the passengers or their luggage.
EU countries may decide to defer application of the regulation until 4 years after the date of its application.
Amendments to Regulation (EC, EC, EC) No 652/08
Amending Regulation (EU, EC and EC) 2018/852 amends Regulation (EEC) no 552(2008) to extend the scope of Article 4(2) of the Regulation to cover:
categories of passenger ships;
classifications of cabin and baggage; and
classification of the number of persons on board the ship.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Carriage: a contract made by or on behalf of a carrier to the carriage or of passengers or of their luggage, or for the disposal of their baggage.
* Luggage: luggage which the passenger has in his cabin or is otherwise in his possession, or which is transported by sea.
** Carriage by ship: a means of transport by sea in which a passenger or his luggage is transported to, from, or to a destination other than the place of departure of the vessel.

Summary 143:
Establishment and organisation of international rail corridors for competitive rail freight with a view to the development of a European rail network for competitive freight
Establishment, organisation and coordination of freight corridors
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 establishing and organising international rail freight for competitive Rail freight
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for the establishment and organisation, and the selection of further freight corridors, in order to develop a European Rail Network for Competitive Rail Freight.
It sets out the rules for:
the selection of the European Union (EU) Member States’ participation in the establishment or modification of additional freight corridors;
the design of the freight corridors.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
international rail freight corridors (i.e. rail freight networks) which cover at least 3 Member States, or of 2 Member States if the distance between the terminals served by the freight corridor is greater than 500 km;
a network of intermodal freight terminals (e.g. ports, warehouses, etc.).
It does not apply to the following:
intermodal rail freight terminals*;
crossing by the rail freight corridor of at least three Member States or of two Member States;
exchanges of freight with other rail transport infrastructure;
interconnections between existing railway infrastructure.
The European Economic Group (EEG) is responsible for the management of the corridors. It is composed of the railway infrastructure managers of the Member States and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC).
the European Transport Network (ETN).
The EESC is the body responsible for managing and coordinating the development and operation of the EU’s rail freight transport network. It also acts as the coordinating body for the implementation of the rules of the EEG.
Management and coordination
Each Member State must:
take the necessary measures to ensure that:
there is sufficient capacity to meet the needs of all types of transport;
there are sufficient infrastructure capacities available along the corridor;
it is planned in a way which meets, subject to economic viability, the specific needs of the corridor.
In order to guarantee the consistency and continuity of the infrastructure capacities, investment in the infrastructure should be coordinated between the infrastructure managers concerned, as well as, where appropriate, between Member States* and European third countries.
A governance structure for each freight corridor must be established, taking into account the need to avoid duplication with already existing governance structures.
Member States may, upon request from a Member State, participate in the creation of a freight corridor or the prolongation of an existing freight corridor, to allow a neighbouring Member State to fulfil its obligation under that regulation. However, the Member State concerned must not be obliged to participate if it would cause a disproportionate burden or would be contrary to the socio-economic interests of the other Member States concerned.
When two or more Member States do not agree on the establishment of a rail corridor, and with regard to the rail infrastructure located on their territory, the Commission, at the request of one of them, consults the Committee referred to in Article 21 on this matter.
Implementation of the regulation
The Commission must ensure that, in the event of a disturbance in the freight freight network, the competent authority of each Member State:
is responsible for ensuring the availability of capacity, fluid movements and a coherent application of priority rules to the different types of traffic;
cooperates with the national authorities of the different Member States to ensure the smooth functioning of the network;
implements the rules relating to the organisation of the board of directors of the various Member States involved in the development, management and operation the freight network.
Review of the legislation
The Regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendment was adopted in 2017. This amendment seeks to clarify the definition of the term ‘competitive rail freight’ and to establish a definition of ‘rail freight network’.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
European rail freight network (European Commission).

Summary 144:
Road transport operators: good repute and professional competence (Regulation (EC) No 552/97)
Road transport authorities: good reputation and professional qualifications (Regulations (EC, EFTA, Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EC), Euratom — good-repute requirements for road transport operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the granting of a Community licence to a road transport operator.
It aims to improve the quality of road transport services in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all undertakings engaged in road passenger transport services exclusively for non-commercial purposes or which have a main occupation other than that of road passenger operator. It does not apply to undertakings which are engaged in the transport of freight with a commercial purpose.
Authorisation requirements
A road transport undertaking must have authorisation from the competent authority of the EU country in which it is established. This authorisation is valid for a period of 5 years.
The authorisation must be valid for:
the duration of the undertaking’s activity;
the number of vehicles and the number of persons involved in the organisation and management of the road transport organisation; and
the type of vehicle used.
Procedure
An EU country must establish a national electronic register of all road transport undertakings. This register must be publicly accessible, in so far as the relevant provisions on data protection are complied with.
In addition, the European Commission must draw up a list of categories, types and degrees of seriousness of infringements leading to the loss of the good reputation of a transport undertaking.
Penalties
A conviction or penalty incurred by a transport manager or a road-transport undertaking in one or more EU countries for the most serious infringements of Community rules may result in a loss of good reputation, provided that a duly completed and documented investigation procedure granting essential procedural rights took place before its final decision and that appropriate rights of appeal were respected.
A model certificate issued by an EU country of establishment is regarded as sufficient proof by the Member State of establishment that the transport manager/road transport undertaking in question is in possession of the appropriate authorisation.
Member States may, however, lower the speed limit for all or some categories of road traffic operations.
Exceptions
A Member State may apply for a derogation from the conditions of the regulation concerning the recognition of professional competence by Member States and the model certificate.
Road Transport Manager
A transport manager is a person who is responsible for the management and operation of the transport system of an undertaking. The transport manager may be a natural person, any legal person, whether profit-making or not, any association or group of persons without legal personality, or any official body, whether having its own legal personality or being dependent upon an authority having such personality.
An undertaking may be authorised to pursue the occupation of transport operator only if it satisfies the conditions laid down in the regulation. The undertakings established in those regions which comply with the conditions to pursue this occupation only as a result of such adaptation should not be able to obtain a Community license.
Transport Manager
An undertakings’ transport manager must be a person whose sole responsibility is to manage the transport systems of the undertakings concerned and who is independent of any other person or body. The undertaking must be managed by a person with a total responsibility for the organisation of the roads in the territory of the Member States in which the undertaking is established, and which is independently responsible for managing the total number and number of road vehicles. The person in charge of this undertaking may decide to delegate certain tasks to other persons or bodies in order to exercise his or her total responsibilities.
Rules on professional qualifications
The Member States must recognise a model certificate of professional qualifications issued by the competent authorities of their Member States. The model certificate must be issued in accordance with the rules set out in Annex II to this regulation. It must contain the following information:
a description of the type of professional qualification to be recognised;
a list of the types of offences that may lead to the revocation of the authorisation;
an explanation of the technical procedures for the exchange of that data.

Social security coordination rules — application of Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the coordination of social security systems
Social security convergence rules — applying Regulation (EU) No 1408/71
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 496/2006 on the application of the rules on social security coordination to employed persons and their families moving within the Community
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the application by EU countries of the basic and implementing social security rules.
It establishes a system for the immediate updating of the annexes to the regulation.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must cooperate in determining the place of residence of persons to whom this regulation applies and, in the event of a dispute, take into consideration all relevant criteria to resolve the matter.
The relevant national social security institutions must forward the information and issue the documents to the persons concerned without delay and in all cases within any time limits specified under the legislation of the EU country in question. The relevant institutions must use the arrangements appropriate to each case, and favour the use of electronic means as far as possible, to facilitate exchanges between EU countries’ institutions.
When data are transferred indirectly via the liaison body of the Member State of destination, time limits for responding to claims start from the date when that liaison body received the claim, as if it had been received by the institution in that Member State.
In the case of claims for benefits provided but not due, the Member States must respond to each other as soon as possible.
within the time limits set out of respect for the country of destination.
Member States must ensure that:
the relevant documents are issued by the social security institution of a Member State and showing the position of a person for the purposes of applying the regulation and the implementing regulation;
the documents are transmitted by electronic means;
there is no time limit for the transmission of information between Member States;
information exchanged between the institutions is transmitted in a form which allows them to be updated in real time.
Where there is a difference of opinion on the validity of a claim, Member States may decide to refer the matter to the competent authority of the other Member States.
Beneficiaries are entitled to benefits only from their Member State in which they reside. However, they should be entitled to benefit only from the country in which the claim was first submitted. The Member States are responsible for the recovery and recovery of the benefits provided, the offsetting and assistance with recovery of benefits not due and the recovery of provisional payments and contributions not due.
A Member State may refuse to provide assistance to a person who has not been able to prove that they have not been entitled to the benefits they have received. In this case, it is the responsibility of the institution of the requested Member State to provide the person concerned with the necessary documents and supporting evidence on the basis of which the documents have been issued.
Annexes 4 and 5 of the regulation provide the details of the national bodies likely to be involved in implementing the regulation, and the electronic details of those national bodies that are likely to do so. The annexes also provide the information of the liaison bodies of Member States that are involved in the implementation of the regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
 
  The social security system is one of the most complex systems in the European Union (EU). It is designed to ensure the smooth operation and efficient management of the complex procedures implementing the rules of the system.

‘Social security’ on the European Commission’s website.
