Statistics on health and work (2013-2014)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009/EC — European statistical system for the collection of data on public and health at work
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules on the collection and dissemination of European Union (EU) statistical data on:
public and health* at work;
health and safety in the workplace;
the European Community Health Indicators (ECHI);
the other sets of indicators which it is necessary to develop for the purpose of monitoring Community actions in the fields of public health.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ national statistical systems and Eurostat’s statistical activities in the area of public and work at work.
EU countries must compile data on the following:
the number of people at work and the number of incidents of serious adverse health and/or safety events at work,
the duration of these events and the severity of the incidents and the duration of the events;
public health indicators (e.g. deaths, serious injuries, serious accidents, etc.).
The Commission (Eurostat) already collects on a regular basis statistical data from the EU countries, which provide such data on a voluntary basis.
Eurostat also collates data for structural indicators, sustainable development indicators and European Community health indicators, as well as for the other set of indicators it is required to monitor Community action in this field.
The European Community actions.
National statistical authorities must:
ensure that the collected data are consistent with the developments and achievements resulting from Community action;
use the statistical methodologies and data collected by the national statistical authorities;
take account of the principles set out in the European Statistics Code of Practice, which was adopted by the Statistical Programme Committee on 24 February 2005.
Cooperation with international organisations
The statistical activities of Eurostat are carried out in cooperation with the United Nations and its special organisations, such as the World Health Organisation (WHO), the International Labour Organisation (ILO) and the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD), when relevant and possible.
In addition, Eurostat collates and disseminates the data it receives from the Member States.
Quality and comparability of the data
The Member States must ensure that the data they collect are:
consistent with the Community action programme for health (2008-2013);
accurate, reliable and comparable;
not duplicative;
inform the European Commission of the quality of the statistical data they have collected;
cooperate with the relevant international organisations;
provide the Commission with the results of the surveys and surveys they have carried out.

Summary 147:
Quality of job vacancy statistics: EU countries’ responsibilities (2007-2013) (European Commission)
Quality of employment statistics: European Commission’s responsibilities (Eurostat)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2007 laying down the requirements for the regular quarterly production of statistics on Community job vacancies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules for the production of quarterly statistics on job vacancies* in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to transmit to the European Commission quarterly data on the number and breakdown of job vacancies in the EU.
The data cover all economic activity, except for the activities of households as employers and activities of extraterritorial organisations and bodies.
They are to be transmitted to Eurostat, the statistical office of the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
The Commission is responsible for assessing the quality of the data transmitted. It is assisted by the Statistical Programme Committee.
It is important that the data are shared with the social partners at national and Community level and that the Commission is informed concerning the implementation of this regulation.
EU Member States must ensure that school career guidance services and vocational training bodies receive the data.
In addition, Member States are obliged to transmit, on an optional basis, data on vacancies for personal care services (residential care activities and social work activities without accommodation) and to transmit data on job vacancy for such services.
Member States must also transmit data for:
personal care services;
employment services; and
employment activities;
industrial activities.
industrial activity;
professional activities. The Commission is to assess the quality and reliability of the quarterly data transmitted by Member States.
Data are transmitted using business surveys. They must be transmitted in accordance with the technical specifications set out in the regulation. They should be transmitted no later than 1 year after the date of the first data transmission, and no more than one year thereafter.
Reference dates and technical specifications
Each Member State must transmit the data using the following reference dates:
1.1.2007: the first reference quarter;
2.2.2008: the second reference quarter.
3.2009: the third reference quarter and the fourth reference quarter, and the fifth reference quarter thereafter. Where necessary, back data may be based on ‘best estimates’.
4.2010: the fourth and fifth reference quarters and the sixth reference quarters.

Summary 148:
Hybrid vehicles: type-approval of hydrogen-powered vehicles and their systems
Hybrid cars: type of approval of hydrogen systems and systems and their components
Hydrogen vehicles — type of acceptance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on type-approved vehicles and systems for hydrogen propulsion
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes requirements for the type of approvals of motor vehicles with regard to hydrogen propulsion* and for the types of hydrogen components and hydrogen systems.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
motor vehicles designed for motor vehicles of categories M and N, including new forms of hydrogen storage or usage;
hybrid systems designed for vehicles of category M, N and E categories;
hydrogen containers designed to use liquid hydrogen.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
the use of pure hydrogen or a mixture of hydrogen and natural gas/biomethane;
the installation of the systems and components in the vehicle.
The regulations also set out the responsibilities of manufacturers concerning the adoption of appropriate measures to prevent misfuelling of the vehicles.
Implementing measures
The European Commission may adopt implementing measures:
specifications for requirements relating to any of the following:
use of pure or mixture hydrogen;
storage or usage of hydrogen; and
the impact protection of vehicles;
integrated safety and security of the system.
Requirements for hydrogen containers and systems
The rules on the testing of the components and systems must be laid down in Annex II of the regulation.
in Annex III.
Testing of the hydrogen containers
The requirements for containers, other than containers, must be tested in accordance with the test procedures set out in Annex III and in Annex IV.
In Annex V, the regulation sets out the requirements for hydrogen components, including:
type of hydrogen component and system;
pressure level of the internal tank;
temperature of the cryogenic hydrogen in the container;
compatibility of the container with other types of vehicles and with the vehicle;
conditions for the use of the storage or use of hydrogen in vehicles of the category M and the category N.
National authorities must refuse to grant:
EC type approval or national type approval in respect of new types of vehicle where such vehicle does not comply with the requirements of this regulation or of its implementing measures;
new types of system, where such component or system does not meet the requirements;
technical specifications for the installation of systems, components and the storage/use of hydrogen, as well as for the protection of the safety of the vehicle and of the public.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 24 February 2009 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 24 February 2011.
BACKGROUND
The introduction of hydrogen as a fuel in motor vehicles is one of the key objectives of the European Union (EU) programme to modernise and modernise the EU’s rules on motor vehicles. To this end, it is necessary to establish a system of type approval for hydrogen vehicles. The regulation establishes a framework for the approval of these vehicles. It is designed to ensure that hydrogen vehicles are safe and reliable and that they are compatible with the other vehicles in their class.
For more information, see:
‘Hybrid Vehicles’ on the European Commission’�s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hydrogen: a fuel that is produced in a sustainable manner, as far as possible from renewable energy resources, so that the overall environmental balance is positive.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2009 of 23 February 2009 on type of authorised vehicles for the provision of hydrogen propulsion (OJ L 127, 24.3.2009, pp. 1–8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 550/09 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 149:
Equal market access to the transmission networks of natural gas transmission system operators
Equity of access for natural gas Transmission system operators in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on conditions for access to natural gas distribution networks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure that natural gas system operators (TSOs) have equal access to gas transmission networks in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
transmission system operators;
storage system operators*;
third party access services.
It does not apply to the following:
gas storage facilities;
gas distribution systems;
transport systems; and
gas transmission networks.
Access to information
TSOs must be able to book entry and exit capacity independently, thereby creating gas transport through zones instead of along contractual paths.
Transit system operators must provide information on the physical status and efficiency of the system, to enable all market participants to assess the overall demand and supply situation and to identify the reasons for movements in the wholesale price.
The transmission system operator must also provide information to market participants on:
the capacity of the transmission system;
the characteristics of the gas transmission network;
capacity of the storage system.
EU countries may establish an entity or body set up in compliance with Directive 2009/73/EC for the purpose of carrying out one or more of the functions typically attributed to the system operator, which shall be subject to the requirements of this regulation.
In addition, the European Commission may adopt guidelines for good third-party access practice for storage system operators. These guidelines are to be adopted by the European Gas Regulatory Forum (the Madrid Forum) in accordance with the principles of subsidiarity, as set out in Article 5 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (see summary).
The Commission may also adopt the Guidelines necessary for providing the minimum degree of harmonisation required to achieve the aims of this Regulation.
National transmission and distribution authorities must ensure that:
all national transmission systems are compatible with network codes and non-binding ten-year network development plans at EU level;
all transmission systems have the necessary capacity to carry out their tasks effectively and efficiently;
there is no discrimination in terms of the terms and conditions of the transport contracts of a particular system operator in an EU country or in another EU country, unless minimum requirements are set which must be met by all transport contracts.
Tarrifs
The tariff applicable to transmission systems must be based on the best available market price. In that respect, and in particular if effective pipeline-to-pipeline competition exists, the benchmarking of tariffs by the regulatory authorities will be a relevant consideration.
If the tariff is not based on market price, it must be determined by the national transmission system authority. The Commission must also take into account the economic, social and environmental aspects of the tariff, as well as the technical aspects of transmission systems.
Regulatory authorities must:
monitor the implementation of the regulation;
make recommendations to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the harmonisation of the rules applicable to the internal market in natural gas;
draw up a report every 5 years on the implementation and application of the regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to create an internal market for gas. The other measures are:
Directive 2003/55/EC on common rules for the internal markets in electricity and gas, and its subsequent amendments and corrections;
Implementing Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1775/2005 on conditions of access to transmission networks;
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) 2018/852 on the conditions for the access of third parties to the natural gas market.
For more information, see:
Natural gas (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: a person or body responsible for the operation of a transmission or distribution system in the territory of an EU Member State.
Storage system operators: a company that has the right to store gas in a storage facility.
Third party access service: a service that is provided by a third party.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.
  I.
Communication from the Commission to the Council, European Parliament and the Council on the application of Council Regulations (EC and Euratom).
Successive amendments to Council Regulation (ec) No 550/2009 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 150:
Judicial cooperation in civil matters between EU countries and non-EU countries: mutual recognition and enforcement of judgments in civil and commercial matters (Regulation (EC) No 652/2006)
Judicial Cooperation in civil matter between EU and non–EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Council Regulation (EU) No 608/2006 on the mutual recognition of judgments and recognition of decisions in civil, commercial and administrative matters between Member States and third countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the European Commission to authorise a Member State to negotiate an agreement with a third country on matters falling within the exclusive competence of the European Community (now the EU).
The regulation applies to all civil matters covered by Article 300 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU).
It does not apply to:
bilateral agreements;
regional agreements between a limited number of Member States, for example two or three, intended to address local situations and not open for accession to other countries;
certain regional agreements between countries neighbouring Member States.
KEY POINTS
The Member State concerned must notify the Commission of its intentions with a view to obtaining an authorisation to open or continue formal negotiations on an agreement as well as to conclude an agreement.
If the envisaged agreement meets the conditions set out in Article 4(2), the Commission must, within 90 days of receipt of the notification, give a reasoned decision on the application of the Member State authorising it to open formal negotiations or to conclude the agreement. The Commission may propose negotiating guidelines and may request the inclusion of particular clauses in the agreement, or to replace the relevant provisions of the original agreement with those of a subsequent agreement. If, on the basis of its assessment, the Commission may not authorise the opening of negotiations or the conclusion of a negotiated agreement, it must, before giving its reasoned decision, give an opinion to the EU country concerned.
Notification must be given by letter or by electronic means.
The Commission must be kept fully informed throughout the different stages of the negotiations as far as matters falling under the scope of this regulation are concerned and should be allowed to participate as an observer in negotiations as regards those matters.
Where the Commission considers that the agreement envisaged does not meet the conditions laid down in the regulation, it may, subject to certain conditions, authorise it to close the agreement or to open new negotiations on the same subject matter, or for a direct replacement of its relevant provisions by the provisions of such subsequent agreement or by a regional agreement. It must also inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of the reasons for this decision.
Authorisation of negotiations and conclusion of the agreement:
the Member State must inform the Commission within 30 days of receiving the notification of its intention to negotiate the agreement;
the Commission must give its reasoned opinion on the agreement’s opening of formal negotiations and its conclusion within 30 months of receiving that notification;
within 30 days, the Member States must give their opinion on whether the agreement should be closed or whether it should be concluded.
In the event of the latter, the agreement may not be concluded, it shall be closed by the Commission. The Member State shall submit a report to the Parliament and to the European Council on its intention not to do so.
Amendments to the regulation
The Regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendments to the Regulation have been incorporated into the original text. These amendments concern:
a bilateral agreement between an EU country and a non-member country;
a regional agreement between a small number of countries and of third countries neighbouring EU countries which is aimed at addressing local situations;
an agreement between countries which are not eligible to join the EU and which is not open to accession.

Summary 151:
Statistics on migration and asylum: collection and compilation of EU statistics (2003-2006)
Migration and asylum statistics: collection, compilation and dissemination
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 311/76 on the collection, compiling and dissemination of Community statistics in the field of migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes common rules for the collection and compiling of statistics on:
immigration to and emigration from the Member State territories, including flows from the territory of one Member State to that of another Member State;
the number of requests for taking back or taking charge of an asylum seeker,
the provisions on which the requests are based, and
the numbers of transfers to which the decisions lead.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all Member States. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries must supply to the European Commission (Eurostat) statistics on the following.
The number of residence permits issued to persons who are third-country nationals, disaggregated as follows:
permits issued during the reference period whereby the person is being granted permission to reside for a limited period of time;
permits issued for a specific category of long-term visa or immigration status to be granted instead of a residence permit;
recipients of visas and grants of status.
Requests for asylum seekers.
Member States must provide Eurostat with the following statistics:
the total number of asylum seekers who have applied for asylum;
requests for the return or taking-charge of a person who is a danger to national security or public policy;
transfers of persons who have left the Member States;
total number of transfers of persons between Member States, including the number of persons transferred.
Eurostat must report to the Commission on the use and probable effects of estimations or other methods of adapting statistics based on national definitions to comply with the harmonised definitions set out in the regulation.
In addition, Member States must supply the Commission with the information on the sources and methods used to collect the statistics.
National authorities must also supply Eurostat data on the number and quality of sources used for the compilation of the statistics, and on the types of data to be selected and the quality of the data they provide.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to improve the quality and reliability of the EU’s statistics on migration. These include:
an action plan for collection and analysis of Community Statistics in the area of migration;
a communication from the Commission to the Council and the European Parliament setting out an action plan to be adopted by the Commission in April 2003 setting out measures to be taken to ensure the production of comprehensive statistics necessary for the development and implementation of fair and effective Community policies on migration; and
a proposal to propose legislation for migration.
For more information, see:
‘Migration statistics’ on Eurostat's website.

Summary 152:
Audits of public-interest entities: independence of statutory auditors and audit firms (Regulation (EU) 2015/856)
Audits on public- interest entities: integrity of statutory audits and independence of audit firms
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2015-856 on statutory audits of public interest entities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the approval of the persons responsible for carrying out the statutory audit* of an audited entity.
It establishes a maximum duration of the audit engagement of a statutory auditor or an audit firm in a particular audited company.
KEY POINTS
Scope of cooperation between the competent authorities
The competent authorities of EU countries must cooperate with regard to:
the quality assurance reviews and assistance with investigations related to the audit;
the selection of the statutory auditor and the audit firm; and
the audit report.
Quality assurance reviews must be organised in such a manner that each statutory auditor* or each audit firm carrying out audits of the audited entities is subject to a quality assurance review on the basis of an analysis of the risks.
The audit report must include sufficient information on the independence of the auditor or the audit firms and on whether the statutory audits were considered capable of detecting irregularities, including fraud.
Auditors must be able to provide non-audit services which are not prohibited under this regulation, if they have been approved in advance by the audit committee and if they can demonstrate that provision of those services does not pose a threat to their independence.
Independence of statutory audit and audit firm
The statutory auditor must ensure that:
there is no conflict of interest;
there are no conflicts of interest between the audit auditor* and the auditor*;
it is possible for the audit audit to be carried out by an independent audit firm.
In addition to the above requirements, the audit report should also include:
a description of how the audit was carried out;
an assessment of the quality of the work;
a summary of the results of the audits carried out.
EU countries must:
ensure that statutory audits are carried out in accordance with the requirements laid down in Directive 2006/43/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council;
carry out the audit on behalf of the accounting firm;
notify the European Commission and the European Banking Authority of any infringements of any of the requirements of this regulation.
Where an audit is carried out under the supervision of a national authority, the national authority must have the necessary powers to undertake their supervisory tasks, including the capacity to access data, obtain information and carry out inspections.
National authorities must also cooperate with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) in the investigation of any infringement of the provisions of the regulation. The OLAF may also carry out its own investigations in cases where an infringement has been committed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Directive (EU), Euratom and the Council have to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2015.
BACKGROUND
The regulation establishes a framework for statutory audits in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Audits’ on the Euratom website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Delegated regulation (Euratom) of 30 November 2015 on statutory audit of public interests entities (OJ L 304, 31.11.2015, pp. 1–8)

Summary 153:
Conservation of marine biological resources and management of fisheries targeting them (Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) (European Commission)
Convention on the Conservation of Marine Biological Resources and the Management of Fisheries Targeting Them
Conservation and Management of Marine Biodiversity and the Marine Economy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2010 — Common Fisheries Policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the sustainable exploitation of stocks and marine ecosystems in Europe’s oceans and seas.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the implementation of the common fisheries policy.
The scope of the CFP includes:
the conservation of marine species and their management;
the management of fishing activities targeting them.
EU countries must ensure that:
fishing activities are based on the best available scientific advice and relevant information exchange;
there is transparency as regards the determination of the surplus and, consequently, a management of the resources that is consistent with the objectives of CFP;
fisheries are managed in accordance with the minimum conservation reference size (MSA) and the maximum sustainable yield (MSY);
the use of modern and effective technologies is promoted in the framework of the EU system for control, inspection, inspection and enforcement of fisheries.
Fisheries must comply with the rules laid down in Regulation (EU) No 571/2002 on the European Union system for the control and inspection of fishing fleets.
Member States must:
ensure that fishing vessels are registered and registered in the same way as those under EU law;
keep a record of all catches and discards;
take measures to reduce the number of unwanted catches and to eliminate discards.
In addition to the MSA, Member States are required to:
monitor and report on their data collection activities, which must be made public;
cooperate with one another, preparing and sending joint recommendations to the European Commission;
identify, monitor and report to the Commission on the management of their fishing fleets;
monitor the use of new control technologies and data management systems;
use the best scientific advice available to ensure that they are implemented in line with the CSP;
make sure that their vessels comply with all applicable rules and regulations;
have the right to use the equipment they have acquired;
apply the rules and procedures laid down by the CPA.
Regulations and implementing acts
The CFP is implemented in a way that is compatible with other EU policies and, in particular, that takes into account interactions with EU actions in other maritime policy areas. It covers:
marine conservation and management measures in the Baltic Sea, North Sea, Celtic seas, Bay of Biscay and the Iberian Coast, Mediterranean and Black Sea sea basins;
marine management measures where marine resources occur in sea areas of different jurisdictional status and obligations to have due regard to other legitimate uses of the seas;
management of fisheries in the Black Sea, Baltic Sea and Baltic Sea basins.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  The CFP has been in force since 10 December 1982 and has to become law in the Member States by 1 January 2012.
For more information, see:
The Common Fisheries Plan (European Fisheries Agency).

Summary 154:
Common organisation of the markets in fishery and aquaculture products - Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 (see summary). Common organisation of markets in fisheries and aqua-fishery products (CMO) - Regulation(EU) 2016/852
Common organisation on the markets of fishery products and aquaquulture products
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 104/2000 on the common organisation of fishing markets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the establishment, recognition and operation of fisherye producer organisations and aquafishery producer organisations (FPOs) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to:
establish the FPOs and the producer organisations;
ensure that their members’ products are placed on the market;
promote the ecological sustainability of their products;

the format and structure of the production and marketing plans, as well as the procedure and the time–limits for submission and approval of them;

collecting and managing unwanted fish;
avoiding the use of unwanted fishing gear;
advising the competent authorities on the management and use of marine resources;
providing information on the environmental and health aspects of fishing activities;
developing information on aquaquaculture production and sales plans.

Summary 155:
Securities settlement: prudential requirements for securities settlement systems (Regulation (ECB) No 521/2014)
Securities settlements: prudentially supervised by European Securities and Trading Board (ECCB)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(ECB/2014/521 laying down prudential rules for the securities settlement* systems of European securities settlement authorities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the prudential supervision of securities settlement operators (CSDs) in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that CSDs are subject to the same prudential obligations as other financial institutions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all securities transactions that are executed on trading venues regulated by Directive 2014/65/EU and Regulation (EU) No 600/2014 or provided as collateral under the conditions laid down in Directive 2002/47/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council. It does not apply to:
transactions that are negotiated privately by the relevant parties, but executed on the trading venues covered by Directive 2004/47 /EC;
trading venues which are executed bilaterally, but reported to a trading venue, but not reported by a CSD.
It applies to the following:
traded securities that are recorded in a securities settlement system (CSD book-entry system);
trades in securities that have a standardised character, i.e. they can be settled in one or more than one CSD;
the execution of a buy-in procedure;
all financial instruments that are traded on a single trading venue.
the settlement of securities in a single CSD’s book.
Settlement procedures and penalties
A CSD is subject to authorisation and supervision by the competent authority of its home EU country, which is well placed and empowered to examine how CSDs operate on a daily basis and to carry out regular reviews and take appropriate action.
The ESCB, which acts as a settlement agent for the purpose of settlement, is exempt from the requirements set out in Title IV of this regulation.
In order to ensure an appropriate level of safety in the provision of services by CSDs in another EU Member State, such CSDs must be subject to a specific procedure where they intend to provide certain core services as provided for in this regulation or set up a branch in a host Member State. This procedure should not prevent the members of the ESCB from accessing the information relevant for the performance of their duties, including the oversight of CSDs and other financial market infrastructures.
EU countries’ competent authorities are responsible for monitoring the CSDs’ compliance with the regulation and for taking appropriate action in the event of a failure of a settlement procedure. The ESCB is also responsible for ensuring that the competent authorities of the Member States monitor the operation of the system and take the appropriate action to prevent any risks arising from the failure of the settlement procedures.
If a settlement fails, the competent national authority must take the necessary measures to ensure the liquidity of the relevant financial instruments and to impose appropriate penalties on the CSD responsible for the failure.
Where a settlement order is issued by a central securities settlement authority in one Member State to another, that order must be recorded in the system of the same CSD in order to guarantee that all the securities involved in the transaction are settled in the same securities settlement. The same rules apply to the execution of multiple settlement instructions on the same financial instruments with the same date of expiry of the extension period with the aim of minimising the number of buy-ins to the extent compatible with the requirements of this Regulation.
A settlement order may be issued by any central security depositary in any Member State where the securities concerned are traded in more than 1 Member State and where the transaction has taken place on more than 2 Member States’ trading venues. The order must contain the following information:
the date of execution of the order,
the number of securities involved, and
the amount of cash that has been put on the market and the amount of collateral that is required for the settlement of the transaction.
An ESCB Member State must ensure that the central securities depositary has the necessary resources to monitor the execution and operation of its securities settlement procedures and to take appropriate measures to prevent the occurrence of any failure of any securities settlement procedure in the Member State in which the order has been issued. It must also ensure that its competent authorities have access to the information necessary for the operation and maintenance of its systems.
To ensure the proper functioning of the internal market, the regulation sets out a number of uniform obligations to be imposed on market participants regarding certain aspects of the settlements cycle and discipline and to provide a set of common requirements for CSDs operating securities settlements systems. These obligations include:
a system of standardised rules to be applied to the settlement cycle and to the discipline of the procedures and procedures relating to settlement fails;
rules to be adopted to the procedures



Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund: transport infrastructure and environment
Cohesion fund: transportation infrastructure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1316/2013 establishing the Cohesion Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
It aims to provide a financial contribution to projects in the fields of environment and trans-European networks in the area of transport infrastructure.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF is the European Union’s investment fund for the period 2013-2020. It provides financial support for transport infrastructure projects of European added value (AADV) of up to €10.5 billion.
Specific provisions concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the Fund, in order to contribute to the investment priorities within the thematic objectives set out in the regulation (see below), are laid down in the implementing acts.
The Cohesion fund supports projects that are part of the Connecting Europe Facility (see summary).
The EADV covers the following types of projects:
transport infrastructure projects for a total of €10 billion under Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1303/2013 for an amount of EUR 10 billion;
environmental projects for an additional amount of €5 billion under the same regulation;
trans-European network projects for the same amount.
Investment to achieve the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions from activities listed in Annex I to Directive 2003/87/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2013 establishing the EU Green Deal for Europe.
Risk management and risk management of investment in the field of transport.
In order to address the specific needs of the Fund and in line with the EU strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth, it is necessary to set out within each thematic objective, the specific actions as 'investment priorities'. These investment priorities must set out detailed objectives, which are not mutually exclusive, to which the Fund is to contribute. These include:
enhancing the capacity of public authorities and stakeholders and efficient public administration;
strengthening the institutional capacity and the efficiency of public administrations and public services related to the implementation of the fund;
promoting noise-reduction measures;
encouraging risk-management investment;
improving the quality of public transport infrastructure;
ensuring the efficient operation of the EU transport network.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 21 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The purpose of the EAFD is to provide financial support to projects that contribute to:
the sustainable development of the environment;
the improvement of the quality and efficiency of the transport infrastructure of the Member States; and
the expansion of the capacity to meet the transport needs of Member States.
For more information, see:
The European Commission's website on the European regional development fund.

Summary 157:
The European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the Cohesion Fund (2013-2020)
The European regional development fund (2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the European Regional development fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for the operation and funding of the EU’s regional development funds.
It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF is designed to support the development of regions with low or very low levels of economic and social development. It does not apply to regions whose GDP per capita is less than 75 % of the average of the Member States’ gross domestic product (GDP) for the period 2007-2013.
The regulation defines the types of activities which can be supported by the ERDF, in order to contribute towards achieving the thematic objectives set out in Regulation (EC) No 1303/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 December 2013 on the implementation of Horizon 2020 (see summary).
It does not support:
the decommissioning or the construction of nuclear power stations;
the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions from activities listed in Annex I to the regulation;
investment in the area of sustainable urban development;
measures to mitigate the impact of tobacco and tobacco-processing.
Funding
The Regulation sets out a list of the specific actions that can be funded under the Regulation. These include:
new solutions that address issues that are relevant at EU level, i.e. new solutions that are related to sustainable urban developments and are of relevance at European level;
innovative actions that are part of a territorial strategy for specific areas;
projects that contribute to the creation of new jobs;
activities supporting sustainable tourism, culture and natural heritage, including the conversion of declining industrial regions. The Regulation also sets out indicative amounts for these actions.
In addition, the Regulation sets up a partnership agreement between the European Commission and the regions of the participating Member States. This allows the Commission to make recommendations to the regions on how to use the funds. It also allows for the sharing of facilities and human resources, and all types of infrastructure across borders in all regions. This partnership agreement is valid for a period of 5 years.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2014.

Summary 158:
European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) — cooperation programmes for the regions of the European Union (EU) and the countries bordering the EU’s eastern and southern borders (InterACT, ESPON and INTERACT II)
European Regional Developing Fund (ERCDF)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1301/2013 on cooperation programmes under the European Structural and Investment Funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing the cooperation programmes supported by the European Regional Development Funds (ERGDF).
It aims to support cross-border, transnational and interregional cooperation with the countries of the eastern, southern and eastern borders of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Cooperation programmes
The ERDF supports the development of regional cooperation in the following areas:
cross-border cooperation;
transnational cooperation, including:
the creation of regional and transnational networks;
the strengthening of transnational cooperation with non-EU countries;
exchange of information and expertise;
cooperation in the field of maritime cooperation.
Cooperative programmes
Cooperating programmes are designed to:
promote:
high employment that results in social and territorial cohesion, including through activities supporting sustainable tourism, culture and natural heritage, as part of a territorial strategy aimed at achieving employment-friendly growth;
develop administrative capacity;
strengthen regional cooperation, in line with macro-regional and sea-basin strategies.
support and facilitate, through specific arrangements, effective cooperation with neighbouring countries.
The regulation sets out specific categories of cooperation programmes.
Cross-border programmes
Interregional programmes aim to strengthen cooperation by means of actions conducive to integrated territorial development linked to the EU's cohesion policy priorities, and should also include maritime cooperation not covered by the current INTERACT and ESPON programmes. In addition, the content of such cooperation programmes should be adapted, especially as regards the definition of the beneficiary or beneficiaries.
Transnational cooperation should aim to promote:
a high level of mutual trust between the EU countries’ territorial authorities;
improving the quality of the information and communication networks between the regions;
promoting the exchange of experience and best practice in the fields of transport, energy, transport infrastructure, maritime transport and maritime transport;
encouraging the creation of a network of experts and experts in the areas covered by these programmes;
contributing to the implementation of the regional development goal of the ERDF;
supporting regional cooperation with countries in the eastern and south-eastern regions, as well as the countries whose territory borders the EU;
providing technical assistance to the regions involved in these programmes, in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 1080/43/2013;
assisting the regions in implementing their regional development goals.
In line with the EU strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth, regional cooperation should provide a more integrated and inclusive approach to tackling local problems. It should also contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and the mountain regions.
A common set of output indicators to facilitate the assessment of programme implementation is set out in an annex to this regulation.
Regulatory procedure
The Regulation sets out a procedure for the approval of cooperation cooperation programmes, which must be submitted to the European Commission. The Commission approves only the core elements of the programmes, while the other elements must be approved by the participating EU countries or Member States.
Member States must submit a report to the Commission every 3 years on the progress of the programme. The report must include a description of how the programme has been implemented and the number of beneficiaries and the results achieved.
Monitoring
The Commission is responsible for monitoring the programme implementation. It must submit reports to the Member States and the European Parliament and the Council on the performance of the cooperation programme, and to the Committee of the Regions, which is composed of representatives from each Member State and the Commission.
Where the programme is not being fully implemented, the Commission may adopt measures to ensure that it is effectively monitored.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulations apply from 1 January 2014.

Summary 159:
Trans-European energy infrastructure — strategic priorities for the period 2020-2030 (Regulation (EC) No 714/2009 on energy infrastructure) (SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — strategic energy infrastructure priorities for 2020 and beyond
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European energy infrastructure priority list.
It aims to identify energy infrastructure projects of common interest in the areas of energy, transport and telecommunication.
KEY POINTS
The European Commission has identified 12 strategic trans-European infrastructure priorities, the implementation of which by 2020 is essential for the achievement of the EU’s energy and climate policy objectives. These include:
modernising and expanding the energy infrastructure;
ensuring security of supply;
reducing greenhouse gas emissions;
increasing the share of renewable energy in final energy consumption to 20% by 2020;
improving energy efficiency.
The priority lists are drawn up by the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EU).
To be eligible for inclusion in the second and subsequent EU lists, projects must be part of the latest available 10-year network development plan for electricity and gas. The Commission also selects projects from existing projects that are to be included in the next EU list as proposed projects; however, care should be taken to minimise the resulting administrative burden as much as possible, for example by using to the extent possible information submitted previously, and by taking account of the annual reports of the project promoters.
In addition to the priority list, the Commission must also draw up a list of projects that have been designated as ‘critical infrastructures’. These are those which have been identified as such by the European Commission or the European Atomic Energy Agency (EAA).
The Commission is responsible for drawing up the list of priority projects. The list is published in the Official Journal of the EEA.
To ensure uniform conditions for access to the network for cross-border exchanges in electricity, the list is drawn up and published by the EAA and the Commission. For gas, it is based on the best available 10 year network development plans of the Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The energy infrastructure needs to be modernised in order to prevent technical failure and to increase its resilience against such failure, natural or man-made disasters, adverse effects of climate change and threats to its security.
For more information, see:
Energy infrastructure priorities (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Strategic energy infrastructure: a network of energy transmission, distribution and interconnecting energy networks, including pipelines, power stations, substations and transmission lines, which are essential to ensure the uninterrupted supply of energy to the EU.
* Critical infrastructure: an infrastructure which is essential to the security of the supply of electricity, gas and oil, and for the protection of the environment.

Summary 160:
Alternative dispute resolution (ADR) platform for online sales and services
Alternative dispute Resolution (ODR)* platform to resolve disputes between consumers and traders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on alternative dispute resolution
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve consumer protection by setting up an online dispute resolution* platform.
It applies to online sales or service contracts where the trader, or the trader’s intermediary, has offered goods or services through a website or by other electronic means and the consumer has ordered them.
KEY POINTS
The ODR platform is an interactive website offering a single point of entry to consumers and trader seeking to resolve online disputes arising from online transactions. It allows for the submission of a complaint and cooperation within the network of ODR contact points. It also allows for disputes to be settled in a case management tool.
The regulation applies to disputes initiated by traders against consumers where the relevant ADR procedures are offered by ADR entities listed in accordance with Article 20(2) of Directive 2013/11/EU.
Traders established within the EU engaging in online sales contracts must provide, on their websites, an electronic link to the platform. They must also inform consumers about the ADR procedure by which those traders are covered and about whether or not they commit to use these procedures to resolve their disputes with consumers.
EU countries must ensure that the ODR platforms are accessible to all EU citizens and that they are accessible through the ‘Your Europe portal’, which provides access to pan-European, multilingual online information and interactive services to businesses and citizens in the EU.
In order to ensure uniform conditions for the implementation of this regulation, the Commission is to confer on the Commission implementing powers in respect of the functioning of the platform, the modalities for submission of complaints and cooperation between the network and contact points, and the application of the regulation.
Where a trader has not complied with the requirements of the Regulation, it may be referred to the competent authority of the EU country concerned. The competent authority must examine the case and submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the application and application of this Regulation.
If a trader does not comply with these requirements, it must inform the Commission of the reasons for this. The Commission may adopt a regulation imposing sanctions on the trader.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Online sales and service contract: a contract between a trader and a consumer.
* Case management tool: a tool that allows consumers to submit a complaint against a trader in the event of a dispute arising from an online sale or service contract.

Summary 161:
Reform of the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD) rules on the allocation of payment entitlements to farmers
REFORM OF THE EUROPEAN AIM OF THE REGULATION
Regulation (EC) No 73/2009 on the application of the Common Agricultural Policy (CAP) to the allocation and distribution of payments to farmers (recast)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE RECOMMENDATION DO?
It sets out the rules for applying the common agricultural policy (CAP).
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC), which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2014.
KEY POINTS
Direct support
The European Union (EU) provides direct support to farmers for the production of crops, livestock, forestry, fisheries, forestry and aquaculture.
The regulation sets out rules on:
the maximum level of direct support for each hectare of land (e.g. €2,000 per hectare);
the minimum threshold for direct support per hectale (e, for example, €100);
a list of eligible land holdings (i.e. the number of hectares of land for which a farmer is entitled to receive direct support).
Eligible land holdings
EU countries are allowed to apply certain limitations when carrying out the first allocation of direct payments:
they must ensure that the eligible area of the holding is not less than 1 hectare;
the number of beneficiaries does not exceed 1,000;
they may not grant more than €100 per hectares to the same farmer in the same year;
to farmers in Bulgaria, Croatia, Romania and Hungary.
They must also ensure that beneficiaries are not granted more than one hectare.
In addition, EU countries must refrain from granting direct payments to certain natural and legal persons unless such persons can demonstrate that their agricultural activity is not marginal.
Payment entitlements
When calculating the value of the payments to be granted to farmers in 2019, the European Commission is allowed to take historical factors into account, provided that no payments in 2019 have a value lower than 60% of the average. However, in order to avoid disruptive financial consequences for farmers, the Commission may decide to reduce the amount of payments granted in 2019.
Direct payments to large beneficiaries
The Commission may, in exceptional cases, decide to increase the level of payments for large beneficiaries by up to €10,000 in the following calendar year:
from 1 January 2019 to 1 January 2021;
from 31 December 2021 to 31 December 2023;
on 1 January 2023 to 30 June 2023 for farmers in Hungary, Croatia and Romania whose direct payments are subject to phasing-in;
until 31 December 2025 for those farmers in Romania, Bulgaria, Hungary and Hungary who are not subject to a phase-in process.
A number of other rules apply, including:
rules on the eligibility of certain types of land holdings;
rules for the transfer of certain seeds and certain plant varieties to the European Union Rural Development Fund;
measures to ensure that seeds and plant varieties are not transferred to the EAFRD for the first time in the first year of their life cycle.
Rules on the distribution of certain entitlements, such as:
grants for the establishment of the legal persons or groups concerned;
grantments for the protection of certain species of wild fauna and flora;
guarantees for the conservation and management of certain protected species of flora and fauna;
reciprocal access to the EU Rural Development Funds.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 31 December 2009.
BACKGROUND
The CAP was reformed with effect from 1 January 2013.
For more information, see:
Directive support for farmers (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Direct support: the amount that farmers receive from the EU’s general agricultural policy.
* Indirect support: support that is paid directly to farmers.

Summary 162:
Support programme for the smaller Aegean islands (SAAG)
Support Programme for the small and medium-sized Greek islands (SAG)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2008 establishing a support programme to the smaller islands of the eastern Aegea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the EU support programme for smaller islands in the eastern Mediterranean, the so-called ‘Aegean Islands’.
KEY POINTS
The programme covers:
the supply of agricultural products and the preservation and development of local agricultural production;
the production, processing, marketing and transport of local raw and processed products.
The support programme is designed to:
ensure the supply of quality agricultural products to the islands;
encourage farmers to supply quality products;
promote the marketing of local products and reduce transport costs;
support the development of the local agricultural sector;
provide technical assistance to the local authorities in order to improve the quality of the agricultural products they produce;
contribute to the sustainable management of the region’s natural resources.
Implementing acts
The regulation sets out:
a forecast supply balance so as to quantify the annual supply requirements for the islands with regard to agricultural products. It must be prepared by the competent local and regional authorities designated by Greece, which must submit it to the European Commission for approval after consulting the competent authorities and organisations at the appropriate regional level;
a list of the islands covered by the programme;
rules for the application of export and import licences for agricultural products, and for processing products, for export to non-EU countries.
An implementing act in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) is delegated to the Commission in respect of supplementing or modifying certain non-essential elements of the regulation.
In order to carry out the programme, Greece must continue to have at its disposal sums equivalent to EU support already granted under Regulation (EU) No 1405/2006 on rural development (see summary).
Implementation of the programme
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It may adopt implementing acts to supplement or modify certain elements of this regulation, in the light of the changes proposed by Greece. These acts may take account of the following elements: the importance of the new measures, whether the changes to the budget allocated to the measures are substantial, changes in the quantities and in the level of aid for products in the forecast supply balances and any amendments to codes and descriptions laid down in Council Regulation (EEC) Nos 2658/87 on the tariff and statistical nomenclature and on the Common Customs Tariff.
Amendments to the regulation
Greece may submit amendments to the programme to bring it more in line with the requirements of the small islands and the strategy proposed. These amendments must be submitted within the framework of the annual evaluation of the implementation and must be substantiated by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 April 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Agricultural support programme: a European Union policy to assist local production in the smaller and medium sized Greek islands.
* Smaller islands: those in the Eastern Mediterranean, except Crete and Evia, which are part of the larger Aegeo-Mediterranean region.

Summary 163:
Road transport statistics: the carriage of goods by road (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Road transport Statistics: EU countries’ statistical data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 852/2019 laying down rules for the collection and transmission of statistical data on the carriage by road of goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the collection, transmission and processing of statistics on road transport by road in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the carriage by means of a combination of road transport vehicles, namely road train or articulated vehicle, in which the goods road transport vehicle and the trailer or semi-trailer are registered in different countries;
vehicles used for combined transport (i.e. when the vehicle is a road train made up of a lorry with trailer);
vehicle-related variables (including: the type of transport;the number of vehicles registered;the load capacity of the vehicle;the place of unloading of goods;the type of road journey made by the vehicle.
It does not apply to Malta, so long as the number of Maltese-registered vehicles licensed to engage in the international carriage of vehicles by road does not exceed 400 vehicles.
Data collection
EU countries must forward to Eurostat, on or before the date on which the first quarterly information is forwarded, a report on the methods used in compiling statistical data. These methods must be sufficiently precise so as to enable the Commission to have comparable, reliable, synchronised, regular and comprehensive statistical data, whilst taking account of the structural characteristics of the road transport in the EU.
The transmission of the data must take place within 5 months of the end of each quarterly period of observation. The Commission must adopt, where necessary, delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU, in order to take account of economic and technical developments, except for any modifications to the optional nature of the required information.
National authorities must ensure that their methods for collecting and processing the statistical data are designed to ensure that the statistical results transmitted to the Commission are sufficiently precise to enable it to have at its disposal comparable and reliable, reliable and comprehensive data.
Where necessary, the Commission may adopt delegated acts concerning the updating of Part 1 of Annex I of the regulation, except where this is optional.
In addition, EU countries must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on their methods of collecting and transmitting statistical data and the methods of processing the results. The report must, if appropriate, be accompanied by proposals for modifying the list of variables, taking into account the findings of related projects, in particular those on air pollution emissions caused by road transport.

Summary 164:
Transmission and service of judicial and extrajudicial documents in civil and commercial matters between EU countries
Transmission, service and reimbursement of costs
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1348/2000 on the transmission and the service of documents between Member States of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system for the transmission, the service and the reimbursement of the costs relating to the transmission of documents and the receipt of these documents.
It sets out the rules for the establishment of the central body responsible for:
supplying information to the transmitting agencies;
seeking solutions to any difficulties which may arise during transmission of the documents for service;
forwarding, in exceptional cases, at the request of a transmitting agency, a request for service to the competent receiving agency.
KEY POINTS
Each Member State must designate one or more central bodies to:
provide information on judicial or extrajudicial proceedings;
make available a manual containing information relevant for the proper application of this regulation;
draw up a manual for the European Judicial Network in Civil and Commercial Matters.
The central body must:
ensure that the information and documents transmitted are transmitted by the fastest possible means of transmission;
send a receipt as soon as possible and in any event within 7 days of receipt;
notify the transmitting agency when it receives a document.
A receiving agency must forward a document for service but not having territorial jurisdiction to serve it to the receiving agency with territorial jurisdiction in the same Member State if the request complies with the conditions laid down in the regulation, and must inform it accordingly using the standard form set out in Annex I.
When a document is refused to be served, the applicant may refuse to accept the document. The applicant may, however, be reimbursed for the costs of the service or the return of the document within 1 week if it is not either in a language which he understands or in the official language or one of the official languages of the place of service.
If the applicant has refused to accept a document, the issue can be remedied through the service on the person concerned or by reimbursing the costs.
Where a document cannot be served within a specified period, the date to be taken into account with respect to the applicant should be that determined by the law of that Member State. This regulation sets out a system where it is the country of law of the Member State addressed which determines the date of service, and not that of the EU country of origin.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 June 2001.
BACKGROUND
The European Union (EU) has adopted a convention on judicial cooperation in civil matters. This convention aims to establish an area of judicial cooperation for the purposes of the proper functioning of the internal market.
For more information, see:
‘Judicial cooperation’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 165:
Advanced therapy medicinal products — Regulation (EC) No 552/2006
Advanced therapy medicines — Regulation(EC) no 552(2006)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, EC, EC) No 550/2006 on the regulation of advanced therapy medicines for human use
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the authorisation, authorisation and labelling of advanced therapies* for the treatment of diseases and dysfunctions of the human body.
It does not apply to the use of any specific type of human cells, such as embryonic stem cells, or animal cells.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
genetic therapies* and somatic cell therapy* medicinal products;
tissue engineered products*;
somatic cell therapies*.
Genetic therapies are those based on genes, cells or tissues that have been genetically modified to have therapeutic properties.
Somatic cells are those that have the ability to repair or replace a human tissue.
Tissue-engineered products are those which have the potential to repair, replace or repair a human body’s own tissues or organs.
The European Agency for Medicinal Products for Human Use (EMA) is responsible for authorising, authorising and supervising the manufacture and importation of these products. It is assisted by the Committee for Advanced Therapies, an advisory group set up by the European Commission.
EU Member States may decide whether to authorise or prohibit the manufacture, importation or use of such products.
Manufacturers must ensure that their products comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
Labelling
The product characteristics, labelling and the package leaflet must be adapted to the technical specificities of the products covered by the regulation, taking into account the specific technical characteristics of the advanced therapy medicinal product.
Products which are prepared on a non-routine basis according to specific quality standards, and used within the same Member State in a hospital under the exclusive professional responsibility of a medical practitioner, in order to comply with an individual medical prescription for a custom-made product for an individual patient, are excluded from the scope of the regulation whilst at the same time ensuring that relevant Community rules related to quality and safety are not undermined.
In addition, products containing or consisting of engineered cells and tissues, and presented as having properties for, or is used in or administered to human beings with a view to regenerating or replacing a tissue, are not covered by this regulation. However, they may be subject to specific labelling requirements to reflect the specific nature of those products. The labelling must also be adapted, where necessary, to reflect these specific characteristics.
Quality and safety
The quality, safety and efficacy of the approved products must be assessed by a committee set up under the authority of the European Medicines Agency (EMA). The committee must prepare a draft opinion on the quality of the product for final approval by the Commission. The committee may also provide scientific advice on whether a given product meets the scientific criteria which define advanced therapy therapeutic products, as well as to address, as early as possible, questions of conformity with other areas such as cosmetics or medical devices, which may arise as science develops.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending Regulation (EU) No 540/2006 has been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
 
 Â  Â
     
  барантанка (‘advanced therapy medicines’) is a new field of biomedicine that offers new opportunities to treat diseases and disorders of the body. The European Commission adopted Directive 2001/83/EC laying down principles and detailed guidelines for good clinical practice as regards investigational medicinal products for human uses.
For more information, see:
* KEY TERMS
* Genetic therapy: a product that:
contains or consists of genetically modified human cells (e.g. stem cells);
somatic cell therapy: an engineered cell or tissue that is capable of repairing, replacing, repairing or repairing a human organ or tissue; or
soma-based therapeutic product: a therapeutic product that can be administered to a patient.
* Tissue engineered product: any tissue or cell-based product that has been genetically engineered to have the properties of a human or tissue-based medicine.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  i.e. the regulation concerning the authorisations, authorisations or authorisations for the manufacture or importation, including the labelling, the presentation and the packaging, and the scientific advice of the Commission, of advanced therapeutic medicinal products and of tissue engineered products (OJ L 183, 1.1.2006, pp. 1-8)




Summary 166:
Emissions from motor vehicles
Exhaust emissions from motor cars
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 on type approval of motor vehicles and their trailers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out rules on the type approval procedure for motor vehicles.
It aims to improve the quality of the air in the European Union (EU) and to reduce air pollution.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
motor vehicles with a reference mass not exceeding 2,000 kg and which meet the conditions laid down in Annex II to Directive 70/156/EEC;
vehicles covered by the regulation’s Annex III to Directive 98/69/EC (see summary);
vehicle types covered by Directive 2004/3/EC.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
all new motor vehicles in the EU have the same type of exhaust emissions control device;
all vehicles are tested and certified in accordance with the emission control requirements set out in Annex I to the regulation;
the vehicle type approval procedures are carried out by the European Commission and the competent authorities of the EU Member States.
In addition, the regulation lays down rules for in-service conformity, durability of pollution control devices, on-board diagnostic (OBD) systems, measurement of fuel consumption and accessibility of vehicle repair and maintenance information.
EU Member States must ensure:
that all new vehicles covered by this regulation are tested, certified and certified according to the requirements;
that new vehicles comply with the requirements laid down by the Commission.
Member States must provide the Commission with a list of authorised dealers and repairers.
The Commission may, at the manufacturer's request, extend the scope of type approval granted under this regulation to M1, M2, N1 and N2 vehicles, as defined in Annex III of the regulation, which are not more than 2,500 kg in mass and which do not exceed the emission limit values in Annexes I and II to this regulation.
Implementing measures
In order to ensure that the regulation is implemented effectively, the Commission must adopt:
a number-based approach to emissions of ultra fine particulate matter (PM 0.1 µm and below) as well as to recalibrate the particulate mass based limit values;
measures to reduce nitrogen oxide emissions from diesel vehicles;
monitoring of the fuel consumption of diesel vehicles and the accessibility of the information provided by authorised dealers;
a system for emission control which has the capability of identifying the likely area of malfunction by means of fault codes stored in a computer memory;
requirements for the monitoring of the exhaust emissions of certain types of pollution-control devices;
rules on the availability of technical information for independent operators on the same basis as for authorised dealers.

Summary 167:
Food safety: common procedure for the authorisation of food additives, food enzymes and flavourings
Food safety
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1334/2008 establishing the European Food Safety Authority and laying down procedures in matters of food safety
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedure for authorising food additives*, food enzymes*, flavourings and certain food ingredients with flavouring properties for use in and on foods.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances authorised to be placed on the European Union (EU) market or used in foodstuffs for human consumption. It does not apply to:
food additives,
food enzymes*,
food flavourings*
food ingredients with flavouring properties.
Authorisation procedure
The Regulation sets out a common procedure to authorise food additives* and food enzymes* for the placing on the EU market or use in foods. It applies to the following substances:
the substances listed in each sectoral food law, which contribute to the free movement of food within the EU and to a high level of protection of human health and the protection of consumer protection;
foodstuffs which are subject to a ban on their use in the EU.
Food additives, enzymes and food flavourings, to the extent that the safety of flavourings must be assessed in accordance with Regulation (EC).
Food flavourings which are not on the Community list of authorised substances must not be put on the market and used in foods, unless they are included on a Community list.
foods which have been authorised substances are not covered by the common procedure.
The common procedure covers the following.
Applications for authorisation
The European Commission must assess the validity and applicability of the application within 9 months of receiving a valid application from the applicant. The Commission may refuse to grant authorisation to a substance if it considers that it is likely to have a serious adverse effect on human health or the environment.
If the Commission does not object within 8 working days of being informed by the Authority, the period referred to in Article 5(1) of the regulation is automatically extended by the additional period.
Applying for an authorisation must be accompanied by scientific data and other information submitted by the applicant for a certain period of time. Applications must be sent to the Commission within 8 weeks of receipt of the Commission’s reply.
In addition, the Commission must inform the Authority of the reasons for rejecting an application. The Authority must give its opinion within nine months after receiving the application. If the Commission considers that the application does not comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation, it may decide to extend the scope of the procedure to other legislation in the area of food.
It may also decide to apply the urgency procedure to remove a substance from the Community lists and for adding, removing or changing conditions, specifications or restrictions associated with the presence of a substance on the lists.
Where appropriate, the specific sectoral law may provide for protection of scientific data, other information and the confidentiality of certain information.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any changes to the EU list of substances. This information must be made public and must be in a language easily understood by the general public.
An application must be submitted to the authority within 8 months of receipt. The application must contain:
a description of the substances concerned;
a risk assessment of the risks to human health;
an explanation of the conditions under which the substances are to be authorised;
the reasons why the substances should not be authorised and the reasons why they should be kept off the market;
information on the conditions and conditions of authorisation for the substances to be kept on the list;
procedures for removing, removing, changing or changing the conditions or specifications associated with their presence on the EC lists. The European Commission may also adopt measures to remove, remove or change the conditions of the authorisations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation establishes the European food safety authority (EFSA). The EFSA is responsible for the implementation of the general principles and requirements of EU food law and the procedures laid down by it.
For more information, see:
Food Safety Authority (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Authorisation procedure: the procedure under which a substance is authorised for the use in food. It is the procedure by which the Commission authorises a substance to be used in the food sector.
* Food flavourings: flavourings that are flavourings or food ingredients that are flavouring substances.
• Food enzymes: food enzymes that are food enzymes or flavourings.
